A Study Based on a Survey and Expert Insights 
 
==PAGE:2==
 
ii 
Abstract  
The transition to regenerative business models is increasingly crucial in response to environmental and 
social crises. Unlike sustainable models, which minimize harm, regenerative models restore and 
enhance natural and social systems. Despite their benefits, widespread adoption remains limited due to 
significant barriers. This study examines key drivers and barriers influencing regenerative business 
practices and provides strategic recommendations for their integration. 
To address these challenges, this study explores four research questions: (1) What are the main barriers 
and drivers for the adoption of regenerative business practices? (2) How can businesses leverage 
drivers and overcome barriers to successfully implement regenerative business models? (3) Which 
factors influence consumer attitudes and commitment toward regenerative products? and (4) How do 
industry-specific and regional characteristics shape the adoption of regenerative business practices? 
A mixed methods approach was used, combining a literature review, a consumer survey, and expert 
interviews. The literature review provides a theoretical basis and identifies key drivers, barriers, and 
research gaps. The survey shows quantitative insights into consumer preferences and willingness to 
pay as well as qualitative perspectives from open-ended responses. Expert interviews explore barriers, 
drivers, and industry-specific challenges and provide strategic insights. The triangulation of these 
methods ensures a comprehensive understanding of the factors. 
The adoption of regenerative business practices is driven for instance by regulations, financial 
incentives, technology, and consumer demand. Cross-industry collaboration and new technologies 
enable systemic change. Consumers prefer regenerative products at competitive prices, and companies 
gain a competitive advantage through brand reputation, customer loyalty, and long-term profitability. 
Significant barriers are hindering the introduction, including for instance the complexity of the 
regulations, regional inconsistencies, and high initial costs. Cultural resistance, lack of standardized 
certifications, and consumer skepticism further limit market acceptance. Supply chain challenges 
complicate alignment, while lack of awareness and education underscore the need for clear 
communication and transparency in business strategies. 
To adopt regenerative practices, companies should align regulations, secure financial incentives, 
engage consumers, and embrace innovation. Standardized certifications and simplified reporting ease 
compliance, while subsidies and tax benefits offset high initial costs. Businesses can leverage consumer 
demand through credible certifications, transparent labeling, and targeted marketing. Education, supply 
chain integration, and advanced technologies enhance scalability. Corporate leadership, training, and 
cross-sector collaboration are essential for long-term success. By implementing these strategies, 
companies can overcome barriers and unlock the full potential of regeneration. 
Consumer commitment to regenerative products depends on awareness, price, trust, and certification. 
Consumers trust scientific studies and third-party certifications over corporate claims. Transparency 
matters and environmental benefits, particularly in food and textiles, drive interest, with students, higher-
educated individuals, and women showing the strongest commitment.  
Sectoral and regional factors shape regenerative practice adoption, requiring customized strategies. 
Product design faces certification barriers, interior design struggles with Scope 3 emissions, chemical 
pest control relies on costly biotechnologies, and agriculture is hindered by resource disparities. 
Regulatory inconsistencies, supply chain complexity, and compliance variations slow progress. 
Environmental factors also matter, and limited land access, especially in dense areas, further restricts 
scalability.

1 Introduction 
1.1 Background and Context 
The progressive worsening of climate change, the decline of biodiversity, and the depletion of 
resources are a cause for great concern (Wang et al., 2023; Johnson et al., 2017). Indications 
are that conventional business models based on linear resource use and profit maximization 
are not sufficient to overcome these crises (Rieger, 2019). Sustainability, which has always 
been a cornerstone of corporate environmental and social responsibility, aims to limit damage 
and maintain the status quo (Marsden & Wilkinson, 2018). The necessary paradigm shift is 
emerging, characterized by a shift in focus from sustainability to regeneration (Reed, 2007). 
Regeneration is a paradigm shift that includes not only the preservation of ecological and social 
systems but also their restoration and growth. (Gibbons, 2020; Konietzko et al., 2023). 
 
For example, data shows a decline in Arctic Sea ice of 13% per decade, which, if it continuous, 
could seriously disrupt global ecosystems (WWF, 2024). In addition, the world recorded a net 
loss of about 178 million hectares of forest between 1990 and 2020, roughly the size of Libya, 
mainly due to deforestation and land-use change changes (FAO & UNEP, 2020). These 
emerging dynamics make it essential to reassess the effectiveness of sustainability as a stand-
alone solution to environmental degradation (Benson & Craig, 2014). In this context, 
regenerative practices are becoming one of the key strategies to prioritize ecological 
restoration and social justice, a paradigm shift necessary for the resilience and continued well-
being of future generations (Haselsteiner et al., 2021; Das & Bocken, 2024).  
 
The goal is to create the right conditions for ecosystems and communities to thrive and not 
only minimize harm but also achieve net positive outcomes (Muñoz & Branzei, 2021; Hahn & 
Tampe, 2020). Regenerative business models reflect this change of paradigm by incorporating 
the principles of planetary health and societal well-being into business strategies and 
emphasizing co-creative partnerships with nature and multi-stakeholder collaboration 
(Konietzko et al., 2023).  
 
The concept of regeneration originates in agricultural and ecological sciences, where the goal 
was to restore damaged ecosystems (Cairns & Heckman, 1996; Rhodes, 2017). Over time, 
this concept has expanded to include business strategies (Hahn & Tampe, 2020). 
 
With increasing pressure from governments, consumers, and investors to adopt 
environmentally and socially responsible practices, companies have the chance to embrace 
regenerative thinking as a foundation for meeting these demands (Hahn & Tampe, 2020; 
Elkington, 1994). There is an increasing number of companies that have already begun to 
adopt the practice of regeneration. Patagonia, for example, has integrated regenerative 
agriculture into its supply chain and actively promotes soil health and biodiversity through its 
products. Since 2017, the company has supported the establishment of Regenerative Organic 
Certified, a holistic certification standard emphasizing soil health, pasture-based animal 
welfare, and fair treatment for farmers and workers. By adopting these practices, Patagonia 
not only reduces greenhouse gas emissions but also helps to sequester more carbon than 
==PAGE:7==
 
2 
conventional agriculture, highlighting its commitment to restoring ecosystems and combating 
the climate crisis (Patagonia, 2024). 
1.2 Problem Statement and Research Objectives 
The application of regenerative business models holds the promise of making a significant 
contribution to addressing key environmental and social challenges (Hahn & Tampe, 2020). 
The low adoption rate of regenerative business practices could be due to a variety of 
interacting economic, technical, regulatory, cultural, and awareness-related barriers (Lemke et 
al., 2024; Haselsteiner et al., 2021).  
 
Research shows that there is only fragmentary and insufficient literature on the barriers and 
drivers that hinder or enable the implementation of regenerative practices. Furthermore, there 
is a lack of detailed knowledge on how companies can effectively overcome these barriers to 
successfully integrate regenerative strategies into their core business processes.  
 
This study is timely and relevant as it addresses the lack of focused research on the influence 
of consumer demand and the differential impact of sector-specific and regional characteristics 
on regenerative practices. By addressing these areas, this thesis aims to make a valuable 
contribution to the theoretical and practical understanding of regenerative business practices 
and serve as a useful reference for ongoing and future regeneration efforts in various sectors. 
1.3 Research Questions 
The transition to regenerative business practices is multi-faceted and requires a deep 
understanding from the perspective of both barriers and drivers across industries. The thesis 
seeks to analyze these components through comprehensive research questions that examine 
the factors that influence the adoption of regenerative practices and the strategic approaches 
that facilitate the integration of regenerative practices into business operations. 
1.3.1 Main Research Questions 
Research Question 1: What are the main barriers and drivers for the adoption of 
regenerative business practices? The question aims to define the important elements that 
either enable or hinder the integration of regenerative practices into business models and by 
understanding these barriers and drivers, this thesis will highlight the challenges and 
opportunities for companies. This knowledge is of great importance for the development of 
strategies that can leverage the drivers and overcome the barriers. 
 
Research Question 2: How can businesses leverage drivers and overcome barriers to 
successfully adopt regenerative business practices? This question puts the barriers and 
drivers into perspective to find actionable strategies and recommendations for businesses on 
how to leverage the drivers and overcome the barriers identified in the first question. The aim 
is to provide a framework that can help companies or policymakers guide their contribution to 
regeneration. 
==PAGE:8==
 
3 
1.3.2 Sub-Research Questions 
To delve deeper into the nuances of regenerative business practice adoption, the following 
sub-research questions are addressed: 
Sub-Research Question 1: Which factors influence consumer attitudes and 
commitment towards regenerative products? This question explores the specific factors 
that shape consumer attitudes toward regenerative practices and their commitment to 
incorporating these practices into their purchasing decisions. It aims to identify how elements 
like education, awareness, and a deep understanding of regenerative principles impact the 
consumer decision-making process in the context of regeneration. 
Sub-Research Question 2: How do industry-specific and regional characteristics 
influence regenerative practices? As the specifics of the sector and region drive the 
development and actual adoption of regenerative practices, this question examines which 
sectoral and geographical factors more specifically explain how the implementation of the 
regenerative strategy differs. 
1.4 Overview of the Methodology 
To answer the research questions of this thesis, an integrated mixed methods approach is 
applied, which includes a comprehensive literature review, an in-depth survey, and extensive 
expert interviews. Given the complexity of the factors influencing the adoption of regenerative 
business practices, such an approach is valuable for in-depth analysis. 
 
The process begins with a literature review that systematically examines academic and 
industry sources to establish a foundational understanding of regenerative practices and some 
of their barriers and drivers. This review is necessary not only to establish a theoretical 
framework but also to identify possible existing knowledge gaps that the study intends to 
address. 
 
Following the literature review, a survey is presented that was conducted to collect both 
quantitative and qualitative data from a representative sample of participants. Various 
analyses are carried out based on the data, starting with a descriptive analysis in which 
demographic characteristics and general attitudes toward regenerative processes are 
described. Correlation and regression analyses are also conducted, which are very important 
to determine the significant relationships between key variables and identify the factors that 
significantly influence the adoption of regenerative practices. In addition, the qualitative 
findings from the open-ended survey responses provide a deeper understanding of the barriers 
and drivers that enrich the quantitative data. 
 
Qualitative data was also collected in the form of expert interviews. The analysis of the expert 
interviews is carried out based on the Gioia Methodology, which subsequently enables 
systematic coding and categorization of the data in order to be able to analyze it systematically. 
Based on these interviews, the identification of barriers and drivers, as well as strategies to 
overcome the identified challenges to the adoption of regenerative practices, are assessed. 
==PAGE:9==
 
4 
The analysis also provides information on sectoral and regional differences, giving a 
customized analysis that maps the specifics of industries and a geographic location. 
 
To ensure the reliability and validity of the findings, the data from the literature review, the 
survey data, and expert interviews are triangulated. This triangulation provides a broad 
perspective on the dynamics of adopting regenerative business practices and ensures that the 
recommendations and conclusions are well-supported and robust. 
 
Overall, this approach aims to comprehensively answer the research questions and integrate 
insights from a variety of perspectives and data sources for a comprehensive analysis of 
regenerative business practices. This approach also aims to provide actionable insights that 
will contribute to the theoretical and practical advancement of the field. 
 
Overall, this approach aims to answer the research questions comprehensively, integrating 
findings from a variety of perspectives and data sources for a broad analysis of regenerative 
business practices. This approach also intends to provide actionable insights that will 
contribute to the theoretical and practical development of the field. 
1.5 Thesis Structure 
Chapter 2 establishes a theoretical foundation for this thesis by defining regeneration and 
exploring its evolution from ecological restoration efforts to its integration into modern business 
practices. The chapter outlines how regeneration differs from sustainability as it focuses on 
net-positive outcomes that actively restore and improve natural and social systems. Historical 
insights are provided, showing the concept’s origins in indigenous practices, regenerative 
agriculture, and biomimicry, as well as its application in companies such as Patagonia and 
Interface. A significant part of the chapter is dedicated to identifying the barriers and drivers 
for regenerative practices as described in the literature. Barriers such as high initial costs, 
regulatory challenges, resistance to change, and technological uncertainties are analyzed, 
highlighting the complexities organizations face in adopting regenerative approaches. 
Conversely, 
drivers 
like 
economic 
benefits, 
regulatory 
incentives, 
technological 
advancements, and consumer demand are discussed as drivers that can accelerate the 
adoption of regenerative practices.  
 
The chapter 3 outlines the mixed methods approach used to explore regenerative business 
practices. A convergent parallel design integrates qualitative expert interviews and a 
quantitative consumer survey, enabling a broad analysis of barriers, drivers, and strategies. 
Expert interviews, analyzed using the Gioia Methodology, provide in-depth insights into 
industry-specific challenges, while the consumer survey captures widespread trends in 
awareness and behavior toward regenerative practices. The chapter also addresses sampling 
strategies, data collection processes, and advanced analytical methods such as regression 
and correlation analyses. Ethical considerations, including informed consent and data 
confidentiality, are highlighted. Despite limitations, such as the small sample size for interviews 
and potential survey bias, the triangulation of findings ensures robust and actionable insights. 
 
Chapter 4 provides a detailed presentation of the findings from the survey and expert 
interviews. It is structured into two main sections. The Survey section analyzes consumer 
==PAGE:10==
 
5 
perceptions and behaviors related to regenerative business practices. It includes, for example, 
demographic trends, correlation, and regression analyses to identify significant relationships 
and predictors and qualitative insights from open-ended responses, which reveal consumer 
concerns and suggestions for improving the acceptance of regenerative products. The Expert 
Interviews section categorizes findings into barriers, drivers, and strategies, using the 
Methodology and industry-specific and regional differences. Barriers such as regulatory 
complexity, cultural resistance, and cost constraints are highlighted, alongside drivers like 
regulatory frameworks, technological advancements, and growing consumer demand. 
Strategies include product innovation, educational initiatives, and collaborative networks, with 
a focus on long-term goals and scaling efforts. Industry-specific barriers, such as technological 
demands in agriculture and chemical industries, and regional variations in regulatory and 
environmental contexts, are also presented. 
 
The analysis and interpretation of the results presented in chapter 4 are discussed in detail in 
chapter 5, with the aim of placing them in both a theoretical and practical context. By examining 
the survey and expert interview findings, the chapter aims to provide a deeper understanding 
of the data, explain the observed trends, and explore their broader implications for advancing 
regenerative practices. While chapter 4 focused on a neutral presentation of results, this 
chapter shifts towards interpretation, providing insights into the reasons behind the results and 
their relevance to the field of regenerative business practices. The chapter is divided into two 
main sections. The first addresses the survey findings, starting with a detailed discussion of 
the descriptive statistics. It interprets consumer awareness, behavior, and attitudes toward 
regenerative practices, followed by an analysis of the correlations and regression models to 
identify influential factors driving consumer preferences. The integration of qualitative data 
further enhances these findings, providing differentiated perspectives on the motivations and 
barriers identified. The second section focuses on the expert interviews, highlighting systemic 
barriers, key drivers, and practical strategies for implementing regenerative practices. It 
incorporates triangulation, linking these insights to the survey findings and literature review to 
present a comprehensive view. It also includes a section about the discussion of the sector-
specific challenges and regional variations. Through this structured discussion, the chapter 
integrates the findings, contextualizes their significance, and provides the basis for the 
recommendations in the following chapter. 
 
The chapter 6 provides actionable recommendations to overcome barriers and leverage 
drivers for regenerative business practices. Building on findings and discussions from previous 
chapters, it outlines strategies for key areas such as regulatory alignment, economic 
incentives, cultural transformation, supply chain optimization, education, and technology 
adoption. It emphasizes aligning policy measures, introducing financial mechanisms like 
subsidies and tax incentives, and promoting leadership-driven cultural shifts. Additionally, the 
recommendations highlight the importance of collaboration, scalable solutions, and credible 
certifications to build trust and drive systemic change. By addressing these aspects, the 
chapter can assist stakeholders in promoting regeneration effectively. 
 
The chapter 7, the Conclusion summarizes the main findings of this thesis and provides clear 
answers to the research questions outlined in the introduction. It highlights the most significant 
results and reflects on the implications of these findings. It also addresses the study’s 
limitations and offers suggestions for future research to explore innovative approaches and 
deepen the understanding of regenerative principles.  
 
==PAGE:11==

2 Literature Review 
2.1 The Concept of Regeneration 
2.1.1 Definition and Scope of Regeneration 
The concept of regeneration goes beyond sustainability by emphasizing the restoration and 
enhancement of natural and social systems rather than simply protecting or reducing negative 
impacts (Reed, 2007; Robinson & Cole, 2014). It is based on the belief that human activities 
have the potential to restore ecosystems to their maximum function, ensuring that they 
continue to provide services for the future of humanity (Reed, 2007; Hahn & Tampe, 2020). 
Regeneration can be defined as being future-oriented to improve conditions, while 
sustainability is primarily about maintaining the current state to prevent it from deteriorating 
further. (Gibbons 2020; Konietzko et al., 2023).  
 
The regeneration framework is essentially rooted in the ecological sciences and originally 
referred to practices aimed at restoring and rehabilitating degraded ecosystems, such as 
reforestation, wetland restoration, and increasing biodiversity (Mang & Reed, 2011). Over time, 
this ecological perspective has broadened to include social and economic aspects, meaning 
that regeneration encompasses not only the restoration of ecosystems but also the economic 
and social systems that depend on these ecological structures (Tedesco et al., 2023; Das & 
Bocken, 2024). 
2.1.2 Historical Development of Regeneration 
The origins of regeneration have their roots in earlier environmental practices that emerged 
from the concept of sustainability (Robinson & Cole, 2014). This progression reflects a shift 
from sustaining current conditions to actively improving and restoring ecosystems and social 
structures (Tedesco et al., 2023). The early focus of sustainability was on conserving 
resources rather than enhancing capacity (Capra & Luisi, 2014).  
 
The foundational principles of regenerative agriculture originate in the traditional practices of 
Indigenous communities worldwide (The Sustainable Agency, 2023). For thousands of years, 
Indigenous farming systems, such as agroecology, intercropping, and polycultures, have 
demonstrated a deep respect for the interdependence of ecosystems (Jekums et al., 2021). 
These practices have served as the basis for modern regenerative agriculture, as they 
emphasize soil health, biodiversity, and ecosystem resilience (The Sustainable Agency, 2023). 
Shiloh Maples, a fair food systems educator, highlights that regenerative agriculture often 
rebrands “generations of ancestral knowledge,” showcasing how Indigenous farming has 
conserved biodiversity for millennia (The Roots of Regenerative Agriculture, 2020). 
 
Permaculture was introduced in the 1970s by Bill Mollison and David Holmgren. It is a design 
system inspired by natural ecosystems (Mollison & Slay, 1991; Holmgren, 2002). Permaculture 
emphasizes soil restoration, water conservation, and biodiversity, building the foundation for 
modern regenerative agricultural methods (Reiff, 2024; Mollison & Slay, 1991; Holmgren, 
2002). Similarly, Robert Rodale defined the term “regenerative organic agriculture” in the 
1980s, focusing on animal welfare, social fairness, and soil health in farming practices (Rodale 
==PAGE:12==
 
7 
Institute, 2024a; 2024b; The Sustainable Agency, 2023). These approaches aimed not just to 
avoid harm but to actively improve the health of ecosystems, marking a significant evolution 
from earlier sustainability models (The Sustainable Agency, 2023). 
 
Biomimicry, popularized by Janine Benyus in the 1990s, further advanced regenerative 
thinking by using nature-inspired design to solve human challenges. Biomimicry goes beyond 
resource efficiency by promoting systems that emulate natural processes and contribute to 
ecological regeneration (Benyus, 1997). Around the same time, John T. Lyle introduced 
regenerative design principles focusing on environmental aspects and laying the groundwork 
for what would become several regenerative design methodologies. Lyle highlighted the 
difference between sustainable systems, which are ecological systems that have been lost 
and are not being restored, and regenerative systems, which can renew themselves and 
become viable again (Lyle, 1994; Roös, 2020). These principles, combined with Charles 
Krone’s “Living Systems Thinking,” emphasized a holistic approach to sustainability, with 
regeneration as the ultimate goal (The Sustainable Agency, 2023). 
 
In the early 21st century, regeneration started to become established as a business concept, 
particularly in sectors like agriculture and architecture (The Sustainable Agency, 2023). 
Traditional approaches to sustainability were increasingly seen as insufficient to address the 
scale of environmental challenges (Reed, 2007). Companies like Interface and Patagonia 
emerged as pioneers, integrating regenerative principles into their operations (Rattalino, 2017; 
Lampikoski, 2012). Interface focuses on restoring ecosystems through sustainable materials 
and circular processes, while Patagonia emphasizes regenerative organic agriculture to 
promote soil health and biodiversity (Patagonia, 2020; Interface, 2021). Another major 
milestone was the creation of the Regenerative Organic Certified (ROC) standard by the 
Rodale Institute in partnership with well-known brands such as Patagonia and Dr. Bronner’s, 
providing both clear guidelines and enhancing credibility for regenerative efforts (Regenerative 
Organic Certified, 2023; Patagonia, 2024; Dr. Bronner, 2024) 
2.1.3 Regeneration in Ecological Systems 
Regeneration in ecological systems is centered on restoring damaged ecosystems to a state 
where they can sustain and improve their functionality and resilience (Jones, 2017). This 
process involves actively repairing the disruptions caused by human activities, such as altered 
hydrological cycles and disrupted carbon and nutrient flows (MacCord & Maienschein, 2019; 
Ehtasham et al., 2024). Key practices like reforestation, wetland restoration, and regenerative 
agricultural methods aim to foster these restorative processes while building resilience against 
future disturbances (Hahn & Tampe, 2020; Konietzko et al., 2023; Das & Bocken, 2024). 
 
A distinctive feature of regenerative agriculture is its ambition to go beyond traditional 
sustainability by systematically enhancing the vitality of ecosystems. This involves increasing 
soil health, biodiversity, and carbon sequestration. For instance, practices such as no-till 
farming, cover cropping, and crop rotation not only mitigate soil erosion but also improve water 
retention, foster plant resilience, and promote long-term carbon storage in soils (Khangura et 
al., 2023; Rhodes, 2017). By embedding these principles, regenerative agriculture supports 
both ecological and climate adaptation goals (White, 2020). 
 
==PAGE:13==
 
8 
As Buckton et al. (2023) emphasize, regenerative ecological systems operate through “positive 
reinforcing cycles of well-being” between human and natural systems. Such approaches 
contrast sharply with conventional extractive practices by prioritizing mutualism, where 
ecosystems and human activities co-evolve and support one another (Buckton et al., 2023). 
For example, the intentional cultivation of diverse plant species and agroforestry contributes 
to richer soil microbiota, enhanced carbon absorption, and greater ecosystem diversity. These 
practices restore the ecological balance while addressing global challenges like biodiversity 
loss and climate change (Tiemann et al., 2015; Young, 2017). 
 
By incorporating biomanipulation, biofacilitation, and bioaffiliation, companies can actively 
participate in the regeneration of natural commons. Natural commons are the shared natural 
resources such as air, water, and ecosystems that are essential for human survival and 
ecological health. Biomanipulation involves direct interventions to restore ecological balance, 
such as controlling species populations that disrupt ecological harmony. Biofacilitation 
improves the self-regulation of an ecosystem by introducing species that stabilize the system. 
Bioaffiliation promotes deeper connections with living systems and emphasizes the mutual 
dependence of human and natural systems, enhancing ecological connectivity and resilience 
(Albareda et al., 2024). 
2.1.4 Regeneration in Social Systems 
The concept of regeneration extends beyond ecological domains and plays a vital role in social 
systems by focusing on restoring and revitalizing communities (Buckton et al., 2023). 
Regenerative social practices aim to promote social equity and enhance the resilience and 
well-being of communities (Das & Bocken, 2024; Konietzko et al., 2023). Unlike traditional 
sustainability approaches, social regeneration emphasizes empowerment and the ability of 
communities to overcome economic, environmental, and social challenges, enabling long-term 
development and growth (Gibbons, 2020; Hahn & Tampe, 2020; Das & Bocken, 2024). 
 
An illustrative example is the Transition Town movement, which originated in the UK. This 
movement is centered around promoting resilience in communities through initiatives that 
rebuild local economies, reduce dependency on global supply chains, and enhance 
collaboration among residents (Transition Network, 2024a; 2024b; Transition Towns, n.d.). 
Such initiatives have helped communities adapt to economic and environmental pressures by 
encouraging self-sufficiency and local decision-making (Kenis & Mathijs, 2014). 
 
Further examples highlight that the role of fair trade and ethical business practices is important 
in driving social regeneration (Das & Bocken, 2024; Konietzko et al., 2023). Companies such 
as Serendipalm in Ghana work with smallholder farmers to produce palm oil in a regenerative 
way while ensuring fair wages and providing education for workers and their families 
(Serendipalm, 2023). By integrating fair compensation, equitable labor conditions, and 
inclusive decision-making processes, these initiatives create conditions for communities to 
thrive and establish a symbiotic relationship between societal and natural systems (Das & 
Bocken, 2024). For example, Greyston Bakery demonstrates this approach by hiring and 
retraining previously convicted prisoners and addicts, providing them with opportunities to 
rebuild their lives and start new careers (Greyston, 2024; Das & Bocken, 2024). 
==PAGE:14==
 
9 
2.2 The Concept of Regenerative Business  
Regenerative business models represent a significant paradigm shift from conventional and 
even sustainable business approaches by prioritizing net positive impacts (Drupsteen & 
Wakkee, 2024). Wakkee and Drupsteen (2024) emphasize that regenerative business models 
prioritize not only economic viability, but also environmental and social well-being, and actively 
seek to restore and regenerate the systems with which they interact (Wakkee & Drupsteen, 
2024). These models go beyond minimizing damage to actively contribute to ecological, social, 
and economic restoration, creating a holistic framework for long-term value creation (Konietzko 
et al., 2023; Hahn & Tampe, 2020; Caldera et al., 2022; Fischer et al., 2024).  
 
For regenerative business models to be effective, supply chains must not only minimize harm 
but actively participate in regenerating social and ecological systems. This involves adopting 
new principles like proportionality, reciprocity, and poly-rhythmicity, which align business 
operations with natural and social systems' rhythms and needs. Proportionality ensures that 
business impacts and resource use are in balance with ecological capabilities and social 
needs. Reciprocity involves mutual exchanges that benefit both the business and its 
environmental and social contexts, fostering a symbiotic relationship. Polyrhythmicity refers to 
aligning business processes with the natural cycles and temporal patterns of the ecosystems 
and communities they interact with (Gualandris et al., 2023). These principles involve restoring 
ecosystems, enhancing biodiversity, and fostering community well-being (Das & Bocken, 
2024; Muñoz & Branzei, 2021; Konietzko et al., 2023; Hahn & Tampe, 2020).  
 
 
 
Figure 1: Bosco Verticale in Milan (Source: Bosco Verticale in Mailand: So lebt es sich im grünen Hochhaus, Retrieved on December 19, 2024) 
==PAGE:15==
 
10 
Companies following a regenerative business model work to actively reduce their ecological 
footprint, which is the negative effects of their services and products, while simultaneously 
increasing their “handprint,” which refers to measurable positive contributions. Organizations 
may focus on saving more carbon than they produce or increasing biodiversity in areas where 
they operate (Konietzko et al., 2023; Das & Bocken, 2024).  
 
Regenerative architecture, for example, supports this approach. In biophilic design, features 
such as natural light, green roofs, living walls, and indoor gardens are integrated into buildings 
to increase the well-being of their occupants. (Armstrong, 2023; Stone, 2022; Aboulnaga & 
Fouad, 2022; Green Roofs and Living Walls, 2023). For instance, the Bosco Verticale in Milan 
(see Figure 1), designed by Boeri Studio, hosts a total of 800 trees, along with 5.000 shrubs 
and 15.000 plants. This vertical forest is intended to improve air quality, reduce the heat island 
effect in the city, and increase biodiversity by providing habitat for various species (Boeri 
Studio, 2014).  
 
Another defining feature of regenerative business models is their commitment to planetary 
health and societal well-being (Drupsteen & Wakkee, 2024; Konietzko et al., 2023). These 
businesses redefine value creation to include qualitative indicators such as ecological 
resilience, human health, and social equity rather than focusing solely on profit-driven metrics 
(Buckton et al., 2023; Das & Bocken, 2014; Konietzko et al., 2023). By co-creating solutions 
that restore ecosystems and promote well-being, regenerative businesses align their 
organizational purpose with values like diversity, inclusion, and environmental stewardship 
(Buckton et al., 2023; Konietzko et al., 2023). This approach ensures that their operations not 
only sustain but actively regenerate ecological and social health, creating long-term value for 
all stakeholders (Rhodes, 2015; Fullerton, 2015; Konietzko et al., 2023). 
 
Central to these models is the concept of co-creation and multi-capital value, which integrates 
environmental, social, cultural, and financial capital into decision-making processes (Konietzko 
et al., 2023). Regenerative businesses engage in partnerships with diverse stakeholders, 
including local communities, customers, employees, and ecosystems, to co-create value that 
benefits all parties (Elkington, 1998; Konietzko et al., 2023). Regenerative business models 
often prioritize transparent supply chains to empower both producers and consumers, 
promoting trust and accountability by enabling informed decisions about the origins and impact 
of products. (Das & Bocken, 2024) Another essential aspect of this approach is nature-
inclusive design, which involves implementing measures to restore suitable habitats for native 
plant and animal species, thereby improving ecological balance and resilience (Hahn & 
Tampe, 2020; Hermans et al., 2020; Konietzko et al., 2023).  
 
For example, the PaperShell of Arper's Catifa Carta chair will be pyrolyzed at the end of its life 
to produce biochar, a resource for soil enrichment (Catifa Carta, n.d.). Similarly, Ecovative is 
a company that exemplifies nature-inclusive design by producing biodegradable packaging 
made from mycelium, a fungus-based material. Unlike conventional plastics, mycelium 
packaging decomposes in the soil, enriching its nutrients and supporting ecosystem health. 
These approaches highlight how regenerative business practices can integrate ecological 
restoration into product design, ensuring long-term positive impacts on natural systems 
(Ecovative, 2020). Supporting regenerative practices within existing supply chains, such as 
rewilding projects or biodiversity protection, allows companies to enhance ecological and 
social outcomes simultaneously (Das & Bocken, 2024). 
==PAGE:16==
 
11 
The ability to enhance resilience and adaptation is another cornerstone of regenerative 
business models (Ryan et al., 2023; Konietzko et al., 2023). Drawing from complexity theory, 
these models are designed as adaptive systems capable of withstanding and responding to 
global challenges such as climate change and resource depletion (Hahn & Tampe, 2020; 
Konietzko et al., 2023; Fischer et al., 2024). Resilience means being able to anticipate 
disturbances, decrease risks, and integrate strategies to keep operations running and take 
advantage of new opportunities. For instance, regenerative businesses allocate resources to 
environmental and social initiatives, enabling them to remain economically viable even during 
crises. This approach not only strengthens ecosystems and communities but also ensures that 
they are better equipped to endure future challenges (Ryan et al., 2023; Mang & Reed, 2018; 
Konietzko et al., 2023). 
 
Finally, justice and social equity are essential to regenerative business models, which promote 
inclusive decision-making, equitable resource distribution, and fair labor practices (Lazic & 
Della Lucia, 2024; Konietzko et al., 2023). On an ecological level, this means recognizing the 
inherent worth of plants and animals and advocating for their rights, while on a societal level, 
it involves fostering stronger and more cohesive communities (Bergesen & Benton, 1994; 
Hernández & Muñoz, 2021; Konietzko et al., 2023). By addressing systemic inequalities and 
prioritizing justice, regenerative businesses help create a more equitable and resilient future 
for all (Das & Bocken, 2024). Patagonia, for example, integrates social equity and ecological 
restoration into its regenerative business model by actively supporting Indigenous land 
management practices. The company provides financial resources and other support to 
grassroots movements led by Indigenous communities, enabling them to restore degraded 
lands and strengthen their livelihoods (Patagonia, 2024).  
 
Furthermore, regenerative business models help balance local traditions with global demands 
by using strategies that fit specific places. An example is Shorefast on Fogo Island, which has 
revitalized its local community by turning its unique culture and natural features into assets 
that attract worldwide attention and investment. This approach allows businesses to support 
their local environment and community while remaining competitive globally (Slawinski et al., 
2021). 
2.3 Key Distinctions Between Regenerative, Sustainable, and Circular 
Business Models 
Regenerative business models diverge significantly from sustainable and circular business 
models by adopting a more holistic and integrated perspective. While all three frameworks aim 
to address environmental and societal challenges, they differ in their respective systems views, 
main goals, and design focuses. Rather than positioning one model as superior, these 
approaches provide overlapping but distinct frameworks for organizations striving to create 
value in future-fit economies (Konietzko et al., 2023; Drupsteen & Wakkee, 2024; Ryan et al., 
2023). 
 
The three models originate in different systems perspectives, which shape their approaches 
to value creation and impact. Sustainable business models focus on socio-technical systems, 
emphasizing product-service systems, organizational structures, and societal interactions. 
These models prioritize mitigating negative environmental and societal impacts, often referring 
==PAGE:17==
 
12 
to nature abstractly as “the environment.” (Schaltegger et al., 2017; Nosratabadi et al., 2019; 
Evans et al., 2017). The focus remains on reducing ecological footprints through low-impact 
products and practices, guided by the “limits to growth” perspective, which assumes a planet 
with limited resources that do not allow for endless consumption and growth in population 
(Comin et al., 2019; Meadows et al., 1972). In contrast, circular business models emphasize 
closed-loop economic systems. Their primary objective is to enhance material and energy 
productivity by designing systems that minimize waste and resource throughput. This 
approach is rooted in the concept of the “technosphere,” wherein material, energy, and 
information flows are managed efficiently to maximize value while reducing volume (Nußholz, 
2017; Geissdoerfer et al., 2017; Bocken et al., 2019; Konietzko et al., 2023). Regenerative 
business models, however, adopt a social-ecological systems perspective, integrating the 
health and resilience of ecosystems with human societies (Konietzko et al., 2023). Inspired by 
the Gaia hypothesis, these models view the planet as a self-regulating organism where natural 
and human systems are interdependent (Kirchner, 1989; Konietzko et al., 2023). Regenerative 
business models prioritize not only sustaining but actively restoring ecosystems, aiming for 
systemic health and well-being at both ecological and societal levels (Konietzko et al., 2023; 
Hahn & Tampe, 2020). 
 
The goals of these models reveal their distinctive priorities. Sustainable business models 
attempt to achieve a healthy trade-off between economic, social, and environmental value 
creation, often referred to as the triple bottom line. This framework focuses on minimizing harm 
and maintaining a balance across these three dimensions (Hao & Dragomir, 2024; Konietzko 
et al., 2023; Nosratabadi et al., 2019). Circular business models, on the other hand, 
concentrate on improving material and resource productivity, often without explicitly 
addressing broader ecological health or societal well-being (Geissdoerfer et al., 2017; 
Nußholz, 2017; Konietzko et al., 2023). Regenerative business models elevate their focus to 
the health and well-being of social-ecological systems (Konietzko et al., 2023; Buckton et al., 
2023). They aim to restore degraded ecosystems, regenerate biodiversity, and enhance 
societal resilience. This holistic approach extends beyond minimizing harm to creating net-
positive impacts for ecosystems and communities, promoting long-term viability and flourishing 
(Drupsteen & Wakkee, 2024; Hahn & Tampe, 2020; Konietzko et al., 2023). 
 
The design principles of these models demonstrate overlapping and complementary 
approaches (Konietzko et al., 2023). Sustainable and circular business models share a focus 
on the “circular technosphere,” designing for technical cycles that emphasize material 
durability, recyclability, and efficiency. This perspective centers on optimizing man-made 
systems to reduce resource use and waste (Konietzko et al., 2023; Haff, 2013). Circular and 
regenerative business models intersect in their emphasis on the “regenerative biosphere,” 
which focuses on biological cycles and ecosystem restoration (Konietzko et al., 2023; Hoffman 
et al., 2022). In circular models, this often involves designing for biodegradability and non-toxic 
materials, while regenerative models delve deeper into repairing human-nature relationships 
and actively enhancing ecosystem health (Hoffman et al., 2022; Kusumo et al., 2022; 
Konietzko et al., 2023). Sustainable and regenerative business models align on the 
“sustainable human sphere,” emphasizing the need to meet human needs within planetary 
boundaries (Konietzko et al., 2023; East, 2020; Upward & Jones, 2015). While sustainable 
models often address basic needs and equity, regenerative models expand this focus to 
prioritize human health and well-being as central to organizational activities (Konietzko et al., 
2023). 
==PAGE:18==
 
13 
Regenerative models build on the foundation of sustainability and circularity by integrating 
restorative practices and emphasizing the interconnectedness of natural and human systems. 
This convergence encourages the development of innovative strategies that address 
environmental, societal, and economic challenges in a consistent manner (Konietzko et al., 
2023; Drupsteen & Wakkee, 2024; Ryan et al., 2023). 
2.4 Key Distinctions Between Regenerative Business and 
Regenerative Business Models 
 
 
Figure 2: Key Distinctions Between a Regenerative Business and Regenerative Business Model (Source: Own Illustration, derived from Drupsteen 
& Wakkee, 2024) 
 
 
Drupsteen and Wakkee's analysis provides a comprehensive definition of the distinction 
between regenerative businesses and regenerative business models, making their paper a 
crucial reference for understanding these differences. Therefore, the following description is 
derived exclusively from their research (Drupsteen & Wakkee, 2024). An additional overview 
of the key distinctions between a regenerative business and a regenerative business model is 
provided in Figure 2. 
 
A regenerative business focuses on the organization’s internal practices and their direct 
contribution to the well-being of the environment and society. These businesses are guided by 
the principle of net-positive value creation, actively restoring and replenishing ecosystems 
while co-creating value with stakeholders. Their efforts aim to repair damages caused by 
traditional operations, enabling ecosystems and communities to thrive. This approach
