==PAGE:19==
 
14 
emphasizes interconnectedness, requiring businesses to understand their relationships and 
effects on societal and ecological systems. A key feature is their emphasis on concrete 
outcomes and supporting flourishing ecosystems, as captured by Drupsteen and Wakkee's 
observation that regenerative businesses “add life to our lives rather than being extractive.” 
 
On the other hand, regenerative business models extend beyond the organizational level and 
include entire value chains, communities, and social systems. They integrate ecological and 
economic dimensions into a complete and balanced framework, ensuring multiple forms of 
value creation. This model prioritizes systemic innovation, aiming for net-positive impacts on 
society and the environment while minimizing negative externalities. Regenerative business 
models are holistic by design, addressing the interconnectedness of various systems and 
enabling natural, human, and social systems to restore and thrive. They focus on long-term 
outcomes, balancing ecological health with economic growth. 
 
The primary distinction lies in the scale and focus: while regenerative businesses originate in 
organizational practices and direct impact, regenerative business models address systemic 
change, emphasizing the integration of ecological and social considerations into broader 
frameworks. Drupsteen and Wakkee's research highlights the need to understand these 
details in order to promote effective regeneration strategies. (Drupsteen & Wakkee, 2024). 
2.5 Barriers to Regenerative Business Practices  
 
 
Figure 3: Overview of the Barriers to the Adoption of Regenerative Business Practices (Source: Own Illustration) 
 
 
The adoption of regenerative business practices is crucial to enhancing planetary health and 
societal well-being (Drupsteen & Wakkee, 2024; Konietzko et al., 2023). The shift to 
regenerative business practices introduces unique complexities and requires substantial 
==PAGE:20==
 
15 
changes in existing business paradigms (Hahn & Tampe, 2020; Rhodes, 2017; Ryan et al., 
2023). Despite the clear benefits, numerous barriers hinder the widespread implementation of 
regenerative practices, making it essential to understand and address these challenges 
(Hecker & Toivonen, 2024; Haselsteiner et al., 2021; Dipu et al., 2022; Lemke et al., 2024). 
 
This section categorizes the barriers into the following key areas and highlights the complex 
nature of adopting regenerative business practices: economic barriers, such as high initial 
investment costs and market unwillingness to pay for regenerative products, regulatory 
barriers, including the lack of supportive policies and inconsistent standards, organizational 
barriers, such as resistance to change and a lack of expertise, technological barriers, including 
limited access to advanced technologies and uncertainties around innovation and cultural 
barriers, like insufficient awareness and interdisciplinary collaboration. An overview of the 
Barriers to the Adoption of Regenerative Business Practices can be found in Figure 3. 
2.5.1 Economic Barriers   
High Initial Investment Costs 
 
Circular Economy, as a component of broader regenerative efforts (Morseletto, 2020), often 
requires substantial upfront investments alongside expensive management and operational 
requirements. Financial demands like that can pose significant challenges for companies 
navigating this transition (Assmann et al., 2023; Bianchini et al., 2019; Heyes et al., 2018; 
Nascimento et al., 2019). Regenerative practices like the implementation of regenerative 
agriculture in the supply chain can take large initial investments (Pearson, 2007; Dipu et al., 
2022; Lemke et al., 2024). Investments in regeneration can also be used to modernize 
infrastructure, acquire new technologies, redesign supply chains, and retrain workers or 
farmers (Mell, 2022; Lacy et al., 2019; Gualandris et al., 2023; Hahn & Tampe, 2020; Das & 
Bocken, 2024).  Many businesses hesitate to invest in practices like recycling infrastructure, 
plastic collection technology, material recovery, and waste reduction technologies due to 
presumably high-cost concerns (Genc et al., 2019; Sommer & Walther, 2020; Martinez-
Sanchez et al., 2014; Stahel, 2016).  Furthermore, the development and production of 
innovative materials often face barriers due to higher costs and extended timeframes required 
to realize their ecological benefits. B2B customers are frequently hesitant to invest in these 
materials, as achieving price parity typically demands increased order quantities, which many 
material science companies cannot accommodate (Hecker & Toivonen, 2024). Technologies 
from the Fourth Industrial Revolution facilitate circular business models and regenerative 
processes that contribute positively to natural resource replenishment (Lacy et al., 2019). 
Studies highlight the essential technologies and practices required for small and medium-sized 
enterprises to transition from sustainability to regenerative business practices (Caldera et al., 
2022). The upfront expenses associated with green energy technologies are often high and 
not competitive, deterring consumers from adopting them. Many companies prioritize keeping 
initial costs low over reducing long-term operating expenses (Luthra et al., 2016). 
 
Short-Term Profit Focus 
 
A related significant barrier to adopting regenerative business practices and models is most 
industries’ focus on short-term profits. Risk-related barriers often drive companies to prioritize 
short-term solutions aimed at minimizing immediate financial risks (Hecker & Toivonen, 2024). 
==PAGE:21==
 
16 
The pressure to constantly maximize quarterly earnings often prevents companies from 
investing, for example, in the long-term sustainability of their strategies as part of regenerative 
models, especially if these have a longer return horizon (Laverty, 1996; Elkington, 2000). This 
short-termism is particularly prevalent in listed companies, where business decisions usually 
reflect shareholder demand (Sjåfjell et al., 2015; Hoffman, 2018). Carbon sequestration, 
ecosystem restoration, and other regenerative practices offer substantial long-term benefits 
but require long-term commitment and patience before they yield financial returns (Villat & 
Nicholas, 2024; Dipu et al., 2022). 
 
One big part of regenerative agriculture is no-till farming, which helps to secure soil structure 
and biodiversity (Al-Kaisi & Lal, 2020; Rhodes, 2017). No-till farming often incurs higher 
production costs compared to conventional and minimum tillage practices, especially in 
regions like the semiarid prairie (Zentner et al., 1996). However, its profitability largely depends 
on stable yields, as increased fuel costs and potential yield reductions can decrease possible 
economic advantages (Osei et al., 2012). Also, it takes time and financial resources to 
implement appropriate practices such as cover cropping and crop rotation effectively (Reeves, 
2018; Dipu et al., 2022). The high costs of these practices tend to discourage companies from 
adopting them despite the significant environmental and economic benefits they can bring in 
the long term, including improved soil health, higher yields, and better natural risk mitigation 
against climate change (Reeves, 2018; Junod et al., 2024). 
 
Most regenerative agriculture practices can take several years to be perceived as positively 
impacting soil health, thereby raising crop yields and minimizing input costs (Jordon et al., 
2022; Rhodes, 2017). The profitability of regenerative agriculture is a major concern due to 
increased production costs and constraints that do not consistently yield a guaranteed 
premium (Lemke et al., 2024). Renewable energy systems help reduce CO2 emissions, 
enabling regenerative businesses to move closer to achieving a net-positive carbon impact, 
which is a big goal of regenerative business practices (Konietzo et al., 2023; Huang et al., 
2021).  
 
Investment in renewable energy infrastructure may also not produce significant financial 
returns in the short term and thereby deter the company from investing in these practices 
(Miralles-Quirós & Miralles-Quirós, 2021).  
2.5.2 Regulatory Barriers  
Regulatory barriers are significant barriers to the adoption of regenerative practices (Lemke et 
al., 2014; Haselsteiner et al., 2021; Kenny & Castilla-Rho, 2022). Although these practices are 
aimed directly at the restoration and enhancement of natural ecosystems, the dominant legal 
frameworks tend to be poorly supportive of these transformative practices (Haselsteiner et al., 
2021; Kenny & Castilla-Rho, 2022).  
 
A critical regulatory barrier to the adoption of regenerative business models is the general lack 
of policies and initiatives that incentivize or even require the use of regenerative practices 
(Haselsteiner et al., 2021; Hahn & Tampe, 2020).  Policies frequently fail to recognize the long-
term advantages of regenerative business models, depriving companies of the essential 
support needed to implement these strategies (Rezaeian et al., 2024). In the absence of 
substantial policies or actions that create a balanced relationship, there is no clear motivation 
==PAGE:22==
 
17 
for change (Kenny & Castilla-Rho, 2022). Environmental regulations frequently aim to 
minimize damage rather than promote ecological restoration, particularly in countries that favor 
economic expansion over environmental conservation (Gemmell & Scott, 2013). Green policy 
tools typically concentrate on encouraging technological advancements rather than fostering 
lasting behavioral shifts, which leaves companies lacking the essential support needed to 
embrace regenerative approaches (Alsaleh & Mahroum, 2014). The absence of clear 
guidelines and supportive public procurement frameworks for implementing circular economy 
practices is a significant barrier (Assmann et al., 2023; Cantú et al., 2021; Guldmann & 
Huulgaard, 2020), with circular economy incorporating elements of regenerative practices 
(Morseletto, 2020).  
 
Legislative bodies have been slow to adapt, with current policies primarily targeting carbon 
neutrality and technological innovation rather than advancing towards the more ambitious goal 
of achieving positive net zero (Hecker & Toivonen, 2024). For example, despite the potential 
of regenerative agriculture to sequester carbon and improve soil health, not every government 
offers tax breaks for businesses that adopt these practices (Industriemagazin, 2023; Metcalf, 
2009). Furthermore, policies that favor short-term economic goals also make it harder for 
businesses to adopt long-term environmental strategies (Carballo-Penela & Castromán-Diz, 
2014; John et al., 1995). Additionally, elevated, high labor taxes can make labor-intensive 
reuse and recycling operations financially inefficient for companies, thereby hindering their 
implementation (Llorente-Gonzalez & Vence, 2020; Guldmann & Huulgaard, 2020) 
Furthermore, the term “regenerative agriculture” used for regenerative products remains vague 
and is not yet clearly defined within scientific or regulatory frameworks (Lemke et al., 2024). 
This lack of clarity can open the door to greenwashing, allowing companies to make 
unsubstantiated claims about their practices without measurable accountability (Manvi et al., 
2019; Nemes et al., 2022). Policy uncertainty hinders organizations from building green 
capabilities within the framework of adaptable environmental regulations (Teeter & Sandberg, 
2016). 
 
Another barrier in the practice of regenerative business is that the inherent complexity in 
compliance and the associated bureaucratic processes can be significantly high (Haselsteiner 
et al., 2021; Hecker & Toivonen, 2024). Environmental regulations have traditionally prioritized 
protection, but in the Anthropocene, a shift towards restoration-focused approaches is 
necessary (Akhtar-Khavari & Telesetsky, 2016). Furthermore, environmental regulations 
create administrative challenges for businesses, with small enterprises more likely to feel this 
burden than medium-sized ones (Kotnik et al., 2020). While the European Union has made 
notable advancements in establishing for example an enabling environment for greener 
agriculture and circular economy models (Silvis et al., 2019; Nosko, 2019), comparable 
progress has not been achieved in other regions of the world, for example Latin America or 
Asia, where the development of similar frameworks remains a challenge (Hines, 2025; Jayne 
et al., 2019). Another example of regulatory disparities between countries is India. There is an 
insufficient number of government incentive schemes or financial mechanisms to encourage 
businesses and industries to adopt renewable energy technologies (Luthra et al., 2016). 
Varying environmental standards can place companies engaged in environmentally and 
socially responsible practices at a competitive disadvantage, as they face higher costs than 
competitors in areas with less strict regulatory demands (Clark, 1995). 
 
==PAGE:23==
 
18 
In addition, companies face the dual barrier of complying to local practices while meeting global 
sustainability standards, which is a paradox that requires navigating complex regulatory 
landscapes. Slawinski et al. (2021) emphasize the importance of balancing community-specific 
needs and global environmental responsibility. This balance is critical from a regulatory 
perspective, but also for operational success, as companies must integrate regenerative 
practices that respect local traditions and cultural specificities while adhering to international 
sustainability goals. Achieving this balance is a significant barrier as it requires companies to 
navigate through many local and global regulations that are often contradictory or not clearly 
defined (Slawinski et al., 2021). 
2.5.3 Organizational Barriers  
Resistance to Change 
 
One of the most significant barriers to the implementation of regenerative business practices 
is the resistance to change that is often observed within organizational structures (Haselsteiner 
et al., 2021; Holt et al., 2007; Kenny & Castilla-Rho, 2022). Ideological barriers in agriculture 
can hinder the shift towards strategies of less CO2 consumption despite the presence of 
financial incentives (Press et al., 2014). This resistance may manifest as skepticism about new 
methods (Jost, 2015), concerns about increased costs, regulations, and lack of clear financial 
incentives (Lemke et al., 2024).  A major obstacle to the adoption of sustainable products is 
consumers’ hesitation to pay premium prices (Nath & Agrawal, 2022), with regeneration being 
the next step beyond sustainable products (Rhodes, 2015; Gibbons, 2020). 
 
Lack of Knowledge and Expertise 
 
A further significant barrier to the adoption of regenerative business practices is the lack of 
required knowledge and expertise within companies. Many conventional industries lack the 
technical expertise required to implement regenerative principles, such as ecosystem 
restoration, carbon sequestration, or circular economy models (Haselsteiner et al., 2021; Dipu 
et al., 2022). This deficiency in knowledge hinders the adoption of innovative regenerative 
practices, as businesses may lack an adequate comprehension of the complexities and 
advantages associated with such approaches (Hecker & Toivonen, 2024). Furthermore, 
insufficient research on the factors that contribute to the successful implementation of 
regenerative practices is hindering adoption within the organization (Rahman et al., 2020). 
Training and know-how in managing and using innovative materials and technologies are often 
insufficient in the example of a built environment. This deficit is largely attributed to the lack of 
detailed and expansive university study programs focused on regenerative principles, along 
with an absence of specific disciplines dedicated to promoting and supporting issues related 
to regenerative sustainability (Johnston, 2010; Haselsteiner et al., 2021). Additionally, the 
hesitation among project leaders and suppliers to advocate for and provide training for their 
employees in regenerative principles to apply these in real-world scenarios contributes to this 
issue (Haselsteiner et al., 2021). 
 
 
 
==PAGE:24==
 
19 
Misalignment of Stakeholder Interests 
 
Stakeholders often have different interests, which makes it hard for businesses to adopt 
practices that help with the business’s environmental and social goals (Espahbodi et al., 2019; 
Cragg & Richardson, 2009). Stakeholders, including employees, shareholders, suppliers, and 
customers, often have different priorities, which can make it difficult to implement long-term 
strategies (Cragg & Richardson, 2009; Haselsteiner et al. 2021; Schaltegger et al., 2017). For 
example, while a company’s regeneration and sustainability team may want to protect the 
environment and society, investors may prioritize short-term financial return, which does not 
always match with regenerative goals (Espahbodi et al., 2019).  External stakeholders, such 
as suppliers and customers, may also resist changes associated with regenerative practices 
because Suppliers are hesitant in general to transition to more sustainable materials or 
processes if they perceive them as costly or complex to implement (Tate et al., 2011). 
Switching to circular business principles can also be challenging for companies when trust and 
alignment among supply chain partners are lacking, hindering effective collaboration 
(Assmann et al., 2023; Cantù et al., 2021; Guldmann & Huulgaard, 2020). Furthermore, the 
adoption of regenerative innovation is often obstructed by B2B customers’ hesitation to invest 
the necessary resources and time required to integrate new materials into their supply chains 
(Hecker & Toivonen, 2024). 
2.5.4 Technological Barriers 
Companies trying to implement regenerative practices often have difficulties getting access to 
new technologies and navigating the uncertainties surrounding emerging innovations 
(Lemeilleur et al., 2019). These challenges slow down the transition to more responsible 
business models (Haselsteiner et al., 2021). 
 
Lack of Access to Advanced Technologies and Technological Uncertainty 
 
Companies often lack the necessary measures and processes to drive innovation, as well as 
sufficient knowledge about available opportunities for innovation support (Uvarova et al., 
2020). Companies require advanced technological knowledge and expertise, particularly in 
areas such as information and communication technologies, which are essential for effective 
product monitoring across multiple life cycles (Assmann et al., 2023; Bianchini et al., 2019; 
Rizos et al., 2016; Uvarova et al., 2020; Lehtimäki et al., 2020). Many regenerative 
technologies, such as carbon capture, renewable energy integration, and advanced recycling 
systems, are still emerging and often come with high implementation costs (Sanna et al., 
2014). Practical implementation of, for example, recycling systems is often hindered by the 
limited availability of technologies designed to assist companies in efficiently carrying out 
recycling and remanufacturing activities (Assmann et al., 2023; Cantú et al., 2021; Guldmann 
& Huulgaard, 2020; Vermunt et al., 2019). For smaller enterprises, especially small and 
medium-sized enterprises (SMEs), these upfront costs are unaffordable, limiting their ability to 
access the necessary technology (Jones & Jain, 2002; International Finance Corporation, 
2017). For example, renewable energy systems, such as solar and wind power, require 
substantial investments in both equipment and infrastructure (Haas et al., 2021).  
 
==PAGE:25==
 
20 
Furthermore, a lack of regional technological infrastructure can worsen this issue, especially 
in emerging economies (Ejiaku, 2014). For instance, carbon capture and storage technologies, 
which are essential for mitigating climate change, are predominantly concentrated in 
developed regions, leaving businesses in less technologically advanced areas without viable 
solutions (Global CCS Institute, 2020). Historically, uncertainty and the potential irreversibility 
of outcomes have consistently posed significant obstacles to the adoption of technologies 
designed to achieve both productivity gains and environmental benefits (Purvis et al., 1995). It 
is particularly problematic for industries that rely on long-term, stable investments, such as 
energy, where technological innovations can be costly and disruptive (Luthra et al., 2016). 
Adopting disruptive innovations poses significant challenges for organizations, as these 
processes often oversimplify complex issues and are constrained by time and funding 
limitations (Hecker & Toivonen, 2024). Assink’s (2006) model identifies key barriers to 
enhancing innovation capabilities, including adoption hurdles, deep-rooted mindsets, 
perceived risks, inadequate infrastructure, and arising challenges.  
 
In sectors such as agriculture, where practices like regenerative farming require the purchase 
of new equipment and the development of new skills, the lack of technological certainty 
represents a significant barrier to the broader adoption of these approaches (Manning, 2024). 
For example, innovations such as precision farming, which employs data-driven techniques to 
enhance crop yields, are considered as promising. However, the lack of certainty regarding 
their long-term efficacy has discouraged investments (Bhavana & Rao, 2023). This uncertainty 
also applies to emerging opportunities such as bio-based materials, closed-loop recycling 
systems, and ecosystem restoration technologies (Falcone & Imbert, 2019).  Moreover, the 
rapid pace of innovation increases the risk that regenerative technologies adopted today could 
become outdated in a few years (Mazur et al., 2019; Eitan & Hekkert, 2023). This phenomenon 
is particularly acute in the renewable energy sector, where new developments in energy 
storage or grid integration can quickly outpace current systems (Eitan & Hekkert, 2023) 
2.5.5 Cultural Barriers and Lack of Awareness  
Cultural and societal issues are a major challenge in making regenerative business practices 
mainstream. In many areas, there is insufficient public awareness and education about the 
importance of regeneration and its many benefits (Burns et al., 2019). Forms of resistance 
have attracted attention in research, particularly in agriculture, culture is known to discourage 
the adoption of new, environmentally friendly techniques (Burns, 2021; Pretty, 2007; Lemke et 
al., 2024). Newer regenerative agricultural methods such as no-till farming or agroforestry often 
face resistance in traditional farming communities, especially among arable farmers, due to 
the lack of empirical evidence demonstrating their benefits and the deeply rooted reliance on 
conventional practices (Khangura et al., 2023; Burns, 2021). Policymakers have developed 
and enacted numerous agricultural policies aimed at tackling erosion, water pollution, climate 
change, and other similar issues (Puertas et al., 2023).  
 
However, these policies frequently overlook the perspectives and beliefs of individual farmers, 
who ultimately decide on the implementation and effectiveness of these policies, often leading 
to a lack of sustained impact (Kenny & Castilla-Rho, 2022).  A substantial educational gap 
exists in the dissemination of knowledge about regenerative farming practices (Hynninen, 
2024). Furthermore, farmers’ perceptions and beliefs about climate change can play a crucial 
role in influencing their adoption of regenerative farming practices (Alexanderson et al., 2023). 
==PAGE:26==
 
21 
In addition, changing entrenched ways of thinking and moving away from outdated and learned 
paradigms is a major challenge for companies, as prejudices often hinder the adoption of 
groundbreaking innovations (Hecker & Toivonen, 2024). Customer attitudes and behavior 
significantly influence the adoption rate of the circular economy, which incorporates elements 
of regenerative practices (Morseletto, 2020). Resistance may arise from a reluctance to 
change or uncertainty regarding new practices associated with circular approaches (Bianchini 
et al., 2019; Cantù et al., 2021). 
 
Studies acknowledge that investor awareness and education are essential to ensure an 
enabling market environment that supports regenerative business initiatives, and that 
coordination of all stakeholders is critical (Delmas & Burbano, 2011; Ryan et al., 2023).  
2.6 Drivers of Regenerative Business Practices 
 
 
Figure 4: Overview of the Drivers to the Adoption of Regenerative Business Practices (Source: Own Illustration) 
 
 
This chapter explores the various drivers that are encouraging businesses to adopt 
regenerative practices, highlighting how these forces collectively foster a robust framework for 
regenerative business practices. These drivers span economic benefits, regulatory incentives, 
rising consumer demand, and pressing ecological needs. 
2.6.1 Economic Drivers  
Organizations can be both regenerative and profitable, demonstrating that having a 
regenerative business model does not exclude the possibility of making a profit (Konietzko et 
al., 2023). Transitioning to regenerative business practices can lead to economic benefits 
(Zülfikaroglu, 2024; Haselsteiner et al., 2021). Emphasizing long-term sustainability and 
==PAGE:27==
 
22 
adaptability enhances the resilience of regenerative businesses to economic downturns and 
crises (Rostone Opex, 2024). 
 
As one example, regenerative agricultural practices can lead to improved soil health and could 
contribute to higher crop yields and lower input costs over time (Khangura et al., 2023; Dipu et 
al., 2022; Lemke et al., 2024), and studies have shown that no-till or minimal tillage practices 
positively affect crop yields and profitability (Khangura et al., 2023; LaCanne & Lundgren, 
2018). A study by the Rodale Institute found that regenerative farming techniques can 
sometimes increase crop yields by as much as 30% compared to traditional farming methods 
(Rodale Institute, 2024c; Hyman, 2020). Furthermore, the literature highlights the cost savings 
possible through the use of circular business practices, such as reducing waste and enhancing 
resource efficiency (Assmann et al., 2023). Furthermore, companies that use renewable 
energy systems like solar or wind energy in their operations will face lower long-term 
operational costs (Pagliaro, 2019; IRENA, 2021).   
 
Regenerative practices are also being promoted by companies to build brand equity and 
customer loyalty because purpose-driven companies often want consumers to align with their 
values and mission (Earthly, 2024). Businesses that successfully adopt regenerative 
sustainability practices can gain a competitive advantage over less sustainable counterparts, 
highlighting their commitment to innovation and long-term value creation (Rahman et al., 
2020). In particular, regenerative agricultural practices, for example, integrated into 
Patagonia’s supply chains, attract environmentally conscious customers and build brand 
equity (Sundheim, 2023). Sustainability strategies can provide a competitive advantage, 
especially in premium market segments (Orsato, 2009). It is reasonable to assume that this 
advantage can also be extended to regenerative products. 
2.6.2 Regulatory Drivers  
The regulatory framework plays an important role in creating opportunities for regenerative 
business practices (Haselsteiner et al., 2021). As demonstrated in the chapter Regulatory 
Barriers, regulatory frameworks can function as both a barrier and a driver. Regulatory 
frameworks are critical to promoting regenerative business practices, and policies are needed 
to encourage their adoption (Konietzko et al., 2023). Effective policies should integrate supply 
chain due diligence, true environmental pricing, and uphold nature and animal rights 
(Konietzko et al., 2023). Globally, the adoption of National Biodiversity Strategies and Action 
Plans (NBSAPs) as supported by the Convention on Biological Diversity is fundamental to 
aligning national efforts with global biodiversity objectives (Convention on Biological Diversity, 
2024; Konietzko et al., 2023). Additionally, fostering health and well-being through policies that 
support sustainable diets and reflect the true costs of goods on society and the environment 
is essential for promoting regenerative practices that are sustainable, equitable, and 
ecologically sound (Konietzko et al., 2023). 
 
Tax cuts, optimized strategies for public funding, and incentives are drivers of applying 
regenerative principles (Haselsteiner et al., 2021). For example, adjusting the EU’s Common 
Agricultural Policy to include subsidies for regenerative farming could encourage the adoption 
of regenerative agriculture practices (Chatterton et al., 2023). Around the world, governments 
are recognizing the need to support companies that contribute to environmental restoration 
(Aronson & Alexander, 2013; Suding et al., 2015).  
==PAGE:28==
 
23 
An example of emerging financial support for regenerative initiatives is the Regenerative 
Communities Fund (RCF), launched in 2024 by ECOLISE, marking a significant milestone as 
the first European fund specifically focused on supporting regenerative communities. Backed 
by €1.53 million from the European Union and an additional 10% co-financing from ECOLISE, 
the fund provides a total of €1.7 million aimed at directly funding community-led regenerative 
projects. The RCF seeks to empower existing communities and mobilize harder-to-reach 
groups, fostering an eco-social transition across Europe (ECOLISE, 2024). 
 
Another prominent example of financial support for regenerative initiatives is the Regenerative 
Fund for Nature, established in 2021 by Conservation International in collaboration with the 
global luxury group Kering, with Inditex joining in 2023. The fund focuses on supporting 
innovative agricultural practices and transforming fashion supply chains by providing grants to 
farming groups, project leaders, NGOs, and others. Its goal is to promote practices that ensure 
long-term land health while delivering benefits for farmers, nature, and the climate, thus 
addressing key environmental and social challenges within the industry (Regenerative Fund 
for Nature, 2023) 
 
Lastly, the One Planet Business for Biodiversity (OP2B) coalition, established by the World 
Business Council for Sustainable Development, focuses on scaling regenerative agriculture to 
address climate, biodiversity, and social challenges. By 2024, the coalition had mobilized $3.6 
billion in investments, enrolled 300.000 farmers, and aimed to impact over 12.5 million 
hectares of farmland by 2030, showcasing the transformative potential of public-private 
partnerships for sustainable food systems (Frank, 2024).  
 
Furthermore, certifications can serve as an influential regulatory or policy driver by establishing 
clear and enforceable standards for regenerative agriculture (Elevitch et al., 2018; Lemke et 
al., 2024). For example, the Regenerative Organic Certified program, developed by the Rodale 
Institute, sets a global benchmark for organic agriculture by setting strict criteria for soil health, 
animal welfare, and social fairness, ensuring comprehensive sustainable practices in food, 
fiber, and personal care ingredients (Regenerative Organic Certified, 2023). Haselsteiner et 
al. note that certifications are viewed positively and recognized as a key driver for regenerative 
practices. However, it is suggested that these certifications should be made available 
universally across all countries to maximize their impact and ensure a broader adoption of 
sustainable practices (Haselsteiner et al., 2021).  
 
Moreover, regenerative business practices aim to surpass carbon neutrality, targeting positive 
net-zero emissions (Konietzko et al., 2023). Ambitious goals set by the European Union, such 
as achieving carbon neutrality and reducing waste by 2050, facilitated by regulations like the 
EU Green Deal, support and align with these advanced objectives for businesses aiming for a 
more significant positive environmental impact (Perissi & Jones, 2022; Gómez, 2022). 
Furthermore, environmental policies compel EU firms to embrace eco-innovations that 
enhance recycling, minimize waste, and reduce material consumption (Cainelli et al., 2019). 
Another study pointed out that policies provide a holistic and integrated approach to 
environmental management by focusing on performance improvements over regulatory 
compliance, utilizing economic incentives for clean manufacturing and pollution prevention 
technologies, and establishing public-private partnerships to enhance environmental quality 
(Rondinelli & Berry, 2000). 
==PAGE:29==
 
24 
2.6.3 Rising Demand  
Consumer demand is increasingly becoming a driver for regenerative business practices, as 
studies reveal a growing preference for regeneration over sustainability. For instance, nearly 
80% of U.S. consumers desire brands to move beyond sustainability and commit to 
regeneration, viewing sustainability as too passive and unambitious. It must be mentioned that 
the study detailed the advantages of regenerative practices over sustainable practices. 
Customers predominantly favor businesses that prioritize principles like “doing good” and “true 
accountability” in their regenerative efforts (Olsen, 2019; World Economic Forum, 2024; Sayo, 
2024). Demand-side factors, including consumer preferences and market demand, encourage 
EU firms to adopt eco-innovations that enhance recycling, minimize waste, and reduce 
material use (Cainelli et al., 2019). Consumer surveys show strong support for environmentally 
and ethically sustainable products: According to a 2020 McKinsey survey, over 60% of U.S. 
respondents are willing to pay more for environmentally friendly packaging, and a NielsenIQ 
study found that 78% value a sustainable lifestyle (McKinsey & Company, 2020; NielsenIQ, 
2022). Also, there is a growing consumer interest in repaired, refurbished, and remanufactured 
products (Assmann et al., 2023; Guldmann & Huulgaard, 2020). Furthermore, investors are 
looking for opportunities to align their investments with ESG criteria (Eccles et al., 2017; Starks 
et al., 2017).  
2.6.4 Technological Drivers 
Technological advancements serve as a key driver for regenerative business practices, as 
new technologies fasten the progress toward processes that contribute to natural resources 
(Lacy et al., 2019). By utilizing traditional knowledge alongside modern technology, farmers 
can implement regenerative practices that reduce environmental impact and enhance 
agricultural yields (Schmidt, 2024). Additionally, precision agriculture allows for more precise 
control of resources like fertilizer, seed, chemicals, and water, which improves profitability, 
reduces environmental risks, and enhances product quality (Cook & Bramley, 1998).  
 
In finance, studies such as that by Nepal et al. (2024) show that green finance contributes 
significantly to energy resilience both directly and through mechanisms such as green 
technological innovations that further support regenerative practices (Nepal et al., 2024). 
Similarly, another study of China’s A-share listed companies shows that customers’ adoption 
of green technologies can increase suppliers’ total factor productivity through knowledge 
sharing, suggesting that the adoption of green technologies can lead to refined innovation 
models and improved productivity. (Yadav & Yadav, 2024). Moreover, studies like the one by 
Hills et al. (2018) show how improved planning at the social-technological interface can 
enhance community resilience and point to the potential of technology to improve societal well-
being (Hills et al., 2018). Technological innovations such as those driven by Industry 5.0, AI, 
and the Internet of Things are driving regenerative supply chains, promoting smart 
transportation, and facilitating the electrification of self-sufficient homes. This demonstrates 
how technology can be used to reduce waste, replace unsustainable resources, and renew 
natural systems. (Yadav & Yadav, 2024). 
 
 
==PAGE:30==
 
25 
3 Research Methodology 
3.1 Methodological Foundations and Framework 
This thesis employs a comprehensive methodology that combines qualitative and quantitative 
approaches to explore the adoption of regenerative business practices. The methodology 
prioritizes actionable results and adapts to the complexity of real-world challenges, making it 
particularly suitable for exploring regenerative practices.  
 
The study uses a mixed methods approach to balance depth and width in data collection. 
Qualitative expert interviews identify detailed narratives about industry-specific barriers and 
drivers, while a quantitative consumer survey shows broader trends in awareness and 
behavior toward regenerative practices. Together, these methods provide a robust 
understanding of the drivers, barriers, strategies, and industry- and regional differences 
associated with regenerative practices.  
 
This chapter provides the methodological basis for the study, starting with the theoretical 
foundations that determine the research design. It proceeds to describe the mixed methods 
approach in detail, illustrating how qualitative and quantitative methods are integrated to 
address the research objectives effectively.  
3.2 Research Design 
This thesis employs a mixed methods design, specifically a convergent parallel design. In this 
approach, qualitative and quantitative data are collected and analyzed simultaneously, and the 
findings are integrated to provide a holistic understanding of the research questions. The 
convergent design is particularly suited for this study because regenerative business practices 
include both industry challenges and broader consumer trends. By addressing these 
dimensions at the same time, the study ensures that findings from one method can inform and 
validate those from the other (Östlund et al., 2011).  
 
The qualitative component involves expert interviews aimed at uncovering the drivers, barriers, 
and strategies for adopting regenerative practices, analyzed using the Gioia Methodology. This 
in-depth approach allows the exploration of varying insights. The quantitative component is an 
online consumer survey that captures trends in awareness, preferences, and behaviors related 
to regenerative practices. It also discovers barriers and drivers through qualitative questions, 
offering a broader perspective on consumer influence and perception. 
 
The integration of qualitative and quantitative findings in the discussion chapter strengthens 
the validity of the study and provides actionable recommendations for businesses and 
policymakers. The detailed process of integration and analysis is described in the following 
chapters.  
==PAGE:31==
 
26 
3.3 Qualitative Methods: Expert Interviews 
3.3.1 Rationale for Qualitative Approach 
Expert interviews were chosen as the primary qualitative method to deeply investigate the 
barriers and drivers of regenerative practices. This approach allows to explore real-world 
barriers faced by professionals across industries, including, among others, regulatory 
constraints, financial constraints, technological limitations, and organizational resistance. It 
also highlights key drivers, such as regulatory incentives, consumer demand, and long-term 
financial benefits. The Gioia Methodology was applied to guide the analysis because it 
provides a structured framework to systematically transform empirical data into theoretical 
insights. The method is widely recognized for its ability to balance the perspectives of 
participants with broader theoretical generalizations (Gioia et al., 2013).   
3.3.2 Sampling and Participant Selection 
Experts were purposively selected using principles of purposive sampling (Patton, 2015), 
focusing on identifying key individuals with specialized knowledge relevant to the research 
objectives. The sample consisted of 15 participants from a variety of sectors. A list of all 
experts, showing their profession, their industry of expertise, and how they are referred to in 
this study, can be found in Figure 5. 
 
 
 
Figure 5: Anonymized Overview of Interviewed Experts (Source: Own Illustration)

firms that integrate sustainable practices in building and design.  
•

The industries were selected due to their varied degrees of engagement with regenerative 
practices, providing both sector-specific and cross-industry insights. This strategic choice 
included selecting experts from companies that actively implemented regenerative practices 
as well as professionals from organizations that integrated sustainable practices to learn from 
their challenges in further adopting regenerative practices. 
 
Criteria for the Selection 
 
• 
A minimum of five years of experience in their respective fields.  
• 
Active involvement in the implementation, study, or advocacy of regenerative practices 
within organizations that adopt such practices. In contrast, a deliberate inclusion of 
experts from organizations that do not employ such practices to understand the barriers 
they face.  
• 
Representation from diverse sectors to ensure a wide range of perspectives. This was 
particularly important for ensuring that the qualitative data reflected the complexity and 
diversity of regenerative business practices, contributing to a comprehensive analysis. 
3.3.3 Data Collection Process 
The expert interviews followed a semi-structured format, allowing flexibility while ensuring 
alignment with the research objectives. An interview guide was developed based on insights 
from the literature review, focusing on themes such as regulatory challenges, technological 
constraints, consumer demand, and stakeholder alignment. Additionally, the interview 
questions were continuously adapted based on findings from the preceding consumer survey 
to facilitate data triangulation. For instance, the survey revealed that labels were a significant 
factor for consumers regarding regenerative practices and that many consumers were 
unaware of what regenerative business practices cover. Therefore, specific questions were 
incorporated into the expert interviews to address these issues, such as asking if their 
companies use labels and, if not, why; and what initiatives are in place to educate the public 
about regenerative practices. This approach ensured that the results from different 
==PAGE:33==
 
28 
methodologies were coherent and complementary, enhancing the robustness of the study's 
conclusions.  
 
Before conducting the interviews, the guide was pilot tested with two experts to refine its clarity, 
relevance, and coherence. Feedback from this pilot included adjustments to terminology and 
the sequencing of questions to enhance comprehension. To ensure the relevance of 
discussions, the interview questions were designed to reflect the specific context of each 
participant's industry and expertise. This customization allowed for deeper insights into sector-
specific challenges and opportunities while maintaining consistency in addressing the research 
objectives.  
 
The expert interviews were conducted via email, virtual meetings, or face-to-face meetings at 
industry events such as Pest-Protect in Frankfurt in November 2024. Each meeting lasted 
approximately 30–60 minutes, and with participant consent, all interviews were recorded and 
subsequently transcribed for analysis. All interview questions and full transcripts are included 
in the external Appendix II. 
 
Example questions included:  
 
• 
“What are the key regulatory challenges your industry faces when adopting 
regenerative practices?”  
• 
“Which organizational resistances have been the most significant in your experience, 
and how were they addressed?”  
  
Special attention was paid to data protection and the adherence to ethical guidelines during 
data processing to preserve the anonymity and confidentiality of the information.  
3.3.4 Data Analysis 
The qualitative data received from the expert interviews were analyzed using the Gioia 
Methodology, as mentioned above. The analysis began with the transcription of all interviews, 
which were carefully reviewed multiple times to develop an in-depth understanding of the 
content. This phase allowed familiarizing with the data and identifying initial patterns and 
notable expressions.  
 
The first stage of analysis involved extracting First Order Concepts, which represented the 
interviewee’s language and terminology. These concepts were extracted directly from the data 
to ensure authenticity and to accurately reflect the participants’ perspectives. The second 
stage of analysis focused on developing Second Order Themes by grouping similar First Order 
Concepts. At this stage, an interpretive view was required that went beyond descriptive 
reporting to recognize broader patterns and relationships that occurred across the dataset. In 
the final stage, the Second Order Themes were consolidated into Aggregate Dimensions that 
highlight the main findings in relation to the research objectives of the study. These dimensions 
provided a higher-level understanding of the barriers, drivers, and strategies associated with 
regenerative practices.   
==PAGE:34==
 
29 
3.4 Quantitative Methods: Survey 
3.4.1 Rationale for Quantitative Approach 
The survey aims to quantitatively assess the influence of various factors such as education, 
awareness, and understanding on consumers’ attitudes and commitment to buying 
regenerative products. This approach provides a statistical basis to evaluate how these 
elements shape consumer preferences and drive their support for regenerative business 
practices. The survey specifically addresses Sub-Research Question 1, which examines the 
factors that influence consumer attitudes toward regenerative practices in order to better 
understand the dynamics that encourage or discourage consumer engagement with these 
practices. However, the survey also helps with strategy development for companies looking to 
adopt regenerative practices in the future, and it also provides some insights into potential 
barriers and drivers.  
3.4.2 Survey Design 
The survey was carefully structured to examine different aspects of consumer behavior, 
awareness, and preferences regarding regenerative business practices. It started by asking 
for essential demographic data such as age, gender identity, highest educational 
qualifications, employment status, and annual net income. This foundational data provided a 
context for analyzing patterns and trends across different demographic segments, highlighting 
how diverse groups interact with regenerative practices. 
 
The survey assessed participants' awareness of regenerative business practices, their 
understanding of the differences between these and sustainable practices, and the depth of 
this understanding. This segment was critical in identifying gaps in knowledge, showing the 
need for educational initiatives that not only introduce but also deepen the public's 
understanding of the benefits of regenerative practices. 
 
Questions regarding the commitment of consumers to regenerative products in their 
purchasing decisions revealed how much importance individuals attach to these practices 
when making purchases. This included inquiries into how often participants consider 
environmental or social responsibility in their buying choices, providing insights into the 
practical integration of regenerative practices in everyday consumer behavior. 
 
The survey also explored consumer demand for regenerative products at competitive and 
premium pricing, assessing preferences for these products compared to conventional ones 
when priced equally and exploring their willingness to pay more. This helped measure the 
market’s price sensitivity and the perceived value of products adhering to regenerative 
practices. 
 
The role of labels and certifications was examined to understand how they influence consumer 
trust and decision-making. This included identifying which types of certifications, such as those 
from independent organizations versus government labels, are most trusted by consumers. 
The importance of credible labeling in building consumer trust and facilitating informed 
decisions was highlighted. 
 
==PAGE:35==
 
30 
Participants were asked about their preferred sources of information for learning about 
regenerative practices. Options ranged from scientific studies to social media and direct 
communications from companies, aiming to identify effective channels for informing about 
regenerative practices. 
 
The survey analyzed what consumers expect from companies that use regenerative business 
practices to earn their trust, such as the need for certification and transparency in production 
processes. Additionally, it explored which benefits of regenerative practices are most valued 
by consumers, such as ecosystem restoration or improvements in soil and water quality and 
examined which product categories are seen as most crucial for the application of regenerative 
practices. 
 
Two open-ended questions were included to capture qualitative insights: participants were 
asked about any specific concerns they have about regenerative products and what measures 
could be taken to increase consumer acceptance of these products. These questions allowed 
participants to express differentiated views and concerns, providing a richer understanding of 
the barriers and opportunities for enhancing the acceptance of regenerative products. 
 
The survey’s comprehensive design, combining closed-ended questions with these qualitative 
inquiries, allowed for analyzing both quantitative data and extensive insights. The survey 
questions and responses were thoroughly documented in the chapter Results and Findings 
and in Appendix I under Descriptive Statistics. 
3.4.3 Sampling and Distribution 
The survey was completed by 252 participants, ensuring a diverse sample to examine 
consumer awareness, preferences, and attitudes toward regenerative business practices. 
Detailed demographic information about the participants is provided in the Descriptive 
Statistics. 
 
To minimize bias, questions were phrased neutrally, and participants were assured anonymity 
to encourage honest responses. Efforts were also made to reach diverse participant 
demographics through targeted online outreach. The survey was distributed through online 
platforms, including PollPool and social media channels, and two different companies to 
maximize reach and demographic diversity.  
 
The survey was distributed to approximately 1.000 individuals. Out of these, 252 participants 
completed the survey, resulting in a response rate of approximately 25.2%. Potential reasons 
for non-response may include a lack of interest in the survey topic, the length of the 
questionnaire, or difficulties in accessing the online survey platform. While this response rate 
is modest, it provides a representative sample size sufficient for the intended statistical 
analyses. The online distribution method of the survey ensured diverse demographic 
participation despite the non-response.   
3.4.4 Data Analysis  
The survey data were systematically analyzed using SPSS Statistics Software to extract 
meaningful insights and address the research questions effectively. The analytical process 
==PAGE:36==
 
31 
was designed to ensure both the accuracy and reliability of the findings through a series of 
structured steps.  
 
Data Cleaning and Preparation 
 
The data cleaning and preparation process was conducted systematically in SPSS to ensure 
the dataset's integrity, reliability, and suitability for subsequent analyses. This involved 
addressing missing data, standardizing responses, and reviewing variables for completeness 
and clarity. First, the dataset was examined for redundant or irrelevant variables. Columns with 
more than 50% missing data were excluded, as they lacked sufficient information to provide 
meaningful insights.  For handling missing data, different approaches were applied based on 
the type of variable. For Likert-scale variables, missing responses were excluded from 
analyses using list-wise deletion. This method ensured that only complete cases were 
included, avoiding the risk of distorting central tendencies by imputing arbitrary values. 
Similarly, missing values were excluded for binary variables, such as “Yes” or “No” responses, 
to prevent unnecessary bias. The overall proportion of missing data was carefully reviewed to 
confirm that these exclusions did not compromise the dataset's representativeness.  
 
To standardize the dataset, binary responses like “Yes” and “No” were recoded into numerical 
values (1 for “Yes” and 0 for “No”). The dataset was thoroughly prepared through these 
systematic steps, ensuring its accuracy and suitability for robust statistical analyses. This 
careful preparation provided a solid foundation for the subsequent exploration of consumer 
behavior and attitudes toward regenerative practices. Further variables had to be prepared for 
meaningful use in the regression and correlation. The “Income” variable (“What is your annual 
net income?”) was prepared, and the mean value was always formed within the income 
brackets. For example, for the income bracket €10.000 to €20.000, €15.000 was used as the 
midpoint value to make it easier to use the variables for regression and correlation. The 
variable “Education level” (“What is your highest level of education?”) was prepared as a 
category variable. Dummy variables were created for each category with “secondary 
education” as the reference category. The same procedure was followed for the “gender 
variable” with “man” as the reference variable. The midpoint variant was again used for “age” 
in order to facilitate comparability in SPSS. Dummy variables were again established for 
“Employment status,” and “Full-time employment” was coded as the reference variable. For 
the question “Would you be willing to pay more for a regenerative product?”, the answers were 
converted into numerical values (e.g. 2 = “up to 5% more”, 3 = “up to 10% more”). 
 
Descriptive Statistics 
 
The descriptive analysis of the survey data was conducted using SPSS to summarize 
demographic, behavioral, and attitudinal variables. This step provided foundational insights 
into the dataset, focusing on response distributions, means, and standard deviations to capture 
central tendencies and variability. For categorical variables, percentages and frequencies were 
calculated to present participant demographics and other response patterns. For Likert-scale 
variables mean values and standard deviations were computed to represent the overall trends 
and variability.  
 
==PAGE:37==
 
32 
The methodology ensured consistency across analyses and provided a comprehensive 
overview of participant responses, enabling possible meaningful interpretation of demographic 
and attitudinal data. This process also established a clear basis for subsequent inferential 
analyses, such as correlation and regression.  
 
Correlation Analysis 
 
The correlation analysis was conducted in SPSS to explore potential relationships between 
key variables in the dataset. This analysis aimed to identify statistically significant associations 
that could provide insights into consumer behavior and perceptions of regenerative business 
practices. The Spearman correlation coefficient (𝑟 ) was used to measure the strength and 
direction of relationships between variables, particularly suitable for ordinal data such as 
responses from Likert scales, which do not assume linearity or interval scaling (Hauke & 
Kossowski, 2011). The 𝑝-values were used to determine the statistical significance of these 
relationships. 
 
Relevant variables were selected, including for example demographic variables (e.g., income, 
education level, age), behavioral variables (e.g., attention to environmentally friendly 
production, willingness to pay more), and attitudinal variables (e.g., the importance of certified 
labels, understanding of regenerative practices).  
 
This method in SPSS calculated the Spearman correlation coefficients for all selected variable 
pairs. Two-tailed tests of significance were conducted to evaluate the 𝑝-values identifying 
statistically significant relationships (𝑝< 0,05). For each tested pair, the strength of the 
relationship was categorized based on the absolute value of 𝑟 : weak (0.1 ≤∣𝑟∣< 0.30), 
moderate (0.3 ≤∣𝑟∣< 0.5), and strong (∣𝑟∣≥0.5). Additionally, the direction of the 
relationships was documented.  
 
Regression Analysis 
 
Regression analysis played a central role in the methodology of the work. This analysis was 
conducted using SPSS, applying a multiple linear regression model to examine the 
relationships between several independent variables and the likelihood of consumers choosing 
products from regenerative sources over conventional products. This likelihood was quantified 
on a Likert scale from 1 (very unlikely) to 5 (very likely) and served as the dependent variable 
in the analysis. 
 
Independent variables included in the model included demographic and behavioral factors 
such as gender, highest educational qualification, annual net income, the influence of labeling 
on purchase decisions, interest in learning more about regenerative practices, and 
employment status. These variables were selected to capture a wide range of influences that 
could affect consumers' decisions. 
 
To ensure the validity of the regression analysis, a multicollinearity check was performed. 
Spearman correlation coefficients were calculated for all independent variables, none of which 
exceeded the threshold of 0.8, indicating that there were no significant multicollinearity issues. 
==PAGE:38==
 
33 
This step was crucial to ensure that the regression model provided reliable and interpretable 
results. 
 
The performance of the regression model was evaluated using various metrics. The R-squared 
value (𝑅²) quantified the proportion of variance in the dependent variable that could be 
explained by the independent variables, thus providing information about the effectiveness of 
the model. In addition, the regression coefficients (𝑏) were examined along with the 
corresponding 𝑝-values to determine the direction and statistical significance of the effects of 
each predictor on the dependent variable. This detailed analysis helped to clarify which factors 
significantly influence consumers' decision-making process when considering regenerative 
products and provided valuable insights into how various demographic and psychographic 
characteristics influence consumer behavior related to regenerative practices. 
 
Triangulation 
 
A key aspect of the analysis in this study was triangulation, integrating findings from qualitative 
and quantitative methods together with the findings from the Literature Review. This approach 
enabled a solid validation and contextualization of the data collected using different methods. 
For example, the barriers and drivers to the adoption of regenerative practices identified in the 
expert interviews were not only matched with the survey data to validate the patterns but also 
aligned with the research findings discussed in the literature review. Similarly, survey trends, 
such as consumer wishes for certifications for regenerative products, were contextualized 
using insights from experts.  
 
This iterative process of triangulation ensured that findings from the expert interviews and 
surveys complemented each other, creating an extensive understanding of the underlying 
dynamics influencing regenerative practices. By also incorporating literature analysis into the 
triangulation process, the study achieved a multi-dimensional perspective. 
3.4.5 Ethical Considerations 
Ethical principles were strictly adhered to throughout the study to ensure the rights, safety, and 
dignity of all participants. The research fully complied with the General Data Protection 
Regulation (GDPR) (Wolford, 2024). 
 
Informed Consent 
 
All participants were thoroughly informed about the purpose and scope of the survey before 
their participation. For the survey, consent was obtained electronically, with participants giving 
their consent before submitting their responses. For the expert interviews, consent was 
obtained by email, explaining the aims and procedures of the study in detail and obtaining the 
explicit consent of each participant. In addition, participants were verbally informed of their 
rights at the beginning of each interview, including the option to withdraw from the study at any 
time without consequences. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:39==
 
34 
Data Management and Confidentiality 
 
To ensure confidentiality, all collected data were anonymized. Interview transcripts were coded 
using pseudonyms to protect the identities of participants, and survey responses were stored 
without any identifying information. The original data of the interview were stored exclusively 
on the hardware of the project team, ensuring physical security and limited access. In 
compliance with ethical guidelines, the data will be retained for five years and subsequently 
destroyed.  
 
Risk Mitigation 
 
The research posed minimal risks to participants. However, potential concerns such as 
discomfort discussing professional challenges during interviews were mitigated by ensuring a 
non-judgmental and supportive interview environment. Participants were encouraged to leave 
out any information they were uncomfortable sharing.  
 
Voluntary Participation 
 
Participation in both the survey and interviews was entirely voluntary. Participants were under 
no obligation to complete the study and could withdraw without providing a reason. No 
incentives were provided to avoid compulsory behavior.  
 
Method-Specific Ethical Practices 
 
For the survey, questions were designed to avoid collecting sensitive personal data and to 
minimize potential discomfort. Open-ended questions allowed respondents to share insights 
freely without pressure. In the interviews, experts’ identities and the sensitive nature of 
industry-specific challenges were protected by anonymizing quotes and ensuring that 
identifiable details were omitted from the findings.  
3.4.6 Ensuring Rigor and Validity 
This study employed a variety of strategies to ensure the rigor and validity of both the 
qualitative and quantitative components, with particular emphasis on transparency, reliability, 
and alignment with methodological standards.  
 
Qualitative Rigor and Validity 
 
To uphold the rigor of the qualitative component, the study followed a systematic and 
transparent approach throughout data collection and analysis. A structured process was 
employed, beginning with the transcription of interviews and followed by iterative coding and 
thematic refinement. This ensured that the findings were consistently grounded in the 
participants' perspectives. The Gioia Methodology was central to this process, providing a 
framework for deriving theoretical insights. Using this method, the analysis systematically 
progressed from the identification of First Order Concepts, which reflected participants’ 
language, to the development of Second Order Themes and the grouping of Aggregate 
Dimensions.  
==PAGE:40==
 
35 
Rigor was further strengthened through regular peer reviews and reflective discussions among 
the project team. These measures helped to minimize researcher bias, ensuring that the 
coding and thematic development were both consistent and reliable. Reflexivity encouraged 
critical examination of assumptions, while peer reviews introduced diverse perspectives to 
challenge and refine the interpretations. To ensure the validity of the qualitative findings, 
triangulation was employed. Insights from expert interviews were cross-referenced with 
secondary sources, including academic literature, to validate the identified themes. This 
approach provided broader contextual support and strengthened the credibility of the findings. 
Additionally, a subset of participants was engaged in member-checking activities, where they 
reviewed preliminary results to confirm the accuracy and relevance of interpretations. Their 
feedback reinforced the credibility and authenticity of the study's conclusions.  
 
Quantitative Rigor and Validity 
 
The quantitative component adhered to rigorous methodological standards, ensuring reliability 
and validity in data collection and analysis. The survey design was informed by a thorough 
review of the literature and refined through pilot testing with a small group of participants. This 
pilot phase facilitated the optimization of question phrasing, structure, and clarity, ensuring that 
the instrument was accessible and comprehensible to a diverse participant pool. To maintain 
consistency and support robust statistical analysis, the survey employed standardized 
measures, including Likert scales. These tools allowed for precise measurement of key 
constructs.  
 
The validity of the survey instrument was assessed through content and construct validation 
processes. Survey questions were aligned with the research objectives and grounded in 
established theoretical frameworks. Pilot testing further ensured that the questions effectively 
captured the intended constructs and were free from ambiguity.  
 
Bias mitigation strategies were also an important part of the survey design. Questions were 
neutrally phrased to avoid leading responses, and randomized question ordering was 
employed to reduce potential order effects. Additionally, participant anonymity was assured to 
encourage honest and uninfluenced responses, minimizing the risk of social desirability bias.  
 
External validity was strengthened by ensuring the inclusion of a diverse participant pool 
through targeted outreach efforts. The use of stratified sampling techniques helped capture a 
broad demographic representation, enhancing the generalizability of the findings. of the target 
population.  
3.4.7 Limitations of the Methodology  
While the mixed methods approach employed in this study provides a robust framework for 
exploring regenerative business practices, it is important to acknowledge certain inherent 
limitations. Efforts were made to carefully mitigate these to ensure the reliability and validity of 
the findings. However, these limitations still impact the generalizability and scope of the study. 
 
The qualitative component relied on a relatively small sample size of 15 expert interviews. 
Each industry represented in the study typically included only 1-2 experts, which limits the 
generalizability of industry-specific insights. While the purposive sampling strategy ensured a 
==PAGE:41==
 
36 
diverse range of perspectives from multiple industries, the findings remain specific to the 
expertise and experiences of the participants. This limits the applicability of the results to 
broader industry contexts or global trends. 
 
Additionally, the regional focus of the interviews, covering EU level, national, and municipal 
perspectives, may not fully address global trends or challenges in adopting regenerative 
practices. The study provides valuable insights into the European context but does not 
extensively capture perspectives from regions with differing regulatory, economic, or social 
conditions. Future research could address this limitation by including a larger and more 
geographically diverse sample. 
 
The quantitative survey achieved a response rate of approximately 25.2%, with 252 completed 
responses out of approximately 1.000 individuals invited to participate. While this response 
rate falls within the acceptable range for online surveys (20-25%, SurveyLab, n.d.), it limits the 
generalizability of the findings, particularly for underrepresented demographic groups such as 
non-binary respondents and individuals from lower-income brackets. These gaps may skew 
the results, as the sample may not fully reflect the diversity of the broader population. 
 
The reliance on self-reported data introduces potential biases, as responses depend on 
participants' honesty, self-awareness, and subjective perceptions. This can lead to 
inaccuracies, particularly in sensitive areas such as personal attitudes toward regeneration. 
Additionally, the online survey distribution method, while ensuring accessibility, introduced 
non-response bias by potentially excluding individuals without consistent internet access or 
those less inclined to participate in surveys. Despite targeted outreach efforts to address 
demographic gaps and enhance inclusivity, the voluntary nature of the survey may have 
introduced self-selection bias. Respondents who participated may have been more engaged 
or interested in sustainability-related topics, potentially resulting in an overrepresentation of 
environmentally conscious perspectives. 
 
The survey also included open-ended questions to provide qualitative depth. While this 
approach yielded valuable insights, the variability in responses created challenges in 
standardizing and generalizing the findings. Moreover, the geographic distribution of the 
survey primarily targeted respondents in Germany, Denmark, and, to a lesser extent, the 
United States. This regional focus limits the applicability of the findings to other geographic 
areas and may not fully capture consumer behavior and attitudes toward regenerative 
practices in diverse cultural or economic contexts. 
 
Access to broader datasets posed another limitation. Industry-wide data, examples of 
regenerative principles adopted by companies, and proprietary company information were not 
readily available, which constrained the depth of certain analyses. While efforts were made to 
gather comprehensive insights, the study was limited by the availability of publicly accessible 
information.  
 
 
 
==PAGE:42==
 
37 
4 Results and Findings 
 
In the following chapter, the results of the survey and the expert interviews are presented in 
detail. This chapter is reserved for the neutral and descriptive presentation of the results. The 
interpretation of the results and the derivation of recommendations for action are dealt with in 
the following chapters: Discussion and Recommendations. 
 
For reasons of clarity and reading flow, this chapter is therefore divided into two large sections. 
The first section, Survey, provides an overview of the findings from the participants' responses 
and is organized into four subsections: The Descriptive Statistics provide an overview of the 
demographic characteristics and general trends in participants' attitudes. In the Correlation, 
the relationships between the key variables were analyzed to identify significant patterns within 
the data. The Regression investigates the factors that explain consumer behavior, focusing on 
the predictive variables. It also includes the Open-Ended Survey Responses to gain qualitative 
insights that complement the quantitative results.  
 
The second section, Expert Interviews, summarizes the findings from the interviews with 
industry experts, focusing on the following: Barriers that hinder the adoption of regenerative 
practices; Drivers that encourage or support these practices; Strategies suggested by experts 
to overcome challenges and capitalize on opportunities; and Industrie-Specific and Regional 
Differences to provide a tailored examination of outcomes relevant to specific sectors. 
4.1 Survey 
This chapter presents a survey-based, structured analysis of consumer perceptions and 
behaviors concerning regenerative business practices. The chapter first describes participants' 
demographic characteristics and attitudes towards regenerative practices, followed by 
correlation and regression analyses to identify significant relationships and predictors that may 
influence consumer preferences for regenerative products. It also includes an analysis of open-
ended responses that provide insight into consumer concerns and suggestions for increasing 
the acceptance of regenerative products. 
4.1.1 Descriptive Statistics 
The survey design aimed to capture both consumer behavior and perceptions of regenerative 
business practices, and a total of 252 responses were collected. The cleansing and processing 
of the data was carried out in a very structured way (see chapter 3). This way, invalid or 
incomplete answers were excluded in order to maintain the integrity of the dataset. The 
responses to all quantitative questions were analyzed, with demographic, behavioral, and 
attitudinal data being collected. 
How old are you? 
Firstly, the survey asked for demographic information to establish a baseline understanding of 
the participant profile. Age distribution shows the largest group of respondents falls into the 
25-34 age range, with 78 participants (30.95%), followed by 55-64 years (66 respondents, 
26.19%) and 45-54 years (37 respondents, 14.68%). Other age brackets include 35-44 (29 
==PAGE:43==
 
38 
respondents, 11.51%), 18-24 (16 respondents, 6.35%), 65 or older (14 respondents, 5.56%), 
and under 18 (12 respondents, 4.76%) (see Figure 21 in Appendix I). 
How do you identify? 
When asked about gender identification, 152 participants (60.32%) identified as men, 95 
(37.7%) as women, 3 (1.19%) preferred not to disclose, and 2 (0.79%) identified as non-binary 
(see Figure 22 in Appendix I). 
What is the highest level of education you have completed? 
The educational level was diverse, with secondary education completed by 68 respondents 
(26.98%), master’s degrees held by 60 participants (23.81%), and primary education 
completed by 59 respondents (23.41%). Other qualifications include bachelor’s degrees (42 
respondents, 16.67%), other degrees (14 respondents, 5.56%), doctorates (7 respondents, 
2.78%), and 2 individuals (0.79%) reporting no formal education (see Figure 23 in Appendix 
I). 
How would you describe your current employment status?  
In terms of employment status, 126 participants (50%) reported full-time employment, 39 
(15.48%) were students at the time of the survey, 27 (10.71%) worked part-time, and 21 
(8.33%) were self-employed (see Figure 24 in Appendix I). Other categories include retired (14 
respondents, 5.56%), apprentice/trainee (10 respondents, 3.97%), homemaker (9 
respondents, 3.57%), unemployed (4 respondents, 1.59%), and other employment statuses (2 
respondents, 0.79%). 
What is your annual net income (after taxes)? 
Respondents’ annual net income was in different ranges: €20.001–€40.000 was the most 
common income bracket, with 77 participants (30.56%) (see Figure 25 in Appendix I). Other 
ranges include €40.001–€60.000 (51 participants, 20.24%), €10.000–€20.000 (32 participants, 
12.7%), less than €10.000 (27 participants, 10.71%), €60.001–€80.000 (20 participants, 
7.94%), €80.001–€100.000 (6 participants, 2.38%), and more than €100.000 (19 participants, 
7.54%). Twenty participants (7.94%) preferred not to disclose. 
Have you ever heard of regenerative business practices before? 
Awareness of regenerative business practices was split, with 139 respondents (55.16%) being 
unfamiliar and 113 (44.84%) indicating prior awareness (see Figure 26 in Appendix I). 
If yes, how well do you understand the difference between sustainable and regenerative practices? 
When those aware were asked about their understanding of the difference between 
sustainable and regenerative practices, responses varied on a 1-to-5 Likert scale (1 = not at 
all important, 5 = very important). 41 (26.97%) respondents rated their understanding at 4, 40 
(26.32%) at 3, 28 (18.42%) at 1, 23 (15.13%) at 2, and 20 (13.16%) at 5, yielding a mean score 
of 3.01 (𝑆𝑡𝑎𝑛𝑑𝑎𝑟𝑑 𝐷𝑒𝑣𝑖𝑎𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛: 1.20). 
How important is the promotion of regenerative business models in your purchasing decision? 
When participants were asked, “How important is the promotion of regenerative business 
models in your purchasing decision?”, responses were measured on a Likert scale (1 = not at 
all important, 5 = very important). The distribution included 75 respondents (34.4%) rating this 
==PAGE:44==
 
39 
as moderately important (3), 50 respondents (22.94%) rating it 4, 34 respondents (15.6%) 
rating it both 1 and 2, and 25 respondents (11.47%) giving it the highest importance (5). The 
mean score is 2.99 (𝑆𝐷: 1.19). 
How often do you pay attention to whether a product is produced in an environmentally friendly way 
and/or in a socially responsible way? 
The question, “How often do you pay attention to whether a product is produced in an 
environmentally friendly way?” (see Figure 6), elicited varied responses: “Sometimes” was the 
most common response, with 114 participants (45.24%), followed by “often” (80 respondents, 
31.75%), “rarely” (37 respondents, 14.68%), “always” (11 respondents, 4.37%), and “never” 
(10 respondents, 3.97%). This question has a mean score of 3.03 (𝑆𝐷: 0.89).

Do you specifically buy products from companies that use regenerative practices?
