respondents, 37.6%), “yes, but rarely” (63 respondents, 25.2%), “no, never” (54 respondents, 
21.6%), “yes, often” (38 respondents, 15.2%), and “yes, always” (1 respondent, 0.4%). 
If regenerative and conventional products were available at the same price, how strongly would you 
prefer regenerative products? 
When evaluating preferences if regenerative and conventional products were available at the 
same price, the majority (165 respondents, 66.53%) indicated they would strongly prefer 
regenerative products (5 on a 1-to-5 Likert scale). Other responses included 44 (17.74%) rating 
their preference as 4, 28 (11.29%) as 3, 8 (3.23%) as 2, and 3 (1.21%) as 1. The mean score 
is 4.45 (𝑆𝐷: 0.93). 
How likely are you to choose a product made from regenerative sources over a conventional product? 
Regarding the likelihood of choosing a product made from regenerative sources over a 
conventional one, participants responded with ratings of 4 (31.3%, 𝑛=72), 5 (28.26%, 𝑛=65), 
3 (28.26%, 𝑛=65), 2 (10.43%, 𝑛=24), and 1 (1.74%, 𝑛=4). The mean score for this question is 
3.74 (𝑆𝐷: 1.02). 
How important would it be for you to have a certified label for regenerative products? 
The importance of a certified label for regenerative products scored a mean of 3.85 (𝑆𝐷: 1.06). 
The distribution included 78 respondents (34.36%) rating it 5 on a 1-to-5 Likert scale, 69 
(30.4%) rating it 4, 55 (24.23%) rating it 3, 17 (7.49%) rating it 2, and 8 (3.52%) rating it 1. 
If regenerative products were labeled accordingly, would this influence your purchasing decision? 
The influence of labeling regenerative products on purchasing decisions yielded a mean score 
of 3.90 (𝑆𝐷: 0.99), with responses distributed as follows: 82 respondents (35.81%) rated this 
as 4 on a 1-to-5 Likert scale, 76 respondents (33.19%) as 5, 48 respondents (20.96%) as 3, 
19 respondents (8.3%) as 2, and 4 respondents (1.75%) as 1. 
What type of regenerative product label would you trust the most? 
When evaluating the type of regenerative product label participants would trust most (see 
Figure 8), the most preferred was a label issued by independent environmental organizations 
(37.44%, 𝑛=85). This was followed by internationally recognized labels (33.92%, 𝑛=77), 
government-issued labels (22.91%, 𝑛=52), “none of the above” (3.96%, 𝑛=9), and internal 
company labels (1.32%, 𝑛=3). 
How much would a label for regenerative products influence your purchasing decision? 
When participants were asked, how much a label for regenerative products would influence 
their purchasing decision, responses were as follows: 79 respondents (35.59%) rated it 4 on 
a 1-to-5 Likert scale, 66 (29.73%) rated it 3, 48 (21.62%) rated it 5, 19 (8.56%) rated it 2, and 
10 (4.5%) rated it 1. The mean score is 3.61 (𝑆𝐷: 1.08).

What benefits of regenerative practices are most important to you when making a purchase decision? 
Participants identified several benefits of regenerative practices (see Figure 12) that they found 
important when making purchasing decisions. “Active restoration and protection of 
ecosystems” ranked highest (70%, 𝑛=161), followed by “products with a positive net impact” 
(59.57%, 𝑛=137), “circular economy” (58.26%, 𝑛=134), and “long-term soil and water quality 
improvement” (50.87%, 𝑛=117). Additional benefits included “enhancing biodiversity” (50.43%, 
𝑛=116), “regenerative procurement” (49.13%, 𝑛=113), “empowering local communities” 
(41.3%, 𝑛=95), “carbon sequestration technologies” (33.91%, 𝑛=78), and “integrating natural 
processes like permaculture” (19.13%, 𝑛=44). 
In which product categories are regenerative practices most important to you? 
When asked in which product categories (see Figure 11) regenerative practices were most 
important to the respondents, the most selected category was “food and beverages” (80.87%, 
𝑛=186). Other categories included “clothing and textiles” (74.78%, 𝑛=172), “electronics” 
(33.91%, 𝑛=78), “cosmetics and personal care” (32.61%, 𝑛=75), “household items” (25.22%, 
𝑛=58), “traveling” (25.65%, 𝑛=59), “other services” (7.83%, 𝑛=18), and “financial services” 
(6.96%, 𝑛=16). 
==PAGE:48==
 
43 
 
 
 
 
 
 
What do you expect from a regenerative company to do to earn your trust? 
Participants were surveyed on what they expect from a regenerative company to earn their 
trust (see Figure 14), with multiple answers allowed. The most frequently cited expectation 
was “certification and independent testing”, selected by 158 respondents (70.85%). This was 
closely followed by “detailed insights into production processes” (69.51%, 𝑛=155) and “long-
term environmental goals” (56.05%, 𝑛=125). Additional expectations included a “commitment 
to community-benefiting projects” (39.46%, 𝑛=88) and “incorporation of customer feedback” 
(26.91%, 𝑛=60).

Lastly, the survey explored the participants' preferred sources of information (see Figure 13)

(36.56%, 𝑛=83), “social media” (32.6%, 𝑛=74), “recommendations from friends and family” 
(30.4%, 𝑛=69), and “direct company information” (15.42%, 𝑛=35).  
4.1.2 Correlation 
A correlation analysis was conducted to identify the relationships between the key variables in 
the dataset. The objective was to determine which connections are statistically significant, thus 
providing insights into consumer behavior and perceptions of regenerative business practices. 
In this study, Spearman's rank correlation coefficient was employed to evaluate the strength 
and direction of associations between variables. This method is particularly suitable for ordinal 
data or when the assumptions of linearity and interval scaling required for Pearson's correlation 
do not hold, ensuring a more appropriate analysis of the relationships within the dataset. Many 
of the hypotheses tested did not yield significant results in identifying correlations (𝑝 >  0.05), 
but several notable relationships, shown in Figure 15 can be outlined. In the table, all tested 
variables are summarized with their correlation coefficient and 𝑝-value to describe the 
relationships identified by the analysis.  
 
Firstly, the relationship between annual net income (“What is your annual net income?”) and 
the willingness to pay more for a regenerative product (“Would you be willing to pay more for 
a regenerative product?”) was examined. It was hypothesized that individuals with higher 
income levels would be more likely to accept price premiums. However, the Spearman 
correlation coefficient was 0.008 with a 𝑝-value of 0.24, indicating that the relationship is not 
statistically significant.

Understanding the difference between sustainable and regenerative practices (“How well do 
you understand the difference between sustainable and regenerative practices?”) showed a 
significant positive correlation with the perceived importance of regenerative business models

(correlation coefficient: 0.20, 𝑝 =  0.012. Participants with a better understanding of 
regenerative practices were more likely to consider these business models important. 
The analysis also examined whether the interest of the participants in learning more about 
regenerative practices (“How interested would you be in learning more about regenerative 
practices and their benefits?”) was influenced by their age (“How old are you?”). The results 
showed a correlation coefficient of 0.07 (𝑝 =  0.28), indicating no meaningful relationship 
between age and the level of interest in learning about regenerative practices. This suggests 
that interest in this topic is not strongly related to age demographics. 
Furthermore, the link between annual net income (“What is your annual net income?”) and the 
preference for regenerative products (“If regenerative and conventional products were 
available at the same price, how strongly would you prefer regenerative products?”) was 
investigated. It was assumed that higher-income participants would prefer regenerative 
options. However, a correlation coefficient of 0.03 (𝑝 =  0.63) shows no significant correlation. 
Attention to environmental and social responsibility (“How often do you pay attention to 
whether a product is produced in an environmentally friendly way and/or in a socially 
responsible way?”) was hypothesized to correlate with purchasing behavior for regenerative 
products (“Do you specifically buy products from companies that use regenerative practices?”). 
This analysis resulted in a correlation coefficient of 0.42 and a 𝑝−𝑣𝑎𝑙𝑢𝑒 <  0.001, indicating 
a statistically significant relationship. Participants who paid closer attention to environmental 
responsibility were more likely to purchase regenerative products. 
A strong and significant relationship was found between the importance of regenerative 
business models (“How important is the promotion of regenerative business models in your 
purchasing decision?”) and the likelihood of choosing a product made from regenerative 
sources over a conventional one (“How likely are you to choose a product made from 
regenerative sources over a conventional product?”) with a correlation coefficient of 0.41 (𝑝 <
 0.001).  
Similarly, the importance of a certified label for regenerative products (“How important would 
it be for you to have a certified label for regenerative products?”) showed a strongly positive 
correlation with the influence of Labeling Regenerative Products on purchasing decisions (“If 
regenerative products were labeled accordingly, would this influence your purchasing 
decision?”). The correlation coefficient is 0.71, with a 𝑝−𝑣𝑎𝑙𝑢𝑒<  0.001.  
The link between choosing a product made from regenerative sources over a conventional one 
(“How likely are you to choose a product made from regenerative sources over a conventional 
product?”) and the influence of certified labels on purchasing decisions (“How much would a 
label for regenerative products influence your purchasing decision?”) was examined. The 
correlation coefficient is 0.58, with a 𝑝−𝑣𝑎𝑙𝑢𝑒<  0.001, indicating a significant positive 
relationship.  
Participants’ interest in learning more about regenerative practices (“How interested would you 
be in learning more about regenerative practices and their benefits?”) was significantly 
correlated with both the importance of the promotion of regenerative business models 
(correlation coefficient: 0.42, 𝑝 <  0.001) and the influence of a label for regenerative products 
on purchasing decisions (“How much would a label for regenerative products influence your 
==PAGE:51==
 
46 
purchasing decision?”) (correlation coefficient: 0.62, 𝑝 <  0.001). This highlights the 
connection between consumer education and their perceptions of regenerative practices and 
labeling.  
Finally, the link between participants’ stated preference for regenerative products at the same 
price as conventional ones (“If regenerative and conventional products were available at the 
same price, how strongly would you prefer regenerative products?”) and their likelihood of 
choosing a product made from regenerative sources over a conventional one (“How likely are 
you to choose a product made from regenerative sources over a conventional product?”) was 
analyzed. The correlation coefficient is 0.60, with a 𝑝−𝑣𝑎𝑙𝑢𝑒 <  0.001, demonstrating a strong 
positive correlation.

education, annual net income, the influence of labeling on purchasing decisions, interest in 
learning about regenerative practices, and employment status. A multiple linear regression 
model was applied due to the continuous nature of the dependent variable and the inclusion 
of several independent predictors. The full results of the analysis, including regression 
coefficients (𝑏), 𝑝-values, and their significance levels, are summarized in Figure 16. 
 
Before conducting the regression analysis, multicollinearity was tested to ensure the validity of 
the model. No correlation coefficient among the independent variables exceeded 𝑟= 0.8, 
confirming the absence of multicollinearity and validating the predictors included in the model. 
The regression model explained a substantial portion of the variance in the dependent variable, 
achieving a 𝑅!-value of 0.429. This indicates that approximately 42.9% of the variability in 
participants’ likelihood of choosing regenerative products can be explained by the predictors 
included in the model.  
 
Independent Variables and Their Effects 
Gender 
Gender was included as a categorical variable, with “Man” as the reference category. Women 
were significantly more likely to choose regenerative products (𝑏= 0.15, 𝑝= 0.04). Non-binary 
participants showed a positive but non-significant effect (𝑏= 0.22, 𝑝= 0.06). However, this 
result should be interpreted cautiously due to the small sample size of non-binary respondents 
(𝑛 =  2). 
Highest Level of Education 
Using “Secondary education” as the reference category, the results showed varying effects 
across educational levels: 
 
• 
Primary Education: Participants with primary education were less likely to choose 
regenerative products, but the effect was not significant (𝑏= −0.05, 𝑝= 0.42). 
• 
Bachelor’s Degree: No significant difference was observed for those with a bachelor’s 
degree (𝑏= 0.08, 𝑝= 0.28). 
• 
Master’s Degree: Participants with a master’s degree were significantly more likely to 
choose regenerative products (𝑏= 0.20, 𝑝= 0.03). 
• 
Doctorate: Individuals with a doctorate also showed a significant positive effect  
(𝑏= 0.25, 𝑝= 0.02). 
• 
Other Degrees: Participants with other types of education did not indicate a significant 
effect (𝑏= 0.06, 𝑝= 0.34). 
• 
No Formal Education: Respondents reporting no formal education were less likely to 
choose regenerative products (𝑏= −0.12, 𝑝= 0.40), though this was not statistically 
significant. 
Annual Net Income 
Income demonstrated a significant positive effect (𝑏= 0.29, 𝑝= 0.02), indicating that 
participants with higher income levels were more inclined to choose regenerative products.  
==PAGE:53==
 
48 
Influence of Labeling 
The influence of labeling on purchasing decisions proved to be the strongest predictor (𝑏=
0.43, 𝑝< 0.01). Participants who rated the labeling as highly influential were significantly more 
likely to prefer regenerative products. 
Interest in Learning About Regenerative Practices 
Interest in learning more about regenerative practices was another significant predictor (𝑏=
0.31, 𝑝= 0.01). Participants showing strong interest in gaining knowledge about regenerative 
practices were more likely to prefer regenerative options. 
Employment Status 
Employment status was categorized using “Full-time employee” as the reference group. Varied 
effects were observed: 
 
• 
Students: Students were significantly more likely to choose regenerative products (𝑏=
0.21, 𝑝= 0.05). 
• 
Part-Time Employees: No significant difference was observed for part-time employees 
(𝑏= 0.10, 𝑝= 0.18). 
• 
Self-Employed Individuals: The effect was positive but not significant  
(𝑏= 0.08, 𝑝= 0.32). 
• 
Unemployed Individuals: Unemployed participants displayed no significant effect  
(𝑏= 0.19, 𝑝= 0.07). 
• 
Retired Individuals: Retirees did not show a significant difference (𝑏= 0.04, 𝑝= 0.45). 
• 
Apprentices/Trainees: Apprentices and trainees showed no significant effect  
(𝑏= 0.06, 𝑝= 0.38). 
• 
Homemakers: Homemakers demonstrated no significant effect (𝑏= 0.03, 𝑝= 0.49). 
• 
Other roles: Participants in other employment categories displayed no significant 
difference either (𝑏= 0.07, 𝑝= 0.44). 
4.1.4 Open-Ended Survey Responses 
Concerns About Regenerative Products 
 
The open-ended survey question: “Do you have any specific concerns about regenerative 
products? If so, what are they?” yielded many responses. These were analyzed and grouped 
into categories based on the most frequently mentioned topics. The categories below highlight 
the main concerns and include some quotes from respondents that illustrate each theme. 
Cost and Affordability 
Many participants expressed concern about the high cost of regenerative products, describing 
them as unaffordable for the average consumer. “They are too expensive and unaffordable for 
average earners, acting as inflation drivers” (Participant 11). “As long as there are so many 
people earning low wages, regenerative products will remain irrelevant because most simply 
cannot afford them” (Participant 36). Another participant noted, “They will most likely not be 
competitively priced compared to most conventional alternatives” (Participant 8). Additional 
concerns were raised: “I’m concerned about the high cost of regenerative products” 
==PAGE:54==
 
49 
(Participant 1), and “Unfortunately, sometimes artificially overpriced” (Participant 20). 
Participant 28 suggested that “The availability of cheaper alternative products could make it 
difficult for regenerative products to enter the market.” Finally, Participant 40 stated, 
“Regenerative products are often more expensive to produce, which is reflected in higher retail 
prices. This can deter consumers, especially in price-sensitive markets.” These answers 
highlight the fact that the perceived higher costs are a significant barrier to the broader 
adoption of regenerative products. 
Availability 
The availability and scalability of regenerative products was also mentioned as an important 
concern. Participants highlighted difficulties in sourcing these products and challenges in their 
production processes. “The production of regenerative products often requires specific raw 
materials or methods, which are not always available in sufficient quantity or quality,” explained 
one respondent (Participant 40). Another commented: “It’s hard to find these products in local 
stores” (Participant 1).  
Credibility and Greenwashing 
Participants frequently questioned the credibility of regenerative product claims and raised 
concerns about greenwashing. “Frequently, 'Greenwashing' is used to deceive consumers to 
increase companies' profit margins, which has significantly eroded trust and transparency” 
(Participant 17). Concerns about authenticity were also expressed: “Fake certificates or a lack 
of independent verifications are major issues” (Participant 42), and “Often, labels on packaging 
claim sustainability, but upon closer inspection, only a small part of the product is actually 
regenerative” (Participant 26). Another participant stated: “The effectiveness or sustainability 
of regenerative products is sometimes not sufficiently proven. Companies risk accusations of 
greenwashing if their products do not offer clearly defined, verifiable benefits” (Participant 40). 
The importance of reliable and independent certifications was stressed by many participants. 
“Without proper independent certifications and audits, regenerative practices risk becoming 
just another form of greenwashing” (Participant 47). Additionally, the significance of 
certification credibility was underscored: “Certifications must be credible and verified by 
independent entities” (Participant 33), and “The trustworthiness of labels is crucial” (Participant 
29). 
Information and Awareness Gaps 
A significant gap in information and awareness was noted. “More information is needed” 
(Participant 2). Participants expressed difficulties in trusting regenerative practices due to 
insufficient data: “How exactly do you want to check all this?” (Participant 45). Additionally, the 
need for better understanding was highlighted: “How are these practices actually controlled, 
tested, and researched?” (Participant 3). 
Efficacy and Quality 
Concerns about the efficacy and quality of regenerative products were shared by many 
participants. “I question whether they are truly better for the environment and how they affect 
our health and well-being long term” (Participant 3). Quality concerns were also raised: “The 
quality of these products should not suffer” (Participant 25), and “The production methods 
seem overly complicated, driving up costs without adding clear value” (Participant 37). Another 
participant added: “Products made from alternative materials (e.g. bio-based plastics) can be 
==PAGE:55==
 
50 
met with skepticism or reservations, especially if they differ from conventional products in terms 
of function or aesthetics” (Participant 40). 
 
Strategies to Increase Consumer Acceptance of Regenerative Products 
 
The open-ended question, “What measures could be taken to increase consumer acceptance 
of regenerative products?” yielded a wide variety of responses. These were analyzed for 
content and grouped by the frequency of strategies mentioned. The categories below outline 
different approaches, with quotes from respondents illustrating each measure. 
Affordability and Pricing  
A large number of participants reported that cost was a significant barrier to the adoption of 
regenerative products and provided suggestions for making such products more affordable. 
For instance, one participant stated, “To increase acceptance, more affordable options should 
be available” (Participant 1), highlighting the need for cost-effective alternatives in the market. 
Another comment was, “Mostly less cost and easy accessibility” (Participant 4), emphasizing 
the importance of reducing prices. Additional feedback included “Lower prices, more detailed 
descriptions” (Participant 10). Similarly, the need for price parity with conventional products 
was underscored by the comment, “Price parity with conventional products” (Participant 37). 
Transparency, Certifications, and Education  
Participants also pointed out that transparency in the production and marketing of regenerative 
products would help to gain consumer trust. One participant pointed out the need to be 
“transparent/authentic about the process, benefits, and risks” (Participant 3). Another 
participant suggested “clear communication about the production methods, for example 
through labels, QR codes, or short videos” (Participant 61) to provide detailed information to 
consumers. In addition, direct interaction with the products through stands or workshops was 
suggested, where “people can explore, ask questions, address their concerns, compare the 
quality, durability, etc.” (Participant 3), emphasizing the importance of hands-on experiences. 
 
Other responses recognized the role of certifications in validating the ethical claims and 
regenerative efforts of regenerative products. The need for “independent certification and 
verification” (Participant 32) was emphasized as essential to ensure authenticity. In addition, 
participants called for “certificates and information on the product” (Participant 29) to be easily 
accessible to consumers at the point of sale to ensure greater transparency. 
 
Educating consumers about the benefits of regenerative products and explaining why these 
products might be more expensive was also seen as crucial to increasing acceptance. 
Participants emphasized the need for “providing more information and highlighting the benefits 
to society” (Participant 2). Education on “how a product contributes to improvement and why it 
might incur additional costs” (Participant 22) was also called for. To familiarize consumers with 
regenerative products, it was noted to do “more extensive education and publicity” (Participant 
15). Participant 9 emphasized the importance of “communicating the societal benefits as a 
whole to justify the likely higher price point,” while Participant 1 pointed to the effectiveness of 
public awareness campaigns that “can help people understand the benefits and make these 
products more accessible in everyday markets.” 
==PAGE:56==
 
51 
Marketing and Communication  
Good advertising and word-of-mouth were highlighted as critical components. “Good 
advertising and word-of-mouth would play a major role” (Participant 23), suggesting that these 
traditional methods remain effective in spreading the word about regenerative products. The 
need for “transparent advertising” (Participant 54) was also stressed. The role of influencer 
campaigns was discussed, with suggestions to “use influencer campaigns: authentic 
advocates to emotionally reach consumers” (Participant 61). The lack of awareness was a 
concern for some, as one participant admitted, “Not sure. I have never heard of it. So, it needs 
to be advertised better” (Participant 5). In addition, the influence of emotionally charged 
marketing was mentioned. “Brutal marketing that appeals to the consumer's conscience” 
(Participant 53) and “emotional marketing—storytelling: telling stories about the effects of 
regenerative products, for example, how they improve habitats” (Participant 61). 
Quality and Accessibility  
The quality and availability of regenerative products were cited as key factors in increasing 
consumer acceptance. According to participants, these products should “should not 
compromise on quality” (Participant 8). The direct link between quality and availability was 
emphasized in comments such as “quality and availability” (Participant 16), indicating that the 
accessibility of quality products is important. Other responses emphasized that regenerative 
products should offer “good value for money and better quality” (Participant 51). In addition, 
the accessibility of such products was emphasized, as noted in one comment: “Regenerative 
products should be easily accessible – online and offline” (Participant 61). 
Regulatory Support and Incentives  
One participant noted that “attractive pricing through government subsidies could stimulate 
demand” (Participant 12), indicating that financial incentives from the government could lower 
prices. This approach was taken up by another participant who mentioned “subsidies or tax 
breaks for regenerative products to make them more price attractive” (Participant 61). Another 
participant stated the same: “Low entry prices through strong government subsidies” 
(Participant 53).  
4.2 Expert Interviews 
The findings from the expert interviews are presented in this section, structured to address the 
research questions systematically. The data is categorized into Barriers, Drivers, Strategies, 
and Industrie-Specific and Regional Differences to provide an overview of the insights 
gathered. 
 
The interview data was analyzed using the Gioia Methodology, offering a transparent and 
structured approach. This makes it possible to organize raw data (First Order Concepts) into 
higher-level themes (Second Order Themes) and Aggregate Dimensions, ensuring clarity and 
rigor in the presentation of results. By applying this framework, the results remain closely linked 
to the collected data while offering a structured categorization for easy understanding 
(Magnani & Gioia, 2023). 
 
==PAGE:57==
 
52 
It is important to note that this section is an exclusively descriptive presentation of the results. 
The interpretation, implications, and recommendations are presented in the following chapters. 
This chapter presents the raw data and the themes that emerge from the material without 
further analysis to ensure objective and neutral reporting of the expert interviews. 
4.2.1 Barriers 
First Order Concepts 
 
This chapter presents a selection of First Order Concepts as identified through the Gioia 
Methodology. The presentation includes quotes by the questioned experts about the barriers 
to implementing regenerative practices. The First Order Concepts were derived directly from 
the interview data and include the detailed challenges faced by the organizations. To maintain 
transparency and provide robust evidence, each concept is supported by direct quotes from 
the interviewees, which are discussed below. This text is accompanied by a structure tree that 
lists all First Order Concepts (see Figure 17). A full list of all quotes can be found in Appendix 
I under First Order Concepts, Second Order Themes, and Aggregate Dimensions. 
 
Several experts emphasized the lack of market mechanisms to compensate for the additional 
costs of regenerative practices. Sustainability Manager 1 explained, “The market is not yet 
paying for this kind of extra aspects in the product.” Derived First Order Concept: [Market unwilling 
to pay for regeneration costs - Interview 1]. A Head of Sales noted that “The high costs 
associated with developing regenerative technologies compared to conventional solutions 
represent a financial burden” [High costs of developing regenerative technologies compared 
to conventional solutions - Interview 14]. For smaller entities, high upfront costs present a 
significant hurdle: “Initial investments in GPS navigation and camera-assisted hoeing 
technology are significant barriers, especially for smaller farms” [High initial costs for 
regenerative technology - Interview 6]. This financial burden is intensified by general economic 
challenges, as Sustainability Consultant 1 points out: “Global crises exacerbate economic 
challenges, limiting investments in regeneration and sustainability” [Economic barriers for 
small and medium-sized enterprises - Interview 5]. Furthermore, the economic focus on 
affordability over regenerative practices often dictates consumer choices. Sustainability 
Manager 4 observed, “Consumers often choose cheaper, less regenerative products due to 
affordability issues” [High costs deter adoption of regenerative options - Interview 10].  
 
The experts frequently referred to the fragmentation and incoherence of the regulatory 
frameworks, which creates systemic challenges. The Municipal Sustainability Project Leader 
stated, “Established structures, such as legislation and organizational silos, make it 
challenging to adopt regenerative practices” [Regulatory silos hinder systemic change - 
Interview 2]. The Renewable Energy Asset Analyst noted the difficulty in navigating the 
different and overlapping regulations: “There are many different regulations across Europe, 
making it challenging to keep up with and comply in different countries” [Navigating regulatory 
complexity across regions - Interview 11]. Furthermore, the “High regulatory requirements for 
developing and approving new technologies pose a significant barrier” [High regulatory 
requirements for developing and approving new technologies - Interview 14]. 
 
Companies also face high compliance costs, as explained by a participant: “The reporting 
obligations of the CSRD are enormous, but they establish essential standards”  
==PAGE:58==
 
53

==PAGE:59==
 
54 
[High documentation demands from regulations - Interview 3]. Sustainability Manager 3 stated: 
“Laws like the Circular Economy Act promote circular approaches and increase manufacturer 
responsibility, adding complexity.” [Complex regulatory requirements - Interview 4]. 
 
Additionally, the absence of standardized definitions, such as for ‘regenerative agriculture,’ 
allows for misuse and greenwashing: “The term ‘regenerative agriculture’ is not protected, 
allowing companies to misuse it for marketing without substantial action” [Lack of regulatory 
clarity and misuse of terms - Interview 6]. Greenwashing further clouds consumer 
understanding: “Greenwashing is pervasive, making it hard for consumers to differentiate real 
efforts” [Mistrust created by greenwashing practices - Interview 10]. Additionally, “NGOs and 
state regulations occasionally create contradictory pressures that can obstruct the 
implementation of regenerative principles” [Contradictory pressures from NGOs and state 
regulations - Interview 14]. 
 
Architect 1 observed, “The biggest challenge is the culture of doing it and the expectations of 
what you do” [Cultural inertia limits innovation - Interview 7]. This resistance is particularly 
prevalent in the traditional sectors, as one interviewee noted, “Many organizations are resistant 
to shifting away from business-as-usual practices” [Cultural resistance in traditional industries 
- Interview 5]. This cultural resistance is often due to the financial orientation of organizations: 
“The driving factor in our field is and was always money” [Economic focus hinders regenerative 
adoption - Interview 7]. Resistance from leadership increases these challenges even more. 
Sustainability Consultant 2 explained, “If leadership doesn’t consistently push regeneration, 
inertia will lead companies back to their old practices” [Leadership resistance to regenerative 
shifts - Interview 9]. Farmers also face resistance that is based on longstanding practices, as 
one participant noted: “Farmers often resist change because they are stuck in old habits and 
practices” [Resistance to change in farming culture - Interview 8]. As Architect 2 highlighted, 
“It took 60 years for the insights of the Club of Rome to be internalized. If it takes that long 
again, the disaster will already have occurred” [Slow societal and industry shifts - Interview 
15]. 
 
Furthermore, many experts emphasized the gap in consumer understanding, with the 
Sustainability Consultant 1 noting, “Most companies don't even know what regenerative 
practices entail and have no mindset for it yet” [Lack of awareness about regenerative 
practices - Interview 5]. Similarly, consumers often oversimplify regenerative issues: “The 
biggest challenge is to explain the complexity of regeneration to our customers” [Oversimplified 
customer expectations - Interview 1]. Furthermore, another expert stated, that “Convincing 
customers of the added value of regenerative products is challenging when cost or 
performance differences are present” [Difficulty convincing customers of the added value of 
regenerative products when cost or performance differences exist - Interview 14] and that the 
“The societal lack of understanding of the complexity of regenerative practices and the 
challenges faced by the industry impedes their acceptance” [Societal lack of understanding of 
the complexity of regenerative practices and the industry’s challenges - Interview 14]. 
 
Another interviewee highlighted the confusion caused by misleading labeling: “Most labels lack 
substance and confuse consumers rather than building trust” [Confusion from untrustworthy 
sustainability labels - Interview 9]. In addition, the societal shift towards a more regenerative 
way of consuming is taking place very slowly, as noted by Sustainability Manager 4: “A 
==PAGE:60==
 
55 
paradigm shift toward consuming fewer, better-quality products takes at least a generation” 
[Slow societal shift toward regenerative consumption - Interview 10]. 
 
While many technologies for regenerative practices already exist, organizations often delay 
action: “All the necessary technologies already exist, but companies sometimes wait for 
innovations instead of acting now” [Dependency on new technologies delays action - Interview 
9]. Operational challenges extend to supply chains, with Sustainability Manager 3 stating, “97% 
of our greenhouse gas emissions come from Scope 3, where we have the least influence” 
[Limited influence on Scope 3 emissions - Interview 4]. Recycling inefficiencies highlight 
another operational challenge. An expert explained, “Previous poor processes make materials 
difficult to reuse” [Recycling inefficiencies due to past designs - Interview 7]. Scalability issues 
further complicate the adoption of new materials, as one participant noted, “There are a lot of 
materials on the market, but not all of them make sense, especially if they’re not scalable” 
[Scalability challenges for new materials - Interview 1]. 
 
The complexity of aligning diverse stakeholder interests was another recurring theme. 
“Farmers face conflicting demands from consumers, landlords, and environmental goals” 
[Conflicting demands from stakeholders - Interview 6]. According to Sustainability Manager 2, 
this discrepancy also extends to other sectors, “Involving all stakeholders, from employees to 
customers, in a unified commitment is particularly challenging” [Difficulty in uniting 
stakeholders - Interview 3]. 
 
Lastly, the lack of comprehensive policy support for ecological farming was noted. The Organic 
Farmer stated, “The lack of comprehensive soil protection and climate laws makes it harder to 
justify regenerative practices” [Limited policy support for ecological farming - Interview 8]. 
Furthermore, the expert added, “There are no policies that financially reward ecological 
services provided by regenerative farming” [Limited policy support for ecological farming - 
Interview 8]. 
 
Second Order Themes 
 
The analysis of barriers to the adoption of regenerative practices, structured using the Gioia 
Methodology, identifies eight key Second Order Themes. These each include several related 
First Order Concepts. The Second Order Themes highlight the complexity and 
interconnectedness of the challenges faced by organizations and stakeholders. In the 
following, the Second Order Themes are presented together with their corresponding First 
Order Concepts to emphasize their scope and importance. A detailed overview can be found 
in the attached structure tree (see Figure 17). 
Regulatory Complexity and Policy Barriers 
Regulatory Complexity was identified as a significant barrier to the implementation of 
regenerative practices. First Order Concepts such as [Regulatory silos hinder systemic change 
- Interview 2] and [Lack of regulatory incentives for regeneration - Interview 7] emphasize 
systemic challenges faced by organizations. Bureaucratic hurdles were also highlighted, in 
concepts like [High documentation demands from regulations - Interview 3] and [Navigating 
regulatory complexity across regions - Interview 11]. Challenges related to supply chains and 
compliance were reflected in concepts such as [Regulatory constraints on supply chain 
==PAGE:61==
 
56 
flexibility - Interview 13] and [Focus on compliance over innovation - Interview 5]. Further 
complexity was identified in the collaboration between different stakeholders, as expressed in 
[Contradictory pressures from NGOs and state regulations - Interview 14]. A lack of supporting 
frameworks and standards was another recurring theme, reflected in concepts such as [Limited 
policy support for ecological farming - Interview 8] and [Lack of industry-wide standards for 
material reuse - Interview 13]. These findings underscore the multiple regulatory and policy 
challenges that organizations face in adopting regenerative practices. 
Economic and Financial Constraints 
Financial and economic barriers were also mentioned often, containing concepts such as 
[Market unwilling to pay for regeneration costs - Interview 1] and [High costs of sustainable 
and regenerative materials and processes - Interview 4]. These restrictions are furthermore 
reinforced by [Economic barriers for small and medium-sized enterprises - Interview 5], [High 
initial costs for regenerative technology - Interview 6], and the [High costs of developing 
regenerative technologies compared to conventional solutions - Interview 14]. Unequal 
resource distribution, as captured by the concept [Uneven resource distribution limits adoption 
- Interview 2], and [Limited budgets for extensive research and development - Interview 14], 
further restrict organizations' ability to invest. The focus on financial growth, exemplified by 
concepts like [Focus on financial growth limits regeneration - Interview 10], shows how deep 
economic pressures reach. 
Cultural Resistance and Behavioral Challenges 
Cultural inertia and resistance to change are significant barriers, reflected in concepts such as 
[Cultural resistance in traditional industries - Interview 5] and [Resistance to change in farming 
culture - Interview 8]. Behavioral challenges extend to consumers, with [Oversimplified 
customer expectations - Interview 1] and [Slow societal shift toward regenerative consumption 
- Interview 10] showing the difficulties in fostering broader acceptance of regenerative 
practices. Leadership also plays a role, with [Leadership resistance to regenerative shifts - 
Interview 9] highlighting the importance of top-down support for transformation. 
Operational and Supply Chain Challenges 
This Second Order Theme emerges as a barrier, with concepts like [Limited influence on 
Scope 3 emissions - Interview 4] and [Challenges in coordinating global supply chains - 
Interview 3] emphasizing the complexity of aligning operational practices with regenerative 
goals. Recycling inefficiencies, as captured by [Recycling inefficiencies due to past designs - 
Interview 7], and the [Circularity challenges in aviation recycling - Interview 13] reveal the 
limitations of current systems in supporting circular approaches. Additionally, [Challenges in 
scaling regenerative methods - Interview 6] show the difficulties of scaling innovative solutions. 
Awareness, Education, and Demand Limitations 
A lack of awareness among consumers and businesses presents significant challenges, as 
shown in concepts like [Lack of awareness about regenerative practices - Interview 5] and 
[Educational gaps among farmers and consumers - Interview 6]. Miscommunication and 
misinformation, exemplified by [Confusion from untrustworthy sustainability labels - Interview 
9] and [Mistrust created by greenwashing practices - Interview 10], further hinder the adoption 
of regenerative practices. The First Order Concepts [Difficulty convincing customers of the 
added value of regenerative products when cost or performance differences exist - Interview 
==PAGE:62==
 
57 
14] and [Societal lack of understanding of the complexity of regenerative practices and the 
industry’s challenges - Interview 14] further complicate the implementation of regenerative 
business practices. 
Technological Barriers 
This Second Order Theme focuses on issues like [Dependency on new technologies delays 
action - Interview 9]. The lack of comprehensive solutions, such as [Undefined end-of-life 
solutions - Interview 1], reinforces these challenges.  
Stakeholder Alignment Challenges 
The complexity of stakeholder alignment is identified as a critical Second Order Theme, with 
concepts such as [Difficulty in uniting stakeholders - Interview 3] and [Conflicting demands 
from stakeholders - Interview 6] highlighting the challenges of creating consistent strategies. 
Misalignment between regenerative goals and core business priorities, as shown in [Difficulty 
in aligning regeneration with core business - Interview 9], further complicates efforts to make 
progress. 
Market Limitations 
Structural barriers within markets prevent the widespread adoption of regenerative practices. 
Issues like [Restricted access to land and resources - Interview 2] and [Challenges in securing 
land and addressing local opposition - Interview 11] underline the challenges faced by 
producers and innovators in entering or expanding within the market. 
 
Aggregation 
 
The employed Gioia Methodology resulted in the identification of several First Order Concepts, 
which again resulted in eight Second Order Themes. These themes were further grouped into 
five Aggregate Dimensions that reflect the underlying connections between the themes and 
simplify the complexity of the dataset. Figure 17 offers a detailed breakdown of all First Order 
Concepts and their connections to Second Order Themes and Aggregate Dimensions. 
Regulatory and Policy Barriers 
Concepts such as [Regulatory silos hinder systemic change - Interview 2] and [Burden of 
extensive reporting requirements - Interview 9] demonstrate the complexity of regulatory and 
policy barriers. By grouping these insights, this Aggregate Dimension presents challenges 
related to fragmented regulations and procedural inefficiencies that hinder the adoption of 
regenerative practices. 
Resource and Market Barriers 
This dimension combines Economic and Financial Constraints & Market Limitations, as both 
are about the limitations of financial resources, economic structures, and market dynamics. 
The underlying reason for this grouping is the shared impact of resource and market 
inefficiencies on the feasibility of regenerative practices. Issues such as [High initial costs for 
regenerative technology - Interview 6] and [Uneven resource distribution limits adoption - 
Interview 2] show the financial and structural barriers that prevent widespread adoption.  
==PAGE:63==
 
58 
Cultural, Behavioral, and Knowledge Barriers 
This dimension integrates Cultural Resistance and Behavioral Challenges & Awareness, 
Education, and Demand Limitations, as both themes address barriers related to societal, 
organizational, and individual perceptions and actions. For instance, [Cultural resistance in 
traditional industries - Interview 5] and [Educational gaps among farmers and consumers - 
Interview 6] underline how inflexible mindsets and lack of awareness hinder progress. 
Structural Barriers 
This dimension merges Operational and Supply Chain Challenges with Technological Barriers, 
as both address the foundational infrastructure and technological capabilities necessary for 
regenerative practices. An emphasis on systemic and technological constraints is common to 
concepts in this dimension. Examples such as [Dependency on new technologies delays 
action - Interview 9] and [Recycling inefficiencies due to past designs - Interview 7] show how 
reliance on emerging technologies and inefficiencies in existing systems prevents regeneration 
efforts.  
Stakeholder Collaboration Barriers 
This dimension includes Stakeholder Alignment Challenges, which focus on the difficulties of 
uniting different actors in achieving regenerative goals. Concepts such as [Difficulty in uniting 
stakeholders - Interview 3] and [Minimal influence of external partners - Interview 12] 
emphasize how misaligned interests and lack of collaboration undermine collective action.  
4.2.2 Drivers 
First Order Concepts 
 
This chapter presents a selection of First Order Concepts developed from the expert 
interviews, along with direct quotes for the drivers of regenerative practices. These First Order 
Concepts are derived from the Gioia Methodology and highlight the key motivators and 
enablers identified through the interview data. A structural tree diagram visualizes the First 
Order Concepts (see Figure 18). The complete list of First Order Concepts and their supporting 
quotes can be found in Appendix I under First Order Concepts, Second Order Themes, and 
Aggregate Dimensions.  
 
While regulations are sometimes perceived as a barrier, many experts recognize their role in 
driving regeneration. One respondent explained, “Regulations are a driver, even if initially 
viewed as a burden.” Derived First Order Concept: [Regulatory pressures create urgency - 
Interview 5]. These policies also encourage systemic change, as highlighted by another 
participant: “The EU Taxonomy and Green Deal drive sustainable and regenerative practices 
forward” [Advancing regeneration through regulatory frameworks - Interview 4]. Additionally, 
the Head of Sales stated: “Regulatory measures often promote regenerative approaches” 
[Regulatory measures often promote regenerative approaches - Interview 14]. National-level 
initiatives further reinforce this impact. For instance, one expert noted, “Laws like the Circular 
Economy Act promote circular approaches and increase manufacturer responsibility, adding 
complexity” [Advancing regeneration through regulatory frameworks - Interview 4].

==PAGE:65==
 
60 
Similarly, government incentives and subsidies create opportunities for growth in renewable 
sectors: “Policies and support schemes from governments are beneficial for scaling renewable 
projects” [Supportive policies and subsidies for renewable growth - Interview 11]. 
 
The Experts stated that collaboration across industries and stakeholders is vital for achieving 
regeneration goals and that partnerships are essential in fostering innovation and building 
systemic change. One respondent stated: “Partnerships play a crucial role in developing and 
implementing innovative approaches.” [Collaboration as a driver for regenerative innovation - 
Interview 2]. Similarly, another participant emphasized: “Working across industries accelerates 
the adoption of regenerative models” [Cross-industry collaboration enables regeneration - 
Interview 5]. Partnerships also enable resource-sharing and co-development of standards, as 
noted: “We have close partnerships with suppliers and regularly discuss ways to advance 
together” [Collaborative networks for regenerative progress - Interview 4]. Furthermore, 
community partnerships play a crucial role in aligning operational goals with local needs: 
“Involving local communities and implementing biodiversity policies create goodwill and 
facilitate project approvals” [Community partnerships and engagement - Interview 11]. 
 
Technological advancements simplify implementation and make regenerative practices more 
accessible, as Sustainability Consultant 1 observed: “Technology enables access to tools and 
systems that make regenerative practices more accessible” [Technological advancements as 
an enabler for regeneration - Interview 5]. Precision agriculture technologies are particularly 
effective, as noted: “Modern tools like GPS navigation and precision agriculture help streamline 
regenerative practices” [Technological advancements supporting regenerative agriculture - 
Interview 6]. Innovation is also driven by the development and scaling of pilot projects: 
“Prototyping and pilot projects are essential for testing new approaches and fostering 
acceptance” [Pilot projects showcase potential, Interview 2]. 
 
Furthermore, organizations use advancements to achieve measurable impacts: “The Science-
Based Targets Initiative helps us measure the success of our actions scientifically” [Innovation 
and technological advances for regenerative practices - Interview 4]. 
 
The experts made clear that growing consumer interest in regeneration is a significant driver 
for regenerative practices. Sustainability Manager 1 noted, “There is a real shift – consumers 
are asking the right questions more and more” [Growing consumer interest in regeneration - 
Interview 1]. Public demand catalyzes regeneration, as highlighted by the growing expectation 
from customers for transparency and responsible action: “Our customers expect transparency 
and responsible action” [Public demand as a catalyst for regeneration - Interview 3]. Some 
corporate clients are also pushing for regeneration, as the following statement shows: “Large 
corporate clients set regeneration criteria, pushing to innovate and comply” [Consumer 
demand and corporate clients - Interview 10]. Additionally, consumer preferences for 
sustainable and regenerative products help drive farming practices: “Consumers are 
increasingly interested in sustainable and regenerative produced food, driving farms to adopt 
regenerative methods” [Growing consumer demand for sustainability and regeneration - 
Interview 6]. 
 
Furthermore, several possible economic advantages of regeneration, evident in market 
opportunities and operational efficiencies, were pointed out. Many organizations recognize the 
potential for long-term profitability, as one respondent stated: “Regenerative practices offer 
==PAGE:66==
 
61 
long-term market advantages despite high initial costs” [Economic and market opportunities 
for regenerative practices - Interview 5]. The business benefits are further enhanced by cost 
savings and efficiency gains: “Regeneration often means focusing on resource use, which can 
save costs and align with long-term business objectives” [Economic viability and resource 
efficiency as a regeneration driver - Interview 9]. Financial support also plays a role: 
“Sustainability funds from corporate partners provide financial support for regenerative 
initiatives” [Corporate interest and funding as financial enablers - Interview 6]. Circular 
practices contribute to both cost efficiency and regeneration goals: “Reusing serviceable parts 
reduces costs for airlines while promoting regeneration and sustainability” [Cost efficiency via 
circular practices - Interview 13]. 
 
Leadership commitment and cultural transformation are essential for embedding regeneration 
into organizational values, as one interviewee stated, “If regeneration is embedded in the 
company’s purpose, it drives long-term growth” [Embedding regeneration in corporate purpose 
- Interview 1]. Internal initiatives, such as employee training, also strengthen ESG goals: 
“Employee training is crucial to deeply integrate ESG principles within the company” [Internal 
culture supports ESG goals - Interview 3]. Personal commitment is another motivator, as 
underlined by one farmer’s vision: “I have seven grandchildren, and I want them to have a 
future” [Personal commitment and long-term vision - Interview 8].  
 
Organizations recognize regeneration as a unique selling proposition, as one participant 
specified: “Regeneration is becoming a unique selling proposition (USP) that differentiates 
from competitors.” [Competitive advantage and profitability through regeneration - Interview 
10]. Regeneration also helps attract and hold talent, especially among younger generations: 
“Younger generations expect transparency and responsibility from employers, making 
sustainability and regeneration a key driver for attracting talent” [Attracting talent through 
regenerative practices - Interview 9]. Moreover, a positive market perception strengthens the 
business benefits of regeneration: “The industry and customers have an extremely positive 
perception of regenerative practices” [Positive market perception - Interview 12]. 
 
Second Order Themes 
 
The analysis of drivers for adopting regenerative practices identifies eight key Second Order 
Themes, which are also visualized in Figure 18. In the following, the Second Order Themes 
are presented, accompanied by examples of associated First Order Concepts. 
Regulatory and Policy Drivers 
The analysis revealed that regulatory and policy frameworks play a significant role in shaping 
regenerative practices. First Order Concepts such as [Regulatory pressures create urgency - 
Interview 5] and [Advancing regeneration through regulatory frameworks - Interview 4] 
highlight the importance of external governance in driving change. Specific policy initiatives, 
including the EU Taxonomy and the Green Deal, were reflected in concepts like [Regulation 
as a catalyst for regeneration - Interview 9] and [Government regulations as a catalyst - 
Interview 10]. In addition, mechanisms such as subsidies and incentives to comply with 
regulations were mentioned as important topics, captured in the concept [Supportive policies 
and subsidies for renewable growth - Interview 11]. 
==PAGE:67==
 
62 
Collaboration and Partnerships 
The role of collaborative efforts was highlighted in the interviews through concepts such as 
[Collaboration as a driver for regenerative innovation - Interview 2] and [Cross-industry 
collaboration enables regeneration - Interview 5]. Examples like [Community partnerships and 
engagement - Interview 11] and [Partnerships with recyclers and researchers are crucial - 
Interview 1] reflect the emphasis on collective actions and shared networks to support 
regenerative initiatives. 
Technological Innovation and Advancements 
First Order Concepts such as [Innovation and technological advances for regenerative 
practices - Interview 4] and [Technological advancements as an enabler for regeneration - 
Interview 5] emphasize the importance of emerging tools and systems in promoting 
regenerative approaches. Practical implementations were underlined in concepts like [Pilot 
projects showcase potential - Interview 2] and [Effective use of existing technologies - Interview 
7], showing how scalable innovations can drive progress. Additionally, businesses’ proactive 
measures were reflected in concepts such as [Focus on non-chemical solutions and scouting 
for innovative global technologies - Interview 14]. Lastly, the importance of technology in 
specific sectors was further supported by concepts like [Technological advancements 
supporting regenerative agriculture - Interview 6] and [Technological advancements as a driver 
- Interview 11]. 
Consumer and Market Demand 
First Order Concepts such as [Growing consumer interest in regeneration - Interview 1] and 
[Growing consumer demand for sustainability and regeneration - Interview 6] show the 
increasing attention of consumers toward regenerative practices. Additionally, the concept of 
[Growing public awareness for regeneration - Interview 5] further highlights the importance of 
societal awareness in market dynamics. Both public and corporate clients are adapting their 
expectations, as seen in concepts like [Public demand as a catalyst for regeneration - Interview 
3] and [Consumer demand and corporate clients - Interview 10]. Societal trends are also 
reflected in concepts like [Shift towards a sharing economy - Interview 7], while specific market 
opportunities are captured in [Financial opportunities from over spenders - Interview 7].  
Economic and Financial Benefits 
Concepts such as [Economic and market opportunities for regenerative practices - Interview 
5] and [Economic viability and resource efficiency as a regeneration driver - Interview 9] 
emphasize how regeneration aligns with business objectives. Financial mechanisms like 
[Economic viability through PPAs (Power Purchase Agreements) - Interview 11] and [Cost 
efficiency via circular practices - Interview 13] show the practical benefits of implementing 
regenerative approaches. Furthermore, [Economic benefits for customers and their end 
customers - Interview 12] highlights the direct financial advantages for businesses and their 
stakeholders. 
Leadership and Cultural Change 
Other concepts such as [Embedding regeneration in corporate purpose - Interview 1] and 
[Pioneering spirit and leadership as a catalyst for regeneration - Interview 4] show the 
importance of leadership in driving change. Internal cultural alignment is emphasized in 
==PAGE:68==
 
63 
[Internal culture supports ESG goals - Interview 3], while the significance of individual 
dedication is reflected in [Personal commitment and long-term vision - Interview 8]. 
Environmental Benefits 
Practical benefits associated with regenerative practices are shown in First Order Concepts 
like [Environmental benefits of regeneration - Interview 6] and [Environmental resilience and 
soil health benefits - Interview 8]. Specific outcomes, including [Potential for carbon 
sequestration - Interview 8] and [Fuel efficiency as a regeneration driver - Interview 13], further 
demonstrate the contributions of regeneration to sustainability and resource efficiency. 
Competitive Advantage 
Regenerative practices can offer a competitive advantage, as pointed out by the First Order 
Concept of [Competitive advantage and profitability through regeneration - Interview 10]. 
Attracting talent, as seen in the concept of [Attracting talent through regenerative practices - 
Interview 9], and enhancing market perception, shown by [Positive market perception - 
Interview 12], further exemplify the business benefits of regeneration. Furthermore, 
[Innovation-driven differentiation from competitors - Interview 14] and an [Enhanced public 
image and reputation through regenerative initiatives - Interview 14] demonstrate the added 
value of regenerative approaches in building brand strength and market leadership. 
 
Aggregation 
 
In order to summarize these findings in a clear framework, the Second Order Themes were 
grouped into five Aggregate Dimensions. The following dimensions aim to provide an 
understanding of the various drivers of regenerative practices. Figure 18 illustrates the 
relationships between First Order Concepts, Second Order Themes, and their corresponding 
Aggregate Dimensions. 
Regulatory Drivers 
This dimension centers on the Second Order Theme of Regulatory and Policy Drivers, which 
highlights the role of structured governance and supportive frameworks in advancing 
regenerative practices. Examples such as [Regulatory pressures create urgency - Interview 5] 
and [Government regulations as a catalyst - Interview 10] show how supportive policies can 
serve as important enablers for regenerative business. Additionally, concepts like [Supportive 
policies and subsidies for renewable growth - Interview 11] underline the importance of 
financial and legislative incentives in encouraging systemic change. 
Collaborative Drivers 
This dimension is about the Second Order Theme of Collaboration and Partnerships, which 
specifies the importance of joint efforts in advancing regenerative practices. Examples such 
as [Cross-industry collaboration enables regeneration - Interview 5] and [Community 
partnerships and engagement - Interview 11] emphasize how cooperative networks and 
shared goals contribute to addressing barriers and fostering regenerative progress. 
Technological Drivers 
The role of innovation in enabling impactful change is underlined by this dimension, focusing 
on the Second Order Theme of Technological Innovation and Advancements. Concepts like 
==PAGE:69==
 
64 
[Pilot projects showcase potential - Interview 2] and [Technological advancements as an 
enabler for regeneration - Interview 5] demonstrate how emerging tools and practical solutions 
help organizations to implement and scale regenerative practices effectively. 
Market Dynamics and Financial Incentives 
Economic forces and market behavior are central to this dimension. It includes the Second 
Order Themes of Consumer and Market Demand, Economic and Financial Benefits, and 
Competitive Advantage. Together, they show the combined influence of consumer 
expectations, financial mechanisms, and competitive positioning in driving regenerative 
practices. 
 
For instance, [Growing consumer demand for sustainability and regeneration - Interview 6] 
reflects shifting market priorities, while [Economic viability and resource efficiency as a 
regeneration driver - Interview 9] shows the benefits of aligning financial goals with 
regenerative approaches. Additionally, [Competitive advantage and profitability through 
regeneration - Interview 10] recognizes how regenerative practices can improve market 
differentiation.  
Leadership and Strategic Transformation Drivers 
The Second Order Theme of Leadership and Cultural Change is included in this dimension, 
which points out the role of leadership and organizational alignment in supporting the cultural 
change necessary for regeneration. First Order Concepts such as [Pioneering spirit and 
leadership as a catalyst for regeneration - Interview 4] and [Embedding regeneration in 
corporate purpose - Interview 1], for example, demonstrate the importance of forward-looking 
leadership. 
Environmental Drivers 
The Second Order Theme of Environmental Benefits is included in this dimension, 
emphasizing how environmental advantages resulting from regenerative business practices 
can function as key drivers for regenerative practices. For example, the First Order Concept 
[Environmental resilience and soil health benefits - Interview 8] shows the importance of 
healthy ecosystems in ensuring long-term regeneration, while [Potential for carbon 
sequestration - Interview 8] specifies how regeneration can help to address climate change 
actively. 
4.2.3 Strategies 
First Order Concepts 
 
This chapter presents a selection of First Order Concepts and the corresponding expert quotes 
to introduce strategies used by organizations to implement regenerative practices. The First 
Order Concepts contain the various approaches and solutions by the interviewees. A structure 
tree is shown in Figure 19 to categorize all First Order Concepts, Second Order Themes, and 
Aggregate Dimensions. Furthermore, a full list of concepts and supporting quotes is available 
in Appendix I under First Order Concepts, Second Order Themes, and Aggregate Dimensions. 
 
==PAGE:70==
 
65 
The experts emphasized integrating regenerative goals into product design. Sustainability 
Manager 1 noted, “80% of a product’s environmental impact is determined during the design 
phase.” Derived First Order Concept: [Embedding sustainability and regeneration goals early in the 
design phase - Interview 1]. This focus is further reflected in strategies like “Products with a 
lifespan of 10–20+ years and rechargeable batteries offer sustainable alternatives to biocides” 
[Development of long-lasting IoT products - Interview 12]. Similarly, Architect 1 highlighted, 
“We should do less and make things more durable” [Rethinking product design for durability - 
Interview 7], stressing the commitment to long-lasting materials. 
 
Sustainability Manager 1 stated, “Regeneration is complex, and we need to ensure that our 
customers understand why our approaches make sense” [Communication is key to explaining 
complex regenerative concepts - Interview 1]. Additionally, public engagement was 
emphasized by Sustainability Consultant 1: “Public and corporate education is a critical starting 
point for broad adoption” [Education and awareness campaigns - Interview 5]. Strategies 
included targeted efforts, such as “We educate consumers through stories on our website and 
transparent communication about materials” [Consumer education and sales training - 
Interview 10]. Trust-building was another priority: “Educating consumers through honest and 
transparent communication helps build trust in regenerative claims.” [Transparency builds 
consumer trust - Interview 10]. 
 
Collaboration was named by many experts as the foundation for systemic change. As the 
Municipal Sustainability Project Leader explained, “Collaborating with businesses can help 
develop and implement shared standards” [Strengthening partnerships for systemic change - 
Interview 2]. Another expert highlighted, “Cross-industry partnerships provide platforms for 
innovation and resource-sharing” [Fostering partnerships and collaboration - Interview 5]. 
Specific examples include, “We collaborate with local developers, lawyers, and advisors for 
regulatory compliance and operational management.” [Local partnerships for operations and 
compliance - Interview 11]. 
 
The Experts pointed out that organizations should leverage regulations as opportunities for 
change. Sustainability Consultant 1 observed, “Adaptation to EU regulations can be used as 
a springboard for deeper regenerative practices” [Leveraging regulatory frameworks as 
opportunities - Interview 5]. Similarly, stricter regulations were identified as drivers: “We 
support stricter government regulations to create a fair competitive environment and prevent 
greenwashing” [Advocating for stricter regulations - Interview 10].  
 
Public procurement was also highlighted: “Municipal procurement principles should be 
extended throughout the value chain to drive broader adoption” [Leverage public procurement 
to influence the value chain - Interview 2]. 
 
The interviews also revealed that technology enables scalable and efficient regenerative 
practices. As Sustainability Manager 2 noted, “Digitalization enables us to make progress 
measurable” [Adopt data-driven approaches for transparency - Interview 3]. Investments in 
technology were also emphasized: “Access to modern technologies enables better 
implementation and tracking of regenerative actions” [Investing in technology - Interview 5] 
and “Digital tools close the gap between ambition and execution” [Investing in technology - 
Interview 5].

Furthermore, pilot projects were mentioned as being essential by Sustainability Manager 3: 
“Scaling pilot projects into economically viable models is central to our efforts” [Scaling pilot 
projects into viable models - Interview 4]. 
 
The Interviews showed that efficiency strategies address barriers and help organizations 
optimize resources. Architect 1 emphasized, “We cannot solve challenges if everything sits 
idle. They need to be in circulation and in use” [Maximizing the use of existing resources -
Interview 7]. Step-by-step approaches, as mentioned in “Farmers can start with simple steps 
like cover cropping and gradually expand their practices” [Incremental implementation of 
regenerative methods - Interview 6], emphasizing the role of gradual implementation in 
adopting regenerative methods. Long-term infrastructure investments further strengthen 
efforts, as noted by the Pest Control Solutions Manager: “An electric vehicle fleet and 
photovoltaic systems are long-term investments in sustainability and regeneration” 
[Investments in durable infrastructure - Interview 12]. Additionally, promoting reuse and 
retrofitting was highlighted as a key strategy: “Rather than demolishing buildings, retrofitting 
and repurposing should become the standard approach to preserve resources and energy” 
[Promoting reuse and retrofitting - Interview 15]. 
 
In addition, one expert stated, “Transformation starts with a shift in mindset and leadership 
priorities” [Embedding regeneration in corporate culture - Interview 5]. Another respondent 
emphasized, “A big cultural change is needed about what we expect things to do and how they 
should circulate” [Promoting cultural change - Interview 7]. Internal leadership was also 
highlighted by Sustainability Consultant 2, “Our leadership model avoids bonuses, rotates 
positions, and gives employees influence” [Setting an example internally - Interview 9]. 
 
Nature-based strategies align ecological principles with regeneration. The Organic Farmer 
noted, “We need 17% of farmland to be covered by hedgerows to restore ecological balance” 
[Implementing biodiversity measures - Interview 8]. Other approaches leverage natural 
processes: “Worm composting and minimal soil disturbance create self-sustaining 
ecosystems” [Leveraging existing natural processes - Interview 8]. Practices like “Agroforestry 
and mixed forests increase water infiltration and biomass production” [Integrating agroforestry 
systems - Interview 8] further showcase ecosystem-based solutions. 
 
Furthermore, the interviews showed that long-term strategies prioritize scalability and 
measurable success. Sustainability Manager 3 stated, “Scientific milestones and measurable 
progress guide our strategy” [Scientific milestones and strategic measurement - Interview 4]. 
Also, pilot projects help to get started: “Demonstrating early successes builds confidence and 
encourages further adoption” [Building confidence through early successes - Interview 6]. 
Additionally, future goals are emphasized: “Our long-term goal is to help reverse global 
warming by becoming CO₂-negative” [Focusing on long-term goals and scaling innovation - 
Interview 4]. 
 
The experts also stated that certifications and transparency support consumer trust in 
regenerative products. As Sustainability Manager 1 shared, “Certifications should guide the 
design process from the beginning, not just at the end” [Certifications make it easier for 
customers to trust our products - Interview 1]. The Regenerative Agriculture Advisor stated, 
“Certification for regenerative agriculture can help establish trust and avoid greenwashing” 
[Enhancing transparency through certifications - Interview 6]. Architect 2 emphasized, 
==PAGE:73==
 
68 
“Certifications like DGNB or LEED are important as they set global standards and drive change 
in the construction industry” [Certifications driving regenerative practices - Interview 15]. Lastly, 
transparency efforts were emphasized: “A readable regeneration report clearly outlines our 
actions and metrics” [Enhancing transparency and customer education and use labels - 
Interview 4]. 
 
Second Order Themes 
 
The analysis of strategies for implementing regenerative practices identifies ten Second Order 
Themes. This section presents the Second Order Themes and some of their associated First 
Order Concepts. The corresponding structure tree for further completeness can be found in 
Figure 19. 
Design and Product Innovation 
This Second Order Theme includes concepts such as [Integrating regenerative practices 
across all products - Interview 4], emphasizing [Material innovation and design principles - 
Interview 10], and focusing on the [Development of long-lasting IoT products - Interview 12]. 
Organizations also highlight [Rethinking product design for durability - Interview 7] as a means 
to reduce waste and extend product lifecycles.  
Education and Awareness 
First Order Concepts like [Communication is key to explaining complex regenerative concepts 
- Interview 1], [Fostering awareness and education - Interview 2], and [Transparency builds 
consumer trust - Interview 10] are included in this theme. Efforts such as [Consumer education 
and sales training - Interview 10] and [Direct marketing and consumer education - Interview 8] 
emphasize actions that aim to address knowledge gaps. Internal education initiatives, like an 
[ESG training program for all employees - Interview 14], further highlight the importance of 
integrating regenerative principles within organizations. 
Collaborative Networks 
This theme includes concepts like [Strengthening partnerships for systemic change - Interview 
2], [Collaborating for scalability and standards - Interview 4], and [Promoting collaboration - 
Interview 9] as essential for achieving shared goals. The First Order Concepts also include 
[Fostering partnerships and collaboration - Interview 5] to align operations with community 
needs and use corporate partnerships to create shared standards. 
Regulatory and Policy Integration 
The experts highlighted First Order Concepts such as [Leveraging regulatory frameworks as 
opportunities - Interview 5], [Advocating for stricter regulations - Interview 10], and to 
[Coordinate and align policies - Interview 2]. Biodiversity-focused initiatives, like [Biodiversity 
policies in park design - Interview 11], demonstrate the integration of environmental aspects 
into regulatory compliance strategies.  
Technology-Driven Solutions 
Organizations employ strategies like [Technology-assisted monitoring - Interview 12], 
[Investing in technology - Interview 5], and [Adopt(ing) data-driven approaches for 
transparency - Interview 3]. Additionally, the [Prioritization of new developments with higher 
==PAGE:74==
 
69 
regenerative potential - Interview 14] highlights how organizations focus their innovation efforts 
on technologies that maximize regenerative and environmental benefits. 
Operational and Infrastructure Efficiency 
This theme includes First Order Concepts like [Incremental implementation of regenerative 
methods - Interview 6], [Maximizing the use of existing resources - Interview 7], and 
[Investments in durable infrastructure - Interview 12]. Additionally, initiatives such as [Flexible 
workplaces and paperless office policies - Interview 14] and [Use of electric vehicles and 
ergonomic workplace improvements - Interview 14] demonstrate how operational adaptations 
and infrastructure upgrades contribute to regeneration and employee well-being. 
Cultural Transformation 
First Order Concepts such as [Embedding regeneration in corporate culture - Interview 5], 
[Promoting cultural change - Interview 7], and [Setting an example internally - Interview 9] 
emphasize the importance of leadership and internal alignment.  
Nature-Based Solutions and Ecosystem Restoration 
Experts also mentioned First Order Concepts such as [Implementing biodiversity measures - 
Interview 8] and [Leveraging existing natural processes - Interview 8]. Additionally, [Integrating 
agroforestry systems - Interview 8] was emphasized as a key strategy. 
Scaling and Long-Term Goals 
The interviews revealed First Order Concepts like [Focusing on long-term goals and scaling 
innovation - Interview 4] and [Building confidence through early successes - Interview 6]. 
Additionally, [Scientific milestones and strategic measurement - Interview 4] show how 
important it is to track progress in a structured way. 
Certification and Transparency for Customer Trust 
Key First Order Concepts include [Enhancing transparency and customer education and 
us(ing) labels - Interview 4], [Establishing credibility through certifications - Interview 10], and 
[Certifications make it easier for customers to trust our products - Interview 1]. Additionally, 
[Enhancing transparency through certifications - Interview 6] and a [Focus on quality over ESG 
transparency - Interview 12] further underline reliable labeling and transparency. 
 
Aggregation 
 
The ten Second Order Themes were subsequently grouped into six Aggregate Dimensions to 
provide an understanding of the various strategies promoting regenerative practices. An 
overview of these dimensions can also be found in Figure 19. 
Innovative and Regenerative Product Development 
This dimension merges the Second Order Themes of Design and Product Innovation & 
Technology-Driven Solutions, emphasizing the integration of regeneration into product design 
and technological advancements. Examples such as [Embedding sustainability and 
regeneration goals early in the design phase - Interview 1] and [Prioritization of new 
developments with higher regenerative potential - Interview 14] show how these forward-
==PAGE:75==
 
70 
thinking approaches align design principles and technological innovations to advance 
regenerative practices effectively.  
Education, Awareness, and Trust Building through Certifications 
Education and Awareness & Certification and Transparency for Customer Trust form this 
dimension. It underlines how knowledge sharing and enhancing credibility can drive 
regenerative practices. These themes complement each other well since education initiatives 
promote understanding, and certifications offer transparency and reliability to build trust 
between stakeholders. Concepts such as [Fostering awareness and education - Interview 2] 
and [Certifications make it easier for customers to trust our products - Interview 1] exemplify 
how these strategies can work together.  
Collaborative and Policy-Driven Strategies 
This Dimension combines Collaborative Networks & and Regulatory and Policy Integration. 
Together, these themes point out how collective action and policy alignment can be important 
for overcoming systemic barriers and achieving large-scale impact. The meaning of 
coordinated efforts between stakeholders and regulatory frameworks is highlighted in concepts 
like [Leverag(ing) public procurement to influence the value chain - Interview 2] and 
[Strengthening partnerships for systemic change - Interview 2]. 
Operational Efficiency and Long-Term Planning 
Operational and Infrastructure Efficiency & Scaling and Long-Term Goals are merged into this 
dimension and underline strategies that address immediate efficiency improvements and 
future-oriented growth. This is shown in concepts like [Incremental implementation of 
regenerative methods - Interview 6] and [Scaling pilot projects into viable models - Interview 
4]. 
Nature-Based Strategies 
This Dimension contains Nature-Based Solutions and Ecosystem Restoration & focuses on 
environmental approaches to regeneration. Strategies like [Implementing biodiversity 
measures - Interview 8] and [Leveraging existing natural processes - Interview 8] specify the 
role of nature-based solutions in improving environmental resilience. Other approaches, such 
as [Adapting practices to local conditions - Interview 6], further demonstrate the benefits of 
combining regenerative practices with ecological principles. 
Cultural Transformation
