Cultural Transformation becomes a dimension in itself. It includes how changing organizational 
mindsets and values to integrate regenerative principles is essential. This can be seen in 
concepts like [Embedding regeneration in corporate culture - Interview 5] and [Promoting 
cultural change - Interview 7]. 
4.2.4 Industrie-Specific and Regional Differences 
This section presents the differences in barriers and drivers for the adoption of regenerative 
practices across different industries and regions, as identified through the expert interviews.  
 
==PAGE:76==
 
71 
The findings are sector-specific insights drawn from industries like product design, agriculture, 
interior design, and the chemical industry and provided by the experts. It is important to note 
that these findings are limited to the industries and regions named in the interviews and reflect 
the specific challenges and drivers within those contexts. Later sections deal with 
interpretations and recommendations. 
 
Sector-specific Challenges 
 
Each industry encounters unique differences when implementing regenerative practices. In 
traditional sectors, organizational culture often hinders progress: “In traditional industries, the 
corporate culture itself becomes a barrier to regenerative change” (Interview 5). Additionally, 
the extent to which advanced technologies are available differs across sectors which affects 
the implementation speed. As noted by Sustainability Consultant 1, “Industries with access to 
cutting-edge technologies can implement regenerative practices faster” (Interview 5). 
 
In the Product Design Industry, challenges arise from the contract-based business model and 
certification requirements. As Sustainability Manager 1 explained, “We are primarily in contract 
business, where decision-makers like facility managers are not end-users. This makes 
regeneration a harder sell” (Interview 1). Additionally, “Certifications such as FSC® (Forest 
Stewardship Council) and ISO 14001 are often prerequisites for large-scale projects” 
(Interview 1). These certifications support regenerative practices by ensuring responsible 
forest management (FSC®) and promoting environmental management systems (ISO 14001). 
New materials also face strict standards, including fire resistance testing: “New materials must 
undergo specific tests, like fire resistance or strength tests, which are often challenging” 
(Interview 1). Frameworks like the Eco Design Directive and digital product passports aim to 
promote ecological practices, but their implementation is slow: “Regulations like the Eco 
Design Directive and digital product passports help, but implementation is slow” (Interview 10). 
 
The Interior Design Industry faces a barrier in adopting regenerative practices as it has limited 
influence on indirect emissions. As Sustainability Manager 3 noted, “97% of our greenhouse 
gas emissions come from Scope 3, where we have the least influence” (Interview 4). Scope 3 
emissions include indirect emissions from the entire value chain, which often fall outside the 
direct operational control of companies (Hertwich & Wood, 2018).  
 
The Chemical Pest Control Industry also faces barriers due to its dependence on highly specific 
biotechnological innovations, which require precise targeting of particular organisms: “High 
specificity required for biotechnological solutions targeting particular organisms” (Interview 14). 
Moreover, advancements in technology, such as the integration of sensors, AI, and digital 
tools, are essential for improving forecasting methods and addressing pest occurrence 
effectively: “Need for better forecasting methods for pest emergence, relying on advancements 
in sensors, AI, and digitalization” (Interview 14). Despite this complexity, the industry enjoys a 
positive impact from regenerative initiatives, as the Pest Control Solutions Manager noted: 
“The industry and customers have an extremely positive perception of regenerative practices.” 
(Interview 12). 
 
The Agricultural Sector encounters barriers to adopting regenerative practices, which vary 
significantly based on farm size. Smaller farms often face resource constraints that limit their 
==PAGE:77==
 
72 
ability to invest in advanced technologies: “Small farms lack the resources to invest in 
expensive technologies, unlike larger operations” (Interview 6). Cultural resistance also plays 
a significant role in this industry, as highlighted by the Organic Farmer: “Farmers often resist 
change because they are stuck in old habits and practices” (Interview 8). Furthermore, the lack 
of financial support for farmers is also a considerable barrier: “There are no policies that 
financially reward ecological services provided by regenerative farming” (Interview 8). Market 
barriers intensify these challenges, especially for regenerative agricultural products, which 
often cannot achieve higher prices: “There is currently no price premium in the market for 
products produced using regenerative methods” (Interview 6). 
 
Regional Differences Discussion 
EU Level 
At the EU level, regulatory consistency across member states creates a framework that levels 
the playing field for businesses by creating common standards: “EU-level regulations ensure 
consistency across member countries, leveling the playing field for businesses” (Interview 9). 
Additionally, recent EU regulations have been important in driving business efforts towards 
sustainability and regeneration: “New EU regulations force companies to address sustainability 
and regeneration” (Interview 5). Similarly, policies like the Circular Economy Act underline the 
importance of specific legislative measures in promoting regenerative approaches: “Laws like 
the Circular Economy Act promote circular approaches and increase manufacturer 
responsibility, adding complexity” (Interview 4). 
National/Regional Level 
Despite the EU framework, national and regional inconsistencies in regulations create 
challenges for companies attempting to implement regenerative practices: “Regional 
inconsistencies in regulation create challenges for companies trying to implement regenerative 
practices” (Interview 10). Examples such as France's requirements for recycled materials in 
construction show the progressive but localized nature of regulatory innovation: “France’s 
requirements for recycled materials in buildings serve as a model for other nations” (Interview 
4).  
Municipal Level 
At the municipal level, public procurement laws often restrict collaboration with local 
stakeholders. This limits the ability to develop customized solutions: “EU procurement laws 
restrict the ability to collaborate directly with local stakeholders on larger projects” (Interview 
2). Additionally, contradictions between municipal policies pose significant barriers to the 
implementation of long-term regenerative strategies: “Contradictions between municipal 
policies create challenges in implementing long-term regenerative projects” (Interview 2). 
Global Supply Chain Challenges 
Coordinating global supply chains introduces complexities since standards and compliance 
requirements vary across countries. Sustainability Manager 2 explained: “Standards differ 
across countries, making coordination more challenging” (Interview 3). Furthermore, there is 
a significant effort required to ensure compliance across diverse global partners: “Ensuring 
compliance across diverse global partners is a significant effort” (Interview 3). 
==PAGE:78==
 
73 
Environmental and Climatic Factors 
Moreover, environmental conditions influence the feasibility and effectiveness of regenerative 
practices, particularly in agriculture. For example, dry regions benefit from measures designed 
to improve water retaining: “Dry regions benefit from regenerative measures that improve 
water retention” (Interview 6). High rainfall areas face challenges such as excessive weed 
growth in cover crops: “High rainfall areas may face challenges like excessive weed growth in 
cover crops” (Interview 6). Climatic changes further complicate standardization efforts, as 
noted: “The impact of climatic change makes it difficult to standardize regenerative practices 
across regions” (Interview 6). 
Access to Land and Resources 
Access to land and resources varies significantly by region and poses another barrier, 
particularly for smaller producers that want to adopt new business models. The Municipal 
Sustainability Project Leader noted: “Access to land is currently a major issue, particularly for 
smaller producers looking to adopt new business models” (Interview 2). This challenge is 
intensified by local opposition and limited availability: “Land accessibility is a significant 
challenge, especially with local opposition and limited availability” (Interview 11). Both experts 
emphasized these issues in the context of Denmark. 
Local Educational Initiatives 
Local educational initiatives to promote the acceptance and adoption of regenerative practices. 
For example, in agriculture, targeted programs have proven to be effective: “Local programs 
like Bodenpraktikum educate farmers on regenerative principles, fostering acceptance” 
(Interview 6). This example highlights efforts in Germany to encounter knowledge gaps and 
support farmers in transitioning to regenerative methods. 
 
  
 
==PAGE:79==
 
74 
5 Discussion 
 
This chapter provides a detailed analysis and interpretation of the survey and expert interview 
results presented earlier. The aim is to deepen the understanding of the data by discussing it 
in the theoretical and practical contexts that support regenerative practices. This discussion 
will clarify why the results are as they are and explore their implications for the wider field. In 
contrast to the previous chapter, which was dedicated to a neutral and descriptive presentation 
of the findings, here, the focus lies on the analysis and interpretation of these findings.  
 
The structure of this chapter is designed to provide clarity and allow for a smooth read adapted 
to the findings and is divided into several sections. The first part deals with the analysis of the 
survey, beginning with an interpretation of the descriptive statistics outlining the demographic 
characteristics and general attitudinal trends of the participants. This is followed by an 
evaluation of the correlation analysis, which examines the relationships between key variables 
to identify significant patterns, followed by an interpretation of the regression analysis, which 
examines the factors that explain consumer behavior. Finally, the qualitative findings from the 
answers to open-ended questions from the survey will be used to improve the quantitative 
results. 
 
In the second part, the focus shifts to the expert interviews. This section begins with a 
discussion of the barriers that hinder the adoption of regenerative practices, followed by an 
examination of the factors that drive these practices. This section includes triangulation, which 
aims to combine the results from the expert interviews with the observations from the survey 
and the literature review. This part also outlines the strategies suggested by the experts to 
overcome these challenges and capitalize on potential opportunities. In addition, this part 
provides an industry-specific and regional analysis that provides insights specific to certain 
sectors. 
5.1 Survey 
5.1.1 Discussion of the Descriptive Statistics 
Awareness and Understanding of Regenerative Practices 
 
The survey data highlights a clear split in awareness and understanding of regenerative 
practices among respondents. While 44.84% of respondents indicated that they were already 
aware of regenerative practices, their understanding of how these differ from sustainable 
practices was moderately low, with an average Likert score of 3.01. This score indicates a 
moderate level of understanding, despite initial awareness. This discrepancy between 
awareness and deep understanding is critical because it underscores the need for targeted 
educational initiatives that not only introduce the concept of regenerative practices, including 
their benefits but also deepen the public's understanding of their unique and beneficial aspects 
relative to sustainability. This is particularly important because the results of the correlation 
and regression analysis below have already shown that a deeper understanding of 
regenerative principles is closely linked to the perceived importance and potential adoption of 
regenerative business models. Meeting this educational need is therefore a key requirement 
==PAGE:80==
 
75 
for fostering a better-informed consumer base that can actively participate in and support 
regenerative initiatives. 
 
Commitment to Regenerative Products in Purchasing Decisions 
 
The results of the survey on the importance of regenerative business models in purchasing 
decisions indicate that while consumers recognize the value of these practices, they are 
committed to varying degrees to integrating them into their daily purchasing decisions. For 
example, the mean importance score of 2.99 on a Likert scale indicates only a moderate, but 
partially present and significant recognition of the importance of regenerative practices in 
consumer decisions. This moderate importance in combination with the different answers 
could underline the critical gap between awareness and actionable consumer behavior. 
 
The median rating of importance in purchasing decisions depicts a discrepancy that may be 
due to several factors, including a lack of clear understanding, as discussed previously, or 
limited availability of regenerative options. This can be seen in the distribution of responses, 
where only 11.47% of respondents placed the highest importance on regenerative models in 
their purchasing decisions. 
 
However, when asked whether they pay attention to environmentally friendly and socially 
responsible production, the majority stated that they sometimes or often take these aspects 
into account, which suggests a general trend toward conscious consumer behavior. However, 
actual purchasing from companies that use regenerative processes shows that while there is 
some commitment (the most common response was “yes, sometimes”), regeneration is not 
yet a predominant and determining factor in the purchasing decisions of many consumers. 
However, the previous answers have already shown that a large proportion of respondents did 
not even know what regenerative products are. This fact could also explain a large part of 
consumer behavior. 
 
This pattern suggests an opportunity to increase consumer awareness of regenerative 
business models through increased education about the benefits and impacts of these 
practices, as general attitudes towards environmentally friendly products are already high. As 
understanding deepens and more regenerative options become available, the significance 
attributed to regenerative practices in purchasing decisions will likely increase. 
 
Consumer Demand for Regenerative Products at Competitive and Premium Pricing 
 
The survey results on consumer preferences for regenerative and conventional products at 
the same price show a strong leaning toward regenerative options. A clear majority of 
respondents (66.53%) even expressed a strong preference for regenerative products, 
indicating that when price is not a barrier, consumers overwhelmingly make a regenerative 
choice. This preference is also reflected by the high average preference score of 4.45 out of 
5, indicating a strong desire for products with greater environmental and social benefits at price 
parity. 
 
Despite this strong preference, the issue of price competitiveness should not be ignored. 
Although a clear majority would choose regenerative products if the prices were equivalent, 
==PAGE:81==
 
76 
the willingness to pay a premium varies. The largest group (41.05%) of respondents indicated 
that they would be willing to pay up to 10% more for regenerative products, and other parts of 
the sample were willing to pay even higher premiums but at a lower percentage. This shows 
that a significant proportion of the market is willing to invest more in products that they believe 
have a higher overall value. This suggests that consumers recognize the additional benefits 
that regenerative processes bring to products and are willing to support them. Additionally, 
11.79% of respondents stated that they would not pay a premium.  
 
This shows that while price competitiveness is crucial, there is a strong market segment that 
values the additional environmental and social benefits so much that they are willing to pay 
more. This finding is crucial for regenerative companies, especially in the B2C sector, where 
maintaining price competitiveness is often key to avoiding crowding out. The findings, 
therefore, suggest that while the preference for regenerative products is clear, companies 
should balance this with strategic pricing to ensure they reach the price-sensitive customer 
base. 
 
Role of Labels and Certifications 
 
Labels and certifications play a crucial role in consumer decision-making, as the survey results 
indicate a significant influence of labels on consumers' purchasing decisions regarding 
regenerative products. The mean score of 3.85 for the importance of a certified label shows 
that a significant number of consumers place importance on certification when it comes to 
regenerative products. This preference is underlined by the fact that 78 respondents (34.36%) 
even rate the importance of such labels as very high. In addition, the specific influence of 
labeling on the purchase decision was rated with a slightly higher mean value of 3.90, which 
indicates a strong consumer preference for labeled and certified products.  
 
Preferences for the type of labeling also reveal significant trends: Labels from independent 
environmental organizations are the most trusted, followed by internationally recognized labels 
and government labels. The low score for corporate labels underlines the importance that 
consumers attach to the independence and consistency of the certification process. This 
skepticism towards corporate labels highlights the clear demand for objective verification that 
guarantees consumers the legitimacy and reliability of environmental claims. This preference 
hierarchy also highlights not only the importance of credibility and perceived independence in 
certification but also the crucial role that labels play in improving transparency and consumer 
education. Furthermore, effective labeling, especially by recognized and independent bodies, 
can significantly close the existing knowledge gap by clearly communicating the different 
benefits and processes associated with regenerative practices. In this way, labeling can 
transform the abstract concept of regeneration into tangible and understandable information, 
facilitating more informed purchasing decisions. This is particularly important as the findings 
suggest that there is a significant lack of understanding among consumers about what 
regenerative practices entail and what benefits they offer. Certifications could support the wider 
acceptance of regenerative products as consumers become more aware of and comfortable 
with the environmental and social benefits these practices bring.  
 
Additionally, participants demonstrated a notable interest in furthering their understanding of 
regenerative practices and their benefits. With a mean score of 3.38, a substantial portion of 
==PAGE:82==
 
77 
the respondents indicated a moderate to high interest in educational opportunities. Specifically, 
only a small minority, 22.03% of respondents, expressed little to no interest in learning more. 
This suggests that educational initiatives, for example, those that labeling strategies could 
support, would likely be well-received and effective in increasing consumer knowledge and 
engagement with regenerative practices. 
 
Preferred Sources of Information 
 
When looking at how consumers prefer to find out about regenerative products, it was found 
that they rely heavily on a variety of authoritative and credible sources, demonstrating a wide 
range of trustworthy information channels. “Scientific studies” was cited as the most trusted 
source by 38.33% of respondents, highlighting a strong preference for fact-based, research-
based information. This reflects a consumer group that values rigorous and verifiable data to 
understand the impacts and benefits of regenerative practices. This was closely followed by 
“certifications and reports” at 37.44%, “news portals” at 36.56%, and “social media” at 32.6%, 
suggesting that both traditional and digital media play an important role in disseminating 
information about regenerative practices. The almost even split between these categories, with 
the possibility of multiple responses, suggests that consumers do not rely on a single source, 
but rather use a mixture of information to form an opinion and make informed decisions. This 
highlights the complexity of educating consumers about regenerative practices and the need 
for a multi-channel approach to communication strategies. However, the lower trust in direct 
company information, preferred by only 15.42% of respondents, underscores the skepticism 
towards direct and potentially promotional content and greater trust in sources that are 
perceived as more objective and less biased.  
 
The diversity of preferred information sources, combined with participants' moderate to high 
interest in learning more about regenerative practices, shows that there is a clear opportunity 
for regenerative companies to use multiple platforms to educate and engage consumers. It 
also highlights the importance of maintaining credibility and transparency across all 
communication channels to build and maintain consumer trust. 
 
Priorities in Consumer Trust 
 
The survey results on what consumers expect from regenerative companies to gain their trust 
shed light on the prioritization of different transparency and accountability measures. The most 
frequently cited expectation, rated as important by 70.85% of respondents, was “certification 
and independent verification.” This high percentage underscores the prevalent consumer 
demand for external validation of a company's environmental claims and reflects the critical 
need for trustworthiness and credibility in the regenerative sector. This is consistent with 
previous findings in this thesis, which highlight the importance of credible labels and 
certifications in consumers’ purchasing decisions and emphasize the critical role of third-party 
validation in building trust. 
 
“Detailed insight into production processes”, which was important for 69.51% of participants, 
is another trust-building factor. This rate, which is almost as high as for certification, illustrates 
the parallel demand for transparency, as a significant proportion of consumers want to know 
==PAGE:83==
 
78 
exactly how products are manufactured. This transparency gives them certainty about the 
authenticity of the regenerative benefits claimed by companies. 
 
In comparison, “long-term environmental goals” were considered important by 56.05% of 
respondents. While this is still a majority, it is significantly less than the immediate importance 
of transparency and certification. This suggests that while consumers are interested in a 
company's plans in the area of regeneration, their immediate trust is more dependent on 
current, verifiable measures than future intentions. 
 
The expectation “commitment to community projects” was selected by 39.46% of respondents, 
suggesting that community impact, while important, is less of a priority than, for example, long-
term environmental goals. This lower percentage suggests a hierarchy of consumer values 
where environmental integrity and transparency appear to be more important than social 
impact. 
 
Finally, “incorporating customer feedback” was only important to 26.91% of participants, 
making it the least important factor in gaining trust. This suggests that while engagement and 
responsiveness are valued, they are not as critical as the other factors for building consumer 
trust in a regenerative context. 
 
Consumer Priorities: Environmental Benefits of Regenerative Practices 
 
In exploring the perceived benefits of regenerative practices, survey participants prioritized 
environmental goals over social ones, reflecting a significant emphasis on ecological 
regeneration. The most valued benefit, as identified by 70% of respondents, was the “active 
restoration and protection of ecosystems,” highlighting a strong consumer preference for 
products that contribute directly to environmental health. This was closely followed by 
“products with a positive net impact” and “circular economy” practices, which were important 
for approximately 59.57% and 58.26% of respondents, respectively, indicating a robust desire 
for products that offer regenerative and sustainable benefits beyond the standard lifecycle.  
 
Furthermore, “long-term soil and water quality improvement” and “enhancing biodiversity” were 
each noted by over 50% of participants, underscoring the importance placed on long-term 
environmental stewardship and regenerative goals. Less prioritized, though still significant, 
was “empowering local communities,” which less than half of the respondents found important. 
This suggests that while social benefits are valued, they are secondary to environmental 
impacts in the minds of consumers. The relatively lower priority given to “empowering local 
communities” may reflect a broader trend where environmental benefits, often perceived as 
more directly connected to urgent global challenges like climate change, tend to overshadow 
the social aspects of regenerative business practices. This perception isn't necessarily about 
witnessing immediate outcomes but rather understanding and acknowledging the potential for 
significant impact. Environmental changes, such as reducing plastic use or carbon emissions, 
are frequently discussed in media and educational contexts, making them more top-of-mind 
for consumers. In contrast, the benefits of empowering local communities, though crucial, 
might seem more abstract and less directly linked to the individual consumer’s daily 
experiences and choices. 
 
==PAGE:84==
 
79 
The survey revealed that respondents viewed regenerative practices as most crucial in the 
“food and beverages” category, with a significant 80.87% indicating its importance. This high 
priority likely reflects direct concerns about health and the environment, areas where 
consumers feel immediate impacts from their choices. Following closely was “clothing and 
textiles,” selected by 74.78% (𝑛=172), where the high priority reflects growing consumer 
awareness of the industry's environmental damage and social issues such as child labor and 
poor working conditions. These sectors, food, and textiles are particularly examined for their 
environmental and ethical impacts, which has led to increased consumer knowledge of 
problems like animal welfare abuses, pollution, and exploitative labor practices. This 
awareness drives demand for change, making consumers more inclined to support or switch 
to brands that adhere to regenerative and ethical practices. 
5.1.2 Discussion of the Correlation 
The analysis of the relationship between annual net income and the preference for 
regenerative products at equivalent pricing revealed a very weak correlation (𝑟 = 0.03, 𝑝 = 
0.63), indicating no statistically significant association. Similarly, the Spearman correlation 
coefficient of 0.008 between income and willingness to pay more for regenerative products, 
with a 𝑝-value of 0.24, further underscores the lack of a meaningful relationship. These findings 
suggest that income levels neither strongly influence participants' preference for regenerative 
products nor their readiness to accept price premiums for them. This challenges common 
assumptions that higher-income individuals are more likely to favor regenerative options or 
provide greater financial support for them. Instead, these results highlight the complexity of 
factors influencing consumer decisions in the context of regenerative practices, suggesting 
that values, awareness, or perceived benefits may play a more important role than economic 
status. 
 
The significant correlation between participants’ understanding of the differences between 
sustainable and regenerative practices and the importance they place on regenerative 
business models (𝑟 = 0.20, 𝑝 = 0.012) underscores the critical role of education in influencing 
consumer preferences. A deeper comprehension of regenerative principles not only enhances 
the appreciation of their benefits but also motivates consumers to prioritize these models in 
their purchasing decisions. This finding implies that educating the public about the specific 
advantages of regenerative over merely sustainable practices could effectively shift consumer 
behavior towards more environmentally and socially responsible choices. 
 
The analysis of the relationship between participants' age and their interest in learning more 
about regenerative practices (𝑟 = 0.07, 𝑝 = 0.28) revealed no meaningful correlation. This lack 
of association suggests that interest in understanding regenerative practices is not significantly 
influenced by age demographics. The results show that the desire for knowledge in this area 
goes beyond generational boundaries, indicating a broad potential audience for educational 
initiatives targeting increased awareness and understanding of regenerative practices. 
 
The analysis examined key relationships influencing consumer behavior toward regenerative 
products. The correlation between attention to environmental and social responsibility and the 
frequency of purchasing regenerative products was moderate and statistically significant (𝑟 = 
0.42, 𝑝 < 0.001). This finding suggests that individuals who consistently prioritize sustainability 
and ethical considerations in their purchasing decisions are more likely to support regenerative 
==PAGE:85==
 
80 
businesses. It highlights the role of ingrained environmentally conscious habits as a driving 
factor for engagement with regenerative offerings. 
 
A significant positive correlation was identified between the perceived importance of 
regenerative practices in purchasing decisions and the likelihood of selecting regenerative 
options over conventional alternatives (𝑟 = 0.41, 𝑝 < 0.001). This suggests that when 
consumers attribute higher value to regenerative practices, they are more inclined to align their 
purchasing behavior with those values. The result emphasizes the necessity of fostering 
consumer understanding and appreciation of regenerative benefits to influence decision-
making effectively.  
 
The relationship between consumers' stated preference for regenerative products at equal 
pricing and their likelihood of choosing such products demonstrated a strong correlation (𝑟 = 
0.60, 𝑝 < 0.001). This indicates that when price parity is achieved, consumer preferences 
strongly align with purchasing actions. The finding underscores the critical importance of 
pricing strategies in converting consumer intention into tangible support for regenerative 
businesses. 
 
The analysis of labeling’s role in consumer decision-making reveals several significant 
correlations, underlining its critical importance in influencing purchasing behavior and 
consumer education about regenerative practices. The strong positive correlations observed 
between the importance of certified labels for regenerative products and their influence on 
purchasing decisions (𝑟 = 0.71, 𝑝 < 0.001), as well as between the likelihood of choosing 
regenerative products over conventional ones and the influence of certified labels (𝑟 = 0.58, 𝑝 
< 0.001), underscore the critical role of credible certifications in shaping consumer behavior. 
These findings highlight that labels serve not only as markers of quality and authenticity but 
also as trust-building tools that enhance consumer confidence. By substantiating the 
environmental claims of companies, certifications can help to overcome the gap between 
consumer intention and action, driving greater commitment to regenerative products and 
fostering their adoption in the marketplace.  
 
The correlation between participants' interest in learning more about regenerative practices 
and the influence of labels on purchasing decisions (𝑟 = 0.62, 𝑝 < 0.001) highlights the 
interconnection between consumer education and labeling. This relationship suggests that 
labels do not merely influence immediate purchasing decisions but also act as an educational 
tool, increasing consumer awareness and understanding of regenerative benefits. This is 
further supported by the significant link between the interest in learning more about 
regenerative practices and the perceived importance of promoting regenerative business 
models (𝑟 = 0.42, 𝑝 < 0.001), which underscores the broader role of education in fostering 
consumer support for these practices. 
 
These findings highlight that effective labeling strategies play a dual role in promoting 
consumer trust and education. By providing credible and transparent information, labels bridge 
knowledge gaps and support consumers in making informed choices, thereby driving broader 
acceptance and adoption of regenerative products. This underscores the need for businesses 
to invest in robust certification processes that align with consumer expectations for credibility 
and education. 
 
==PAGE:86==
 
81 
5.1.3 Discussion of the Regression 
The regression model achieved an 𝑅!-value of 0.429, indicating that approximately 42.9% of 
the variance in participants’ likelihood of choosing regenerative products over conventional 
ones can be explained by the predictors included in the model. This value demonstrates a 
moderate-to-strong explanatory power, particularly in the context of social science research, 
where consumer behavior is influenced by numerous complex and often unmeasured factors. 
The result suggests that the selected independent variables, such as gender, education, 
income, labeling, interest in learning about regenerative practices, and employment status, 
collectively provide meaningful insights into the drivers behind consumer preferences for 
regenerative products. However, the remaining 57.1% of the variance remains unexplained, 
which could be attributed to external factors not included in the model, such as other personal 
values and cultural influences. This underscores the multifaceted nature of consumer decision-
making and highlights opportunities for future research to identify additional determinants of 
behavior. 
 
The regression analysis revealed a significant positive relationship between gender and the 
likelihood of choosing regenerative products, with women being more inclined to prefer these 
options compared to men (𝑏 = 0.15, 𝑝 = 0.04). This finding aligns with broader literature 
suggesting that women often show higher environmental concerns and are more likely to 
engage in such consumer behaviors (Hunter et al., 2004). This tendency could be driven by 
factors such as heightened awareness of social and ecological issues or greater empathy 
towards the long-term impacts of consumption on future generations. 
 
The analysis of educational levels as predictors of choosing regenerative products revealed 
notable trends. Participants with a master’s degree (𝑏 = 0.20, 𝑝 = 0.03) and a doctorate (𝑏 = 
0.25, 𝑝 = 0.02) were significantly more likely to prefer regenerative products, suggesting that 
higher levels of education are positively associated with engagement in environmentally 
conscious purchasing behavior. This aligns with prior research that links advanced education 
to greater environmental awareness and the ability to process complex environmental-related 
information. This relationship is further supported by the significant correlation observed before 
between participants’ understanding of the differences between sustainable and regenerative 
practices and the importance, they place on regenerative business models (𝑟 = 0.20, 𝑝 = 
0.012). These findings underscore the critical role of education not only in promoting a deeper 
understanding of regenerative principles but also in shaping consumer preferences and driving 
demand for products aligned with these practices. Advanced education appears to equip 
individuals with the knowledge and critical thinking necessary to value and prioritize the 
broader benefits of regenerative approaches. 
 
The regression analysis revealed a significant positive effect of annual net income on the 
likelihood of choosing regenerative products (𝑏 = 0.29, 𝑝 = 0.02), indicating that participants 
with higher income levels were more inclined to prefer regenerative options. However, this 
finding contrasts with the results of the correlation analysis, which showed no statistically 
significant relationship between income and either the preference for regenerative products at 
equivalent pricing (𝑟 = 0.03, 𝑝 = 0.63) or the willingness to pay a price premium for these 
products (𝑟 = 0.008, 𝑝 = 0.24). These differing results highlight an important methodological 
distinction. The regression analysis examines the effect of income while controlling for other 
factors, such as labeling influence, interest in learning, and employment status. By isolating 
==PAGE:87==
 
82 
income’s unique contribution, the regression identifies a significant effect, which suggests that 
when accounting for other variables, higher income does play a role in the likelihood of 
choosing regenerative products. In contrast, the correlation analysis measures the simple 
linear relationship between income and the dependent variables without controlling for 
confounding factors. The lack of significant correlations suggests that income alone, without 
considering other influences, does not strongly determine preferences for or willingness to pay 
for regenerative products. This divergence may also reflect the complexity of consumer 
decision-making. While higher-income individuals may generally have greater purchasing 
power and thus a slightly higher likelihood of choosing regenerative products, their willingness 
to pay a premium or their initial preference might be driven by other factors such as values, 
education, awareness, or perceptions of the product's benefits. The findings challenge the 
assumption that higher economic status necessarily equates to greater support for 
sustainability, emphasizing the importance of addressing non-economic barriers to adoption, 
such as education and awareness, to appeal to a broader audience. 
 
The regression analysis identified the influence of labeling on purchasing decisions as the 
strongest predictor of the likelihood of choosing regenerative products (𝑏 = 0.43, 𝑝 < 0.01). 
This result underscores the critical role that labels and certifications play in consumer decision-
making, particularly in the context of regenerative practices. Participants who considered 
labeling to be highly influential were significantly more likely to prefer regenerative products, 
suggesting that labels serve as a crucial mechanism for building trust, validating claims, and 
enhancing the perceived value of these products. The regression findings align with the 
correlation analysis, which similarly highlights the critical role of labeling in consumer decision-
making. The strong correlations between the perceived importance of certified labels, their 
influence on purchasing decisions (𝑟 = 0.71, 𝑝 < 0.001), and the likelihood of choosing 
regenerative products (𝑟 = 0.58, 𝑝 < 0.001) underscore that credible and transparent labeling 
fosters trust, reinforces consumer confidence and directly motivates the adoption of 
regenerative products. These results further emphasize that labels not only validate claims but 
also serve as educational tools, enhancing consumer understanding and commitment to 
regenerative practices. 
 
The regression highlights that interest in learning about regenerative practices significantly 
predicts a preference for regenerative products (𝑏 = 0.31, 𝑝 = 0.01). This highlights the pivotal 
role of education and awareness in driving consumer choices. Participants who demonstrated 
a greater interest in understanding regenerative practices were more likely to favor these 
products, emphasizing the importance of informed decision-making. This finding aligns with 
the correlation analysis, which revealed significant positive relationships between interest in 
learning and both the perceived influence of labeling (𝑟 = 0.62, 𝑝 < 0.001) and the importance 
of regenerative practices in purchasing decisions (𝑟 = 0.42, 𝑝 < 0.001). Together, these results 
underscore that educational initiatives and transparent labeling can bridge knowledge gaps, 
foster consumer confidence, and encourage support for regenerative products. 
 
The analysis of employment status revealed that students were significantly more likely to 
choose regenerative products compared to full-time employees (𝑏 = 0.21, 𝑝 = 0.05). This 
suggests that younger individuals, often more engaged with educational and social 
discussions on sustainability, may show a stronger preference for regenerative options. This 
aligns with research indicating that students often adopt progressive attitudes toward 
environmental issues, potentially due to exposure to academic knowledge and activism 
==PAGE:88==
 
83 
(Roncević & Cvetković, 2017). For other employment categories, including part-time 
employees, self-employed individuals, retirees, homemakers, and unemployed participants, 
no significant differences were observed. These results suggest that employment status, 
except for the student category, may not be a decisive factor in predicting regenerative product 
preferences.  
5.1.4 Discussion of the Open-Ended Survey Responses 
Concerns about Regenerative Products 
 
The concerns raised by participants regarding the cost and affordability of regenerative 
products reveal a significant barrier to their broader adoption. Many respondents perceive 
these products as too expensive and often unaffordable for individuals with average or low 
incomes. This perception suggests that price competitiveness remains a critical challenge for 
regenerative businesses aiming to penetrate mainstream markets. Several participants 
highlighted the risk of regenerative products being priced out of reach for the average 
consumer, exacerbating socio-economic divides. Others linked the higher retail prices to 
increased production costs, emphasizing that these economic realities could deter consumers, 
particularly in price-sensitive markets. Participants further noted that the availability of cheaper 
conventional alternatives could pose an additional barrier to the market entry of regenerative 
products. To address this issue, it becomes essential to close the affordability gap by making 
regenerative products more competitively priced through reducing production costs. Another 
possibility is that their long-term value and social benefits need to be better communicated. 
 
The concerns around the availability and scalability of regenerative products highlight another 
significant barrier to their widespread adoption. Participants pointed out that the production 
processes for regenerative products often rely on specific raw materials or methods, which 
may be difficult to source in sufficient quantity or quality. This limited availability not only 
hinders production but also affects the visibility of these products in local markets, making 
them harder for consumers to access. These challenges reflect the need for more robust 
supply chains and increased scalability of regenerative production methods. Without sufficient 
availability, even the most well-intentioned consumers may struggle to integrate regenerative 
products into their purchasing behavior. 
 
The concerns regarding credibility and greenwashing reflect a critical barrier to consumer trust 
in regenerative products. Many participants highlighted skepticism about the authenticity of 
claims made by companies, expressing doubts about the transparency of regenerative 
practices. These perceptions are rooted in broader issues of greenwashing, where companies 
exploit narratives to enhance profit margins without delivering tangible environmental or social 
benefits. This lack of trust reflects the challenges regenerative products face in differentiating 
themselves from mere marketing gimmicks. A recurring theme in the responses is the 
importance of credible, independently verified certifications. Participants emphasized that 
without reliable audits and clear standards, regenerative practices risk being dismissed as 
another iteration of greenwashing. The concerns are strongly supported by findings from both 
the regression and correlation analyses, which emphasize the important role of labeling in 
shaping consumer behavior. The critique of misleading labels further underscores the 
complexity of this issue. Participants expressed frustration with labels that claim regeneration 
but offer only marginal regenerative benefits. This not only misleads consumers but also 
==PAGE:89==
 
84 
diminishes the perceived value of truly regenerative products, making it more challenging for 
ethical companies to establish a foothold in the market. These findings emphasize the 
necessity of establishing rigorous, independent certification systems that clearly define what 
constitutes a regenerative product. Beyond certification, companies must prioritize transparent 
communication about their practices, ensuring that consumers understand the verifiable 
benefits their products deliver. Education and transparency are key to closing the gap between 
consumer skepticism and the transformative potential of regenerative practices. 
 
The concerns raised about the efficacy and quality of regenerative products highlight a 
significant barrier to their widespread acceptance. Participants expressed doubts about 
whether these products deliver on their environmental promises or provide long-term benefits 
for health and well-being. This skepticism reflects a broader challenge faced by regenerative 
products: the need to strengthen claims of efficacy with concrete, transparent evidence. 
Without clear proof of their positive impact, consumers may hesitate to invest in such products, 
particularly when faced with higher costs. Quality concerns were another recurring theme. 
Participants worried that regenerative products might compromise functionality, durability, or 
aesthetic appeal compared to conventional alternatives. This highlights the importance of 
ensuring that regenerative products meet the quality expectations of consumers. If products 
do not meet the same standard or are perceived as inferior, they run the risk that even 
environmentally conscious consumers will decide in favor of the conventional product. 
 
Strategies to Increase the Consumer Acceptance of Regenerative Products 
 
Affordability and pricing emerged as critical strategies to increase consumer acceptance of 
regenerative products, with participants emphasizing the need for lower costs and price parity 
with conventional options. These proposals are consistent with previous findings that high 
prices are a major barrier that discourages even environmentally conscious consumers. While 
some participants indicated a willingness to pay a price premium, as highlighted in the survey 
findings, closing the price gap remains crucial to making regenerative products accessible to 
a broader audience and attracting a larger and more diverse group of consumers. 
 
Transparency and certifications were repeatedly highlighted by participants as essential 
strategies for increasing consumer acceptance of regenerative products. While this topic has 
been extensively discussed earlier, the open-ended responses further underscore its 
importance. Suggestions such as providing clear and authentic information about production 
processes, benefits, and risks, as well as using tools like labels, QR codes, or interactive 
experiences, reinforce the findings from the quantitative survey questions and earlier 
discussions and align with previous insights emphasizing the critical role of transparent 
communication and credible certifications in building trust and addressing consumer concerns. 
 
The responses emphasizing education as a strategy to increase consumer acceptance of 
regenerative products further validate findings previously discussed in the thesis. Participants 
highlighted the importance of communicating the societal and environmental benefits of 
regenerative products, particularly to justify their higher price point. This aligns with earlier 
insights that consumer understanding of regenerative practices is closely tied to their perceived 
importance and willingness to adopt such products. Calls for public awareness campaigns and 
more extensive education reflect the need to bridge the knowledge gap that exists around 
==PAGE:90==
 
85 
regenerative practices. These responses illustrate the importance of education when it comes 
to reducing skepticism and concerns and strengthening trust. While the qualitative responses 
provide valuable details such as emphasizing the role of advertising and practical familiarity, 
they ultimately reinforce the overarching theme that consumer adoption depends not only on 
affordability but also on clear, accessible, and effective communication about the benefits and 
unique attributes of regenerative products. Through increased education efforts, companies 
and policymakers can address both knowledge gaps and misconceptions, creating a more 
informed and supportive consumer base. 
 
The responses highlight the critical role of marketing and communication strategies in 
increasing consumer acceptance of regenerative products. Traditional methods, such as 
advertising and word-of-mouth, are seen as powerful tools to build awareness and trust. 
Transparent advertising, as emphasized by participants, underlines the importance of 
authenticity in marketing messages, particularly in an era where skepticism about 
greenwashing is prevalent. The mention of influencer campaigns reflects the growing reliance 
on social media and trusted figures to engage consumers emotionally. Authentic advocates 
can personalize the message and make regenerative products more relatable, especially for 
younger, digitally connected audiences. Emotional marketing strategies, such as storytelling, 
were also noted as impactful, particularly when they illustrate the tangible benefits of 
regenerative products. These approaches appeal to consumers’ values and can foster a 
deeper emotional connection to the products. 
 
The responses furthermore show the dual importance of quality and accessibility in driving 
consumer acceptance of regenerative products. Participants consistently highlighted that 
these products must maintain high quality, with some emphasizing that consumers expect 
regenerative products to deliver “good value for money and better quality.” This suggests that 
perceived compromises in quality could undermine the appeal of regenerative offerings, even 
among environmentally conscious consumers, as highlighted before in the thesis. Accessibility 
was also a recurring theme, with participants calling for easier availability both online and 
offline. This reflects the need to integrate regenerative products into mainstream markets, 
ensuring that they are as readily available as conventional alternatives. The connection 
between quality and availability is particularly important. If consumers perceive that high-
quality regenerative products are difficult to find or purchase, they may revert to conventional 
options out of convenience. Therefore, ensuring widespread accessibility of premium 
regenerative products is critical to fostering their broader acceptance and adoption.  
 
The feedback regarding regulatory support and incentives highlights the important role that 
government intervention can play in promoting the adoption of regenerative products. 
Participants emphasized the potential impact of financial mechanisms such as subsidies, tax 
breaks, or other pricing incentives to make these products more competitive with conventional 
alternatives. This aligns with earlier findings about the affordability barrier, as participants 
repeatedly stressed the need to close the price gap to drive broader consumer acceptance. 
Subsidies and tax incentives could serve multiple purposes: they not only lower costs for 
consumers but also signal a strong institutional commitment to supporting regenerative 
practices. By reducing entry barriers, such measures could help establish regenerative 
products as a viable option for a larger segment of the population, including price-sensitive 
consumers. Additionally, financial incentives could encourage producers to adopt regenerative 
==PAGE:91==
 
86 
practices by offsetting higher production costs, thereby enhancing scalability and market 
presence. 
5.2 Expert Interviews 
5.2.1 Discussion of the Barriers 
Regulatory Complexity and Policy Barriers 
 
The experts identified regulatory complexity and policy barriers as significant challenges to the 
widespread adoption of regenerative practices. A recurring theme was the fragmentation and 
incoherence of regulatory frameworks, which created systemic obstacles. The lack of 
harmonized legislation across regions, particularly within Europe, forces companies to 
navigate differing and overlapping regulations. This inconsistency not only complicates 
compliance but also discourages companies from adopting regenerative practices due to the 
administrative burden and associated costs. These findings suggest that clearer, streamlined, 
and harmonized regulatory frameworks are crucial to reducing complexity and enabling 
businesses to adopt regenerative principles more effectively. Literature supports this barrier, 
noting that global regulatory disparities exacerbate challenges, placing companies in regions 
with stricter regulations at a competitive disadvantage (Clark, 1995).  
 
High compliance costs, particularly due to strict reporting obligations like those under the 
CSRD (Corporate Sustainability Reporting Directive), were also highlighted as a significant 
barrier. While such regulations are essential for establishing accountability and standards, the 
resource-intensive nature of compliance can be prohibitive for smaller firms. This indicates a 
need for policies that balance accountability with practicality, potentially through support 
mechanisms such as financial incentives or simplified reporting processes tailored to smaller 
enterprises.  
 
Another critical issue is the absence of standardized definitions for key terms like “regenerative 
agriculture.” This lack of clarity enables greenwashing, where companies exploit undefined 
terminology for marketing purposes without substantial action. This not only undermines 
consumer trust but also creates a confusing landscape for businesses genuinely committed to 
regenerative practices. Standardized definitions and protections for key terms are necessary 
to build credibility and ensure accountability within the sector. In the survey, participants 
emphasized the importance of credible certifications and independent verification as 
mechanisms to combat misleading claims. The literature further emphasizes that vague 
definitions within regulatory frameworks can open avenues for unsubstantiated claims, making 
standardized terminology essential for accountability and trust (Manvi et al., 2019; Nemes et 
al., 2022). The conflicting pressures created by NGOs and state regulations further exacerbate 
the challenges. Contradictory demands from these entities can create confusion and hinder 
the implementation of regenerative principles.  
 
This points to the need for greater collaboration and alignment among stakeholders, including 
policymakers, NGOs, and businesses, to ensure that regulatory and advocacy efforts are 
complementary rather than obstructive. These barriers highlight the need for systemic reforms 
that address fragmentation, reduce compliance burdens, and establish clear standards. 
==PAGE:92==
 
87 
Policymakers should focus on creating an enabling environment that fosters alignment, 
minimizes administrative challenges, and promotes trust and accountability through 
standardized definitions and streamlined regulations. 
 
Economic and Financial Constraints 
 
The experts emphasized that economic and financial barriers significantly hinder the adoption 
and implementation of regenerative practices. A central issue is the lack of market 
mechanisms to offset the higher costs associated with regenerative products and 
technologies. The current economic system, which prioritizes affordability over regeneration, 
creates a structural disadvantage for regenerative products. This aligns with survey findings 
where participants frequently cited cost and affordability as major barriers to adopting 
regenerative options. Without mechanisms to distribute these additional costs, regenerative 
practices remain financially inaccessible to many businesses and consumers.  
 
Another major challenge lies in the high upfront costs of regenerative technologies, particularly 
for smaller enterprises or farmers. Innovations such as precision agriculture tools or 
regenerative materials require significant capital investments that many small businesses 
cannot afford. The literature underlines these findings, emphasizing that substantial upfront 
investments, particularly for technologies like precision agriculture tools and regenerative 
materials, pose significant challenges for businesses (Pearson, 2007; Dipu et al., 2022; Lemke 
et al., 2024). This unequal resource distribution limits widespread adoption and creates a 
competitive disadvantage for smaller actors. Furthermore, the economic focus on short-term 
profitability over long-term sustainability perpetuates these challenges. Organizations often 
lack the financial flexibility to prioritize regeneration over immediate financial returns. The 
literature supports these observations, highlighting that short-term profit focus often prevents 
companies from pursuing long-term strategies (Laverty, 1996; Elkington, 2000) This systemic 
issue reflects deeper structural challenges within the global economy, where financial growth 
often outweighs environmental considerations. Without a shift in priorities, the introduction of 
supportive policies, such as subsidies, tax incentives, or grants, and the lowering of production 
costs, the economic pressures that limit regenerative adoption are unlikely to decrease. 
 
Cultural Resistance and Behavioral Challenges 
 
The experts noted that there are significant cultural resistance and behavioral issues that stand 
in the way of adopting regenerative practices. These resistances are deeply rooted in 
organizational and industry norms, where traditional sectors often have a certain inertia when 
it comes to moving away from established business-as-usual approaches. Such resistance, 
as seen in traditional agriculture, for example, is based on long-standing habits and a 
reluctance to adopt new methods, mirroring findings from the literature that ideological barriers 
in agriculture can impede shifts toward lower CO2 consumption, even when financial incentives 
are present (Press et al., 2014). These findings suggest that established mindsets and 
operational routines are a huge barrier to innovation and regenerative transitions.  
 
Leadership also plays a crucial role in enabling or hindering regenerative change. Without 
consistent and proactive leadership support, organizations risk reverting to established 
practices due to inertia. This highlights the need for strong top-down advocacy to drive cultural 
==PAGE:93==
 
88 
change. Leaders not only set the strategic course but also influence organizational culture and 
employee engagement in the adoption of new practices. The literature corroborates this, noting 
that entrenched ways of thinking within companies and learned paradigms often act as barriers 
to innovation, requiring leaders to break these prejudices (Hecker & Toivonen, 2024). 
On a broader societal level, consumer behavior adds another layer of complexity. Behavioral 
challenges include inflated customer expectations and limited societal shifts towards 
regenerative consumption, reflecting a gap between consumer awareness and action. Many 
consumers may not fully understand the value of regenerative practices or prioritize 
convenience and cost over regeneration. This aligns with findings that customer attitudes 
toward circular economy practices, which incorporate elements of regeneration, are 
significantly influenced by reluctance to change and uncertainty about new approaches 
(Morseletto, 2020; Cantù et al., 2021). This is also consistent with the survey results, which 
highlighted that while many participants showed interest in regenerative products, barriers 
such as lack of understanding, perceived high costs, and insufficient awareness hinder broader 
adoption. The findings emphasize that addressing cultural and behavioral resistance requires 
a multifaceted approach. It involves shifting industry norms, promoting leadership commitment, 
and encouraging societal change. 
 
Operational and Supply Chain Challenges 
 
The experts also highlighted operational and supply chain challenges as significant barriers to 
the broader adoption of regenerative practices. These challenges arise from the complexity of 
managing and aligning global supply chains to regenerative targets, particularly in areas where 
companies have limited influence, such as Scope 3 emissions. Dependence on upstream and 
downstream partners in the supply chain complicates efforts to implement regenerative 
change, as these areas are often beyond the direct control of companies. This lack of control 
highlights the need for greater collaboration and shared accountability within supply chain 
networks. These issues align with the literature, which underscores that a lack of trust and 
alignment among supply chain partners can hinder collaboration and effective adoption of 
circular business principles (Assmann et al., 2023; Cantù et al., 2021).  
 
Recycling inefficiencies also emerged as a critical issue, reflecting the limitations of current 
systems in supporting regenerative approaches. Many materials, designed without future 
reuse in mind, pose significant barriers to achieving the goals. These inefficiencies are 
compounded by past design flaws and inadequate processes, which make it challenging to 
close material loops effectively.  
 
The scalability of regenerative methods adds another layer of complexity. While innovative 
materials and methods are increasingly available, their practicality and scalability remain 
limited. This creates a disconnect between the availability of solutions and their feasibility for 
widespread application. These findings emphasize the interconnected nature of operational 
and supply chain challenges. The systemic nature of these barriers highlights the importance 
of investing in scalable solutions, redesigning processes to enhance recycling and circularity, 
and developing industry-wide standards that facilitate collaboration. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:94==
 
89 
Awareness, Education, and Demand Limitations 
 
The barriers surrounding awareness, education, and demand reflect a multi-faceted issue that 
affects both consumer and industry adoption of regenerative practices. The experts' insights 
highlight not only a lack of understanding but also a deeper disconnect between the 
complexities of regenerative principles and the simplicity often expected by consumers. This 
disconnect creates a significant barrier, as consumers may undervalue the broader societal 
and environmental benefits of regenerative products, focusing instead on cost or immediate 
functionality. The findings from the survey reinforce these themes, as the results revealed that 
many participants were either unaware of regenerative practices or lacked a deep 
understanding of their principles. Without informed demand, companies have little incentive to 
invest in regenerative methods, which in turn limits market visibility and consumer education.  
 
Moreover, the pervasive influence of greenwashing further complicates the situation. 
Misleading claims erode trust in regenerative efforts, making it difficult for genuinely 
regenerative products to differentiate themselves in the market. This mistrust, as noted by the 
experts and also in the survey, is often reinforced by the lack of reliable, standardized 
certifications, which could otherwise serve as a bridge to enhance credibility and consumer 
confidence. 
 
From an industry perspective, the educational gap extends beyond consumers. Many 
companies themselves lack a clear understanding of what regenerative practices entail, 
limiting their ability to effectively implement or advocate for these approaches. This highlights 
a systemic issue where education is not just a consumer-level challenge but also a critical 
factor for business leaders, and other stakeholders. This aligns with the literature, which 
identifies a substantial educational gap in disseminating knowledge about regenerative 
practices, particularly in sectors like agriculture (Hynninen, 2024).  
 
Additionally, farmers' deeply rooted reliance on conventional practices and the lack of empirical 
evidence demonstrating the long-term benefits of newer methods like no-till farming further 
underscore the complexity of driving awareness and adoption (Burns, 2021; Khangura et al., 
2023). Ultimately, these barriers show the importance of closing the knowledge gap. By 
fostering a more informed consumer base and equipping businesses with the necessary 
knowledge and tools, the acceptance and demand for regenerative practices may be 
significantly enhanced. 
 
Technological Barriers 
 
The technological barriers identified by some of the experts highlight a complex interplay 
between innovation readiness, action inertia, and the limitations of existing solutions. While 
many technologies needed to support regenerative practices already exist, organizations often 
delay action, expecting further innovations. This “waiting game” not only slows progress but 
also leads to a mindset that underestimates the potential of current tools and methods. Such 
reliance on future advances leads to a paradox where existing opportunities are overlooked, 
hindering the adoption of regenerative approaches. This aligns with the literature review, which 
emphasizes that uncertainties surrounding the long-term efficacy and economic viability of 
==PAGE:95==
 
90 
emerging technologies, such as precision farming and restoration technologies, often deter 
investments (Bhavana & Rao, 2023; Falcone & Imbert, 2019).   
 
Another key challenge lies in the lack of comprehensive solutions, particularly in areas like 
end-of-life product management. The absence of clearly defined strategies for managing the 
lifecycle of regenerative materials, such as reuse or recycling pathways, limits their practicality 
and scalability. Without these critical components, even the most innovative regenerative 
technologies struggle to achieve widespread acceptance or integration into existing systems. 
These barriers point to the need for a dual focus: leveraging and optimizing available 
technologies while simultaneously investing in research to address existing gaps. The findings 
also resonate with the survey results, where participants expressed concerns about the 
scalability and practicality of regenerative solutions.  
 
Stakeholder Alignment Challenges 
 
The complexity of aligning diverse stakeholder interests represents a significant barrier to 
advancing regenerative practices. Stakeholders, including for example farmers, customers, 
landlords, employees, and organizational leadership, often have conflicting priorities. These 
tensions can make it challenging to develop and implement unified strategies. For example, 
farmers may face simultaneous demands for lower costs from consumers, adherence to 
regenerative standards, and compliance with landlord expectations, creating competing 
pressures that are difficult to reconcile. Involving multiple stakeholder groups in a shared 
commitment to regeneration proves challenging, particularly when their goals do not align with 
the organization's core business priorities. For example, regeneration objectives often clash 
with short-term financial targets, resulting in a lack of consistent support for regenerative 
initiatives. The literature further highlights that this misalignment of stakeholder interests, 
particularly in B2B settings, hinders the adoption of regenerative innovations, as many 
customers are hesitant to invest the time and resources needed to integrate new materials 
(Hecker & Toivonen, 2024). Such conflicts can delay progress, weaken the impact of 
regenerative efforts, and encourage resistance from key stakeholders who see these initiatives 
as incompatible with their immediate needs or interests. Addressing these challenges requires 
fostering collaboration and communication among stakeholders to create shared values and 
goals.  
 
Market Limitations 
 
Market restrictions represent significant structural barriers that hinder the wider introduction of 
regenerative processes. A key problem is limited access to land and resources, particularly for 
smaller producers seeking to adopt regenerative business practices. The limited availability of 
land, compounded by local resistance, creates significant barriers for producers seeking to 
expand or even enter the market. This challenge disproportionately impacts smaller 
companies, which often lack the capital or influence to overcome such barriers. These 
constraints reflect broader systemic issues, such as the unequal distribution of resources and 
competing demands for land use. For example, urban expansion, agricultural demand, and 
environmental protection often compete for the same land resources, further limiting 
opportunities for regenerative initiatives. 
==PAGE:96==
 
91 
5.2.2 Discussion of the Drivers 
Regulatory and Policy Drivers 
 
Regulatory and policy frameworks emerged as possible critical drivers of regenerative 
practices, illustrating how external governance can catalyze systemic change. As stated 
before, regulations are sometimes recognized as a barrier, but many experts emphasized their 
transformative potential. Policies like the EU Taxonomy, the Green Deal, and national 
initiatives such as the Circular Economy Act create clear incentives for businesses to adopt 
regenerative approaches, demonstrating that external mandates can establish priorities and 
accountability across industries. By embedding regenerative goals into legislative frameworks, 
these policies foster an environment where regenerative practices become strategic 
imperatives rather than optional endeavors. The literature also confirms that regulatory 
framework plays an important role in creating opportunities for regenerative business practices 
(Haselsteiner et al., 2021).  
 
The experts further stated that for example, pressure from regulators is forcing companies to 
re-evaluate their practices, creating an urgency for systemic innovation. Similarly, subsidies 
and government incentives provide tangible support, helping businesses offset the often higher 
initial costs associated with adopting regenerative practices.  These findings resonate with 
earlier insights from the survey, where some participants highlighted the importance of 
subsidies and regulatory support in making regenerative products more competitive and 
accessible. Policies that both enforce and support regenerative initiatives help level the playing 
field, enabling businesses of all sizes to participate in the shift toward regeneration. However, 
the effective implementation of these drivers depends on consistent policy frameworks. 
Fragmented or contradictory regulations, as discussed in the barriers section, could hinder 
their impact.  
 
Collaboration and Partnerships 
 
Collaboration and partnerships emerged as key drivers for regenerative practices, highlighting 
the need for joint efforts between industries, stakeholders, and communities. Experts 
emphasized that partnerships not only accelerate the adoption of regenerative models but also 
foster innovation through the sharing of resources and co-development of solutions. Cross-
sector collaboration was particularly highlighted as a catalyst for systemic change, as it allows 
organizations to bring together their expertise and establish common standards that promote 
regeneration on a broader scale.  
 
These partnerships also extend to local communities, where engagement and collaboration 
help to align business goals with community needs. By fostering goodwill and facilitating 
project approval, community partnerships play a dual role in operational success and social 
inclusion. Involving local stakeholders ensures that regenerative practices are not only 
environmentally beneficial but also socially equitable, creating a stronger foundation for long-
term success. Partnerships with suppliers, researchers, and even recyclers can enhance these 
efforts by co-creating solutions that are not only innovative but also trusted by end users. 
Although partnerships offer numerous benefits, their success depends on effective 
communication and coordination between the parties involved. Differing priorities and 
operational objectives can hinder collaboration, as noted in the discussion on the barriers to 
==PAGE:97==
 
92 
alignment between stakeholders. To maximize the impact of partnerships, it is critical to 
establish common goals, foster trust between stakeholders, and ensure open channels of 
communication.  
 
Technological Innovation and Advancements 
 
Technological innovation is a key driver for regenerative practices as it enables companies to 
implement regenerative approaches more effectively and on a larger scale. New technologies 
provide tools and systems that simplify the adoption of regenerative practices and make them 
accessible to a wider range of industries. The literature confirms that technological 
advancements are a key driver for regenerative business practices, with emerging 
technologies accelerating progress toward regenerative and restorative processes that 
actively contribute to the replenishment of natural resources (Lacy et al., 2019). One expert 
explained that precision agriculture, for example, uses GPS navigation and advanced tools to 
optimize regenerative farming methods, demonstrating the practical impact of technology in 
reducing barriers to adoption.  
 
Pilot projects and prototyping play an important role in promoting innovation by providing proof 
of concept and encouraging wider adoption. These initiatives allow organizations to test and 
refine new technologies, producing measurable results that can be scaled up to drive larger 
systemic change. Tools such as the Science-Based Targets initiative enhance the impact of 
technological advances by enabling organizations to scientifically measure the results of their 
regeneration efforts, increasing accountability and credibility.  
 
While technological advances offer significant potential, their success depends on scalability 
and accessibility. As highlighted in the discussion of barriers, affordability, and equal access 
must be considered in the development and deployment of new technologies to ensure 
widespread adoption. If these barriers are not addressed, the technology's potential to drive 
regeneration may remain limited to well-resourced organizations, limiting its broad impact. 
Collaborative efforts to scale innovation and integrate it into different market contexts are 
critical to maximize its role as a driver of regenerative practices.  
 
Consumer and Market Demand 
 
Consumer and market demand is proving to be a key driver for regenerative practices, with 
growing social awareness and interest in topics like regeneration changing expectations 
across all industries. Experts emphasized that consumer behavior has changed significantly, 
and people are increasingly looking for transparency, accountability, and environmentally 
friendly products. This increased demand not only puts companies under pressure to innovate 
but also gives them an advantage if they can meet these changing preferences. Corporate 
customers also play a crucial role in promoting regenerative practices. Large organizations are 
setting strict criteria for their supply chains, forcing companies to adopt innovative solutions to 
maintain their business relationships. This aligns with the literature: investors are seeking 
opportunities to align their investments with environmental, social, and governance (ESG) 
criteria (Eccles et al., 2017; Starks et al., 2017). This shows how both individual consumer 
choices and institutional demand can act as catalysts for systemic change. The survey results 
underline the existing demand from consumers for regenerative products. Participants 
==PAGE:98==
 
93 
expressed a preference for products focused on regenerative practices, especially when clear 
labeling and transparency were in place. This is in line with the findings of experts and 
highlights the growing market opportunity for companies that can effectively communicate their 
commitment to regeneration. 
 
Economic and Financial Benefits 
 
Economic and financial benefits are another driver for the adoption of regenerative practices, 
especially as companies recognize the long-term profitability and operational efficiencies 
associated with these approaches. While the initial cost can be high, experts emphasized that 
regenerative practices offer significant benefits over time. These include improved resource 
efficiency, reduced operating costs, and the ability to meet changing consumer expectations 
and regulatory requirements, all of which align with long-term business goals. The literature 
review showed that regenerative agricultural practices, such as no-till or minimal tillage 
methods, have been shown to enhance soil health, increase crop yields, and reduce input 
costs over time, contributing to improved profitability (Khangura et al., 2023; Dipu et al., 2022; 
Lemke et al., 2024; LaCanne & Lundgren, 2018).  
 
One of the most important economic advantages lies in resource optimization. By focusing on 
circular practices, companies can reduce waste and operational costs while supporting 
regenerative goals. For example, the reuse of materials or parts is not only in line with 
regenerative goals but also offers immediate opportunities for cost savings, as noted in certain 
industries such as aviation.  
 
Corporate partnerships and financial support also play a central role. Corporate Stakeholder 
Sustainability Funds provide the financial flexibility needed to develop and implement 
regenerative initiatives. For example, initiatives such as the Regenerative Fund for Nature and
