the One Planet Business for Biodiversity coalition showcase the role of public-private 
partnerships in scaling regenerative efforts and addressing systemic environmental challenges 
(Frank, 2024; Regenerative Fund for Nature, 2023). Similarly, an expert stated that 
mechanisms such as power purchase agreements (PPAs) and other financing instruments 
illustrate how financial incentives can reduce the burden of initial investment and make 
regenerative practices more economical.  
 
As stated previously, regenerative practices create economic value beyond internal operations 
by offering market opportunities. As consumer demand for regenerative products grows, 
businesses adopting these practices can position themselves as industry leaders, enhancing 
their competitive advantage. This market alignment not only attracts environmentally 
conscious consumers but also strengthens relationships with corporate clients who prioritize 
regeneration in their supply chains. Survey findings reinforce the economic potential of 
regeneration. The results revealed a significant portion of consumers willing to pay a price 
premium for regenerative products. This willingness further reflects the economic viability of 
regeneration, suggesting that consumers recognize the added value these products offer, 
despite their higher upfront costs. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:99==
 
94 
Competitive Advantage 
 
Regenerative practices are increasingly recognized as a source of competitive advantage, 
providing businesses with distinct differentiation in a crowded marketplace. Experts highlighted 
that regeneration acts as a unique selling proposition (USP), helping organizations stand out 
from competitors. By integrating regeneration into their core strategies, businesses can appeal 
to environmentally conscious consumers, corporate clients, and other stakeholders who 
prioritize regeneration, thereby reinforcing their market position and profitability. The experts' 
observations align with the literature, which highlights that regenerative practices not only build 
brand equity and customer loyalty by aligning with consumers' values and mission but also 
provide a competitive advantage by demonstrating innovation and a commitment to long-term 
value creation (Earthly, 2024; Rahman et al., 2020).  
 
One other important dimension of this competitive edge is talent attraction and retention, 
particularly among younger generations. Younger employees are drawn to companies that 
demonstrate transparency, responsibility, and a commitment to regeneration. By embracing 
regenerative practices, organizations can align with the values of these employees, positioning 
themselves as forward-thinking and socially responsible employers. This helps not only in 
retaining top talent but also in fostering a purpose-driven corporate culture, which can drive 
innovation and long-term success. Positive market perception further amplifies the benefits of 
regeneration. Businesses that adopt regenerative practices are often viewed favorably by 
customers and industry peers alike, which strengthens brand loyalty and enhances public 
image. As highlighted by experts, regeneration boosts a company's reputation as an innovator 
and leader in regeneration, creating opportunities to expand market share and attract 
partnerships.  
 
Leadership and Cultural Change 
 
Leadership and cultural transformation emerge as drivers for embedding regenerative 
practices within organizations. The experts made clear that leadership commitment is essential 
for aligning regeneration with long-term corporate goals. By integrating regeneration into the 
company’s purpose, leaders can create a clear direction that drives regenerative growth and 
fosters systemic change. This strategic alignment ensures that regeneration becomes more 
than a peripheral initiative, instead forming a core component of organizational values and 
decision-making. Cultural change within organizations is equally critical. Internal alignment, 
facilitated by initiatives like employee training, helps embed environmental, social, and 
governance (ESG) principles deeply into the DNA of the company. Empowering employees to 
understand and contribute to regenerative goals ensures collective accountability and creates 
a workforce that is not only engaged but also purpose-driven. This alignment can accelerate 
the adoption of regenerative practices as employees across all levels contribute to the shared 
mission.  
 
Personal commitment further reinforces these efforts, particularly when leaders and 
stakeholders view regeneration through a long-term lens. For example, individual motivations, 
such as ensuring a livable future for the next generation, add a personal dimension to the drive 
for change. This blend of professional responsibility and personal dedication enhances the 
authenticity and effectiveness of regenerative leadership. However, the path to cultural 
==PAGE:100==
 
95 
transformation is not without challenges. As described earlier, resistance to change, 
particularly in traditional industries or financially driven organizations, can slow down the 
progress. Strong, visionary leadership is therefore crucial in overcoming inertia, breaking down 
silos, and ensuring alignment across all levels of the organization. 
 
Environmental Benefits 
 
The environmental benefits of regenerative practices represent one of the most foundational 
drivers for their adoption. Some Experts, especially the farmers, emphasized the substantial 
ecological advantages, ranging from improved soil fertility and water retention to enhanced 
biodiversity and ecosystem stability. These benefits make regenerative approaches not only a 
solution for environmental degradation but also a proactive driver for building resilience against 
climate change. For example, diverse cropping systems and grass-fed livestock management 
reduce harmful emissions and restore soil health, creating systems that are both productive 
and regenerative. Moreover, the potential for carbon sequestration highlights the 
transformative impact of regenerative methods. Practices such as planting hedgerows and 
managing grasslands can capture significant amounts of CO2 annually, contributing directly to 
climate change mitigation. This capacity to store carbon underscores the dual role of 
regenerative practices as both a means of reducing greenhouse gas emissions and restoring 
degraded ecosystems.  
 
These environmental benefits resonate with one of the original motivations for adopting 
regenerative practices: addressing the urgent need to combat climate change and 
environmental degradation. Unlike other drivers, such as economic or social benefits, the 
ecological impact is the most fundamental rationale for these approaches. Regeneration, at its 
core, is about restoring balance to natural systems and creating a future worth living. The 
survey findings also align with these expert insights, demonstrating strong consumer interest 
in products that contribute to environmental regeneration. Many participants highlighted the 
importance of tangible environmental benefits, such as biodiversity enhancement and carbon 
reduction, in influencing their purchasing decisions.  
5.2.3 Discussion of the Strategies 
Design and Product Innovation 
 
Design and product innovation emerge as critical strategies for advancing regenerative 
practices, reflecting a proactive approach to embedding regenerative principles at the core of 
production processes. Experts emphasized that decisions made during the design phase have 
a profound and long-lasting impact on a product's environmental footprint, with one expert 
highlighting that up to 80% of this impact is determined during this stage. This insight highlights 
the importance of integrating regenerative principles early in the design process to maximize 
environmental benefits and minimize waste. Durability and longevity were recurring themes, 
with experts advocating for the development of long-lasting products and materials. These 
efforts not only lower the environmental impact but also address consumer expectations for 
quality and value.  
 
Material innovation also plays a pivotal role in advancing regenerative design. Rethinking 
material use, whether through durable alternatives, recyclable options, or materials that 
==PAGE:101==
 
96 
actively contribute to ecosystem health, enables organizations to align product development 
with regenerative objectives. This shift away from disposable, resource-intensive designs also 
represent a significant cultural and operational transformation for many industries. The survey 
findings reinforce the importance of design and product innovation as a driver of consumer 
acceptance. Participants emphasized the need for quality, durability, and accessibility in 
regenerative products.  
 
Education and Awareness 
 
Education and awareness are important strategies for advancing the adoption of regenerative 
practices, addressing both consumer knowledge gaps and internal organizational 
understanding. Experts highlighted the complexity of regenerative concepts and the critical 
need for clear communication to help stakeholders understand their value. By explaining these 
practices, companies can foster broader acceptance and trust among consumers, creating a 
foundation for long-term behavioral shifts. Public and corporate education were frequently 
cited as starting points for broader adoption.  
 
Transparent communication about materials, processes, and benefits, for example through 
storytelling on company websites, emerged as an effective strategy to educate consumers. 
These efforts not only inform but also build trust, reinforcing the authenticity of regenerative 
claims. As described before, trust is a crucial driver, as consumers are more likely to support 
practices they perceive as credible and transparent.  
 
Internal education initiatives, such as ESG training programs, underscore the importance of 
educating these principles within organizations. By equipping employees with the knowledge 
and tools to manage these approaches, companies can align internal practices with external 
messaging, creating consistency in their commitment to regeneration. Survey results further 
align with these findings, revealing a strong interest among participants in learning more about 
regenerative practices and their societal benefits. This connection underscores the dual 
necessity of educating consumers and building trust through transparency. Effective education 
strategies not only enhance consumer understanding but also empower them to make 
informed choices, ultimately driving demand for regenerative products. 
 
Collaborative Networks 
 
Collaborative networks were identified as a foundational strategy for fostering systemic change 
and advancing regenerative practices. Many experts emphasized that partnerships across 
industries and stakeholders provide critical platforms for resource-sharing, innovation, and the 
co-development of standards. By aligning diverse actors under shared goals, collaboration 
increases the scalability and impact of regenerative initiatives. Cross-industry partnerships 
were highlighted as particularly effective for leveraging collective expertise and fostering 
innovation. These alliances enable organizations to address common challenges, such as 
regulatory compliance or operational inefficiencies, by pooling resources and knowledge. 
Local partnerships, such as those with developers, advisors, and community stakeholders, 
were also noted for their role in aligning operations with regional needs, strengthening the 
connection between businesses and their broader ecosystem. Moreover, collaboration is not 
limited to external stakeholders; internal networks within organizations also play a vital role. 
==PAGE:102==
 
97 
Aligning employees and departments through shared regenerative goals promote a cohesive 
culture that drives meaningful change. When combined with external partnerships, these 
internal efforts can create a holistic framework for advancing regeneration. 
 
Regulatory and Policy Integration 
 
The integration of regulatory and policy frameworks into organizational strategies was 
identified by experts as an important mechanism for driving regenerative practices. 
Regulations were not solely viewed as constraints but as opportunities to catalyze deeper 
systemic change. By aligning organizational goals with regulatory requirements, businesses 
can use these frameworks as a “springboard” to accelerate the adoption of regenerative 
practices. Adapting to policies such as the EU regulations was recognized as a proactive 
approach to meeting compliance standards while embedding long-term regenerative goals. 
Stricter regulations, when uniformly applied, were advocated as necessary to level the playing 
field and curb practices like greenwashing. This perspective underscores the potential for 
regulations to enhance market fairness and drive accountability across industries. Public 
procurement was another key strategy discussed, with experts emphasizing its potential to 
influence entire value chains.  
 
By extending municipal procurement principles across suppliers and contractors, public 
institutions can promote the widespread adoption of regenerative approaches. This 
demonstrates the critical role of governments not only as regulators but also as active 
participants in fostering regenerative markets. Furthermore, the integration of biodiversity-
focused initiatives into regulatory compliance, such as in park design or other local projects, 
highlights the opportunity to align environmental priorities with policy adherence. Such efforts 
not only meet regulatory requirements but also contribute to broader ecological and societal 
goals. The survey results echo this sentiment, revealing that participants value transparency 
and government involvement in creating trust for regenerative practices. Regulatory measures 
that promote clarity and accountability can address consumer concerns, build trust, and create 
the framework for broader adoption. 
 
Technology-Driven Solutions 
 
As described in the discussion of the drivers before, the role of technology in advancing 
scalable and efficient regenerative practices was a recurring theme in the expert interviews. 
By leveraging technological innovations, organizations can bridge the gap between ambition 
and execution, transforming regenerative goals into actionable strategies. Digital tools were 
particularly highlighted for their ability to enhance transparency and measurability, enabling 
organizations to track progress and refine their approaches effectively. Investments in modern 
technologies were identified as a critical strategy for implementing and scaling regenerative 
practices. For example, precision agriculture enables the precise management of resources 
such as fertilizer, seeds, chemicals, and water, leading to increased profitability, reduced 
environmental impact, and improved product quality (Cook & Bramley, 1998). Data-driven 
solutions not only provide insights into the impact of these initiatives but also foster 
accountability by making progress visible to stakeholders. For example, digitalization allows 
for precise tracking of resource use, emissions, and regenerative outcomes, which can help 
organizations align their actions with their stated goals and communicate these efforts credibly 
==PAGE:103==
 
98 
to consumers and partners. The technological advancements discussed here directly address 
barriers such as operational inefficiencies and the difficulty of measuring regenerative 
outcomes, as previously identified in the interviews. 
 
Operational and Infrastructure Efficiency 
 
Operational and infrastructure efficiency emerged as a critical strategy for advancing 
regenerative practices, with a focus on optimizing resource use and implementing long-term 
solutions. Experts emphasized that existing resources should be maximized, highlighting the 
importance of circulation and active utilization in achieving regenerative goals. This aligns with 
broader regenerative principles of minimizing waste and promoting circularity. Adoption of 
regenerative practices, such as methods like cover cropping, was identified as a practical 
approach to overcoming adoption barriers. This strategy allows to progressive integration of 
regenerative methods without overwhelming operational capacity, thus fostering sustained 
engagement and incremental improvements over time.  
 
Long-term investments in durable infrastructure, such as electric vehicle fleets and 
photovoltaic systems, were also highlighted as essential for achieving both sustainability and 
regeneration. Such investments not only reduce environmental footprints but also position 
organizations as leaders in regenerative innovation, reinforcing their commitment to systemic 
change. Infrastructure upgrades, including ergonomic workplace improvements and flexible 
office policies, further demonstrate how operational efficiency can align with broader ESG 
goals, enhancing employee well-being while driving regenerative outcomes. These findings 
resonate with the earlier discussion of economic and financial benefits as drivers of 
regeneration. Operational efficiencies and infrastructure upgrades not only reduce costs but 
also enhance the long-term economic viability of regenerative practices, creating value for 
organizations and their stakeholders. Moreover, these strategies address some of the 
technological and supply chain barriers identified in the interviews by providing practical 
pathways for implementation. 
 
Cultural Transformation 
 
Cultural transformation is a fundamental strategy for embedding regenerative principles into 
organizational practices, with experts emphasizing the need for a shift in mindset and 
leadership priorities. Transforming the expectations around consumption, production, and 
resource circulation is a critical first step in fostering systemic change. This cultural shift 
requires rethinking not only operational processes but also broader societal attitudes toward 
regeneration. 
 
Internal leadership emerged as a key strategy for cultural transformation. Experts highlighted 
the importance of leadership models that prioritize inclusivity, transparency, and shared 
decision-making. For example, practices such as rotating leadership positions, avoiding 
performance-based bonuses, and empowering employees to have greater influence 
demonstrate how organizations can set an internal example that aligns with regenerative 
values. By fostering a culture of accountability and collective responsibility, organizations can 
encourage deeper employee engagement with regenerative initiatives. This also connects to 
earlier discussions on leadership and cultural change as a driver for regeneration. Leadership’s 
==PAGE:104==
 
99 
role in modeling regenerative values is essential for overcoming inertia and resistance to 
change, as identified in barriers like cultural and behavioral barriers. Additionally, cultural 
transformation aligns closely with strategies like education and awareness, where internal and 
external engagement efforts are crucial for creating alignment across all organizational levels 
and with external stakeholders. 
 
Nature-Based Solutions and Ecosystem Restoration 
 
Nature-based solutions and ecosystem restoration strategies play an important role in aligning 
regenerative practices with ecological principles. By leveraging the inherent capacity of natural 
systems, these approaches not only enhance biodiversity but also create self-sustaining 
ecosystems that support long-term environmental health. Experts underscored that 
biodiversity measures, such as hedgerow implementation, are essential for restoring 
ecological balance and improving the resilience of agricultural landscapes. Strategies like 
agroforestry and mixed forest systems highlight the multifunctionality of nature-based 
approaches. These methods enhance water infiltration, increase biomass production, and 
create more robust ecosystems capable of withstanding environmental stressors like climate 
change. Similarly, practices like worm composting and minimizing soil disturbance emphasize 
leveraging natural processes to build soil health and sustain ecosystems without reliance on 
external inputs. These strategies align with earlier discussions on environmental benefits as a 
key driver for regenerative practices. Experts consistently identified the restoration of 
ecosystems and the mitigation of climate change as motivations for adopting regenerative 
approaches. Nature-based solutions directly address these concerns, showcasing their dual 
role in environmental restoration and as a strategic framework for implementing regenerative 
principles. 
 
Scaling and Long-Term Goals 
 
The emphasis on scaling and long-term goals reflects the critical need for regenerative 
practices to move beyond isolated initiatives and achieve systemic impact. By prioritizing 
scalability, organizations can expand successful pilot projects into broader applications, 
ensuring that regenerative practices are both economically viable and environmentally 
impactful. Experts highlighted the importance of early successes in building stakeholder 
confidence and fostering a commitment to long-term strategies. These initial steps are 
particularly vital in overcoming the barrier of high upfront costs, which has been identified as a 
significant challenge in previous discussions. 
 
By scaling regenerative solutions, economies of scale can be achieved, ultimately reducing 
costs and making these practices more financially accessible for businesses and consumers. 
Measurable progress and scientific milestones play a role in guiding these efforts. Structured 
tracking not only provides accountability but also enables organizations to adjust strategies 
based on data-driven insights. This systematic approach ensures that scaling efforts are 
effective and aligned with broader regenerative objectives, such as reversing global warming 
or achieving net positive operations. These ambitious long-term goals signal a shift from 
reactive sustainability measures to proactive regeneration strategies that address the root 
causes of environmental degradation.  
 
 
==PAGE:105==
 
100 
Certification and Transparency for Customer Trust 
 
Certifications and transparency emerge as strategies to build consumer trust in regenerative 
products. The findings from the expert interviews emphasize that certifications provide not only 
credibility but also a structured framework for embedding regenerative principles into product 
design and marketing. This aligns with survey results, which highlighted that consumers place 
significant importance on credible labels and certifications as a key factor influencing their 
purchasing decisions. Moreover, certifications address a critical barrier identified earlier: the 
education gap among consumers. Transparent labeling and reporting make complex 
regenerative concepts more accessible, helping to prove the benefits and practices associated 
with these products.  For example, certifications like the Regenerative Organic Certified 
program play a significant role in standardizing practices, ensuring credibility, and driving 
adoption (Regenerative Organic Certified, 2023). As discussed in the survey findings, 
consumers who understand the added value of regenerative practices are more likely to 
support them. Certifications, when paired with clear and honest communication, bridge this 
knowledge gap by providing verifiable and digestible information about the environmental and 
social impacts of products. By clearly outlining metrics and actions, organizations can 
counteract skepticism and greenwashing concerns, which were noted as significant barriers in 
both the expert interviews and survey results. Furthermore, transparency initiatives 
complement certification by reinforcing the authenticity of claims, thereby increasing consumer 
confidence and encouraging broader adoption of regenerative products. 
5.2.4 Discussion of the Industrie-Specific and Regional Differences 
Sector-Specific Characteristics 
 
In the product design industry, unique challenges related to its contract-based business model 
and stringent certification requirements influence the adoption of regenerative practices. The 
reliance on contract work, where decision-makers like facility managers are not the end-users, 
complicates the promotion of regeneration. This structure creates a disconnect between those 
making procurement decisions and the broader benefits of regenerative approaches, limiting 
the potential for systemic change. The findings reflect a limitation in engaging stakeholders 
who prioritize cost and immediate functionality over long-term environmental impact. 
Certification requirements also play a dual role in this sector. On the one hand, certifications 
such as FSC® and ISO 14001 support regenerative practices by promoting responsible 
resource management and environmental systems. On the other hand, these certifications are 
often prerequisites for large-scale projects, which can increase administrative burdens and act 
as barriers for smaller organizations that lack the resources to comply. Similarly, strict 
standards for new materials, such as fire resistance and strength testing, add to the complexity 
of introducing innovative, regenerative materials into the market. Frameworks like the Eco 
Design Directive and digital product passports offer significant promise in promoting ecological 
practices, but their slow implementation hampers progress. These regulatory tools have the 
potential to mainstream regenerative approaches by providing clearer guidelines and ensuring 
transparency in the supply chain. However, the delay in their widespread adoption highlights 
a misalignment between policy development and market readiness. It is important to note the 
limitation of these findings, as they are based on input from only two experts in the product 
design industry. While the insights are valuable, they may not capture the full range of 
==PAGE:106==
 
101 
perspectives or challenges faced across the sector. Future research should explore a broader 
pool of stakeholders to validate and expand these findings. 
 
In the interior design industry, a significant barrier to adopting regenerative practices lies in the 
limited influence companies have over Scope 3 emissions. These emissions, which account 
for most greenhouse gas outputs in this sector (97%, as noted by the interviewed expert), 
originate from indirect sources across the value chain, such as material production, 
transportation, and disposal. The challenge is compounded by the fact that these emissions 
often fall outside the direct operational control of interior design companies, making it difficult 
to implement and enforce regenerative initiatives effectively. This finding highlights a systemic 
issue within the industry: the dependence on suppliers and external stakeholders to adopt 
regenerative practices. Without a cohesive effort across the value chain, achieving meaningful 
reductions in emissions and aligning with regenerative goals becomes a complex barrier. It 
underscores the importance of collaborative networks, which were discussed earlier, as a 
strategy to influence upstream and downstream partners to commit to shared environmental 
objectives. However, it is important to acknowledge the limitation of this analysis, as it is based 
on insights from a single expert.  
 
The chemical pest control industry faces unique barriers in adopting regenerative practices 
due to its reliance on highly specific biotechnological innovations. These solutions require 
precise targeting of particular organisms, which adds complexity to their development and 
implementation. The demand for precision highlights the industry's dependence on cutting-
edge research and innovation, as well as its vulnerability to barriers like high development 
costs and lengthy regulatory approval processes. Advancements in technology, such as the 
integration of sensors, AI, and digital tools, are essential for improving forecasting methods 
and managing pest emergence more effectively. These technologies offer the potential to align 
pest control strategies with regenerative goals by enhancing efficiency and reducing 
environmental impacts. However, their adoption requires significant investment and technical 
expertise, which may be challenging for companies with limited resources. Despite these 
barriers, the industry appears to view regenerative practices positively, as noted by one of the 
experts. This optimism reflects an awareness of the potential benefits of regeneration, such as 
improving ecosystem balance and reducing the need for harmful chemicals. It also indicates a 
willingness within the sector to innovate and adapt to evolving environmental expectations. It 
is again worth noting that this discussion is based on insights from a limited number of experts, 
which may not capture the full spectrum of perspectives within the chemical pest control 
industry.  
 
The agricultural sector also faces some unique barriers in adopting regenerative practices, 
influenced heavily by farm size and economic constraints. Smaller farms often encounter 
significant resource limitations, making it difficult to invest in advanced technologies essential 
for regeneration, such as GPS-guided equipment or soil monitoring systems. This disparity 
between small and large operations highlights the structural inequalities in resource access, 
which impede the widespread implementation of regenerative methods. Cultural resistance 
further compounds these barriers, as traditional farming practices remain deeply ingrained. 
Many farmers hesitate to adopt new methods, not only due to unfamiliarity but also because 
of perceived risks to productivity or economic stability. Overcoming this resistance requires 
robust education campaigns and demonstration projects that showcase the tangible benefits 
of regenerative practices. The lack of financial incentives for farmers practicing regeneration 
==PAGE:107==
 
102 
is another critical barrier. Unlike other industries, agriculture often relies on direct financial 
support to implement regenerative changes. The absence of enough policies that reward 
ecological services, such as improved soil health, biodiversity conservation, or carbon 
sequestration, discourages farmers from transitioning to regenerative systems. Introducing 
subsidies, tax breaks, or market-based rewards for ecological benefits could address this gap 
and accelerate adoption. Market limitations also play a significant role in hindering regenerative 
agriculture. Products produced using regenerative methods often fail to secure a price 
premium, leaving farmers without the financial justification for adopting more regenerative 
practices. This lack of market differentiation devalues the environmental and societal benefits 
of regenerative farming, making it harder to compete with conventionally produced goods. 
Addressing this issue requires stronger labeling, consumer education, and market 
development to increase demand and willingness to pay for regenerative products. The 
findings from the survey reinforce these issues, particularly regarding affordability. The survey 
results also showed that the food industry was the most important among the participants. 
 
Regional Differences Discussion 
 
At the EU level, regulatory frameworks provide a unifying structure that facilitates consistency 
and fairness among member states. By establishing common standards, these regulations 
level the playing field for businesses, enabling fairer competition across regions. The 
introduction of recent EU directives and regulations, such as the Circular Economy Act, 
underscores the EU's commitment to sustainability and regeneration. These measures not 
only compel companies to prioritize regenerative practices but also highlight the EU's 
leadership in driving systemic change. However, while the EU's regulatory consistency creates 
opportunities for shared progress, the complexity of implementing such comprehensive 
measures can pose barriers for businesses, as discussed before. The Circular Economy Act, 
for instance, promotes circularity and increased producer responsibility, but its complexity can 
be particularly demanding for smaller firms or those navigating diverse supply chains. Despite 
these barriers, the EU's role as a regulatory driver reinforces the importance of coordinated, 
large-scale approaches to achieving regenerative goals. 
 
The expert interviews highlighted, that at the national and regional levels, inconsistencies in 
regulatory approaches present barriers for businesses striving to adopt regenerative practices. 
While the EU framework establishes overarching standards, the lack of uniformity across 
individual countries can create significant hurdles. Companies must navigate varying 
requirements, complicating the implementation of consistent regenerative strategies across 
borders. Some nations, like France, have emerged as leaders in regulatory innovation, 
exemplified by their mandates for incorporating recycled materials in construction. Such 
initiatives demonstrate the potential for localized policies to act as models for broader adoption. 
However, the fragmented nature of these efforts highlights the need for greater alignment 
between regional and national policies to reduce complexity and foster more cohesive 
progress toward regenerative goals. Balancing localized innovation with broader 
harmonization will be key to enabling businesses to scale their efforts effectively. 
 
At the municipal level, regulatory constraints and policy contradictions present significant 
challenges to implementing regenerative practices. Public procurement laws, while designed 
to ensure fairness and transparency, often limit direct collaboration with local stakeholders. 
==PAGE:108==
 
103 
This restriction can hinder the development of tailored, community-specific solutions that align 
with the unique needs and opportunities of local projects. Furthermore, inconsistencies and 
contradictions between municipal policies create additional barriers to long-term planning and 
execution. Without cohesive and aligned municipal frameworks, organizations may struggle to 
implement comprehensive regenerative strategies effectively. Addressing these issues 
requires greater flexibility in procurement processes and better coordination among municipal 
policies to foster localized, innovative, and regenerative solutions. 
 
Global supply chain challenges are another regional barrier to implementing regenerative 
practices, as varying standards and compliance requirements across countries complicate 
coordination efforts. Organizations must navigate a fragmented regulatory landscape, where 
differing national standards create inefficiencies and inconsistencies in aligning global partners 
to regenerative goals. The effort required to ensure compliance across diverse partners further 
exacerbates this complexity, demanding significant resources and careful oversight. These 
challenges highlight the need for harmonized international standards and stronger 
collaboration within supply chains to streamline regenerative implementation and ensure 
consistency across regions. This alignment would not only reduce administrative barriers but 
also drive effective scaling of regenerative practices globally. 
 
Regional environmental and climatic factors significantly influence the feasibility and 
implementation of regenerative practices, particularly in agriculture. Regional environmental 
conditions, such as dry or high-rainfall climates, shape the practicality and effectiveness of 
specific regenerative measures. For instance, in dry regions, strategies that improve water 
retention provide critical benefits, while high rainfall areas must address barriers like excessive 
weed growth in cover crops. These environmental nuances highlight the need for localized 
approaches to regenerative practices. Moreover, the ongoing impact of climate change further 
complicates the ability to standardize regenerative methods across diverse regions. This 
variability underscores the importance of flexibility and adaptability in designing regenerative 
strategies, ensuring they account for region-specific environmental and climatic conditions 
while maintaining overall regenerative goals. Furthermore, access to land and resources 
represents one more regional barrier to adopting regenerative practices. Regional disparities 
in land availability and use exacerbate this challenge, as highlighted in the context of Denmark. 
Producers often struggle to secure sufficient land to implement regenerative practices, further 
constrained by local opposition and limited availability. These barriers not only hinder the 
scalability of regenerative initiatives but also emphasize the need for equal policies and 
incentives to improve access. Addressing these challenges could involve reforms in land 
allocation policies, promoting shared land use models, and fostering local stakeholder 
collaboration to mitigate opposition. Such measures are essential to ensuring that access to 
land and resources does not remain a regional limiting factor for regenerative progress.  
 
Regional educational initiatives play a role in bridging knowledge gaps and fostering the 
acceptance of regenerative practices. Programs like Germany's Bodenpraktikum exemplify 
targeted efforts to educate farmers on regenerative principles and support their transition to 
regenerative methods. These localized approaches address specific regional needs, providing 
practical knowledge and resources tailored to the challenges faced by farmers in the particular 
region. Such initiatives demonstrate the effectiveness of hands-on, context-specific education 
in overcoming resistance to change, particularly in traditional industries like agriculture.  
 
 
==PAGE:109==
 
104 
6 Recommendations to Overcome the Barriers and 
Leverage the Drivers 
 
Building on the findings and discussions presented in the previous chapters, this section 
provides targeted recommendations to address the identified barriers and leverage the drivers 
to promote regenerative business practices. These recommendations aim to provide 
actionable strategies for overcoming barriers and leveraging drivers while increasing 
opportunities for all stakeholders, including business leaders, consumers, and policymakers.  
 
The proposed recommendations are based on insights from industry experts, survey 
respondents, original ideas, and the theoretical frameworks discussed earlier. They are 
structured to systematically address the grouped barriers and drivers and provide customized 
solutions for each. By adapting these recommendations to the particular needs and 
responsibilities of stakeholders, the aim is to promote a more coherent and effective transition 
to regeneration. These recommendations include both immediate actions and long-term 
initiatives that ensure practical relevance while contributing to broader systemic change. The 
attached graphic shows the links between the recommendations for action and the dimensions 
of the barriers and drivers. It is also easy to see that many recommendations are linked to 
several dimensions (Figure 20). 
 
 
 
 
Figure 20: Overview of Proposed Recommendations (Source: Own Illustration) 
==PAGE:110==
 
105 
6.1 Regulatory and Policy  
Regulatory and policy framework conditions present both barriers and drivers for the 
integration of regenerative processes, as discussed before. A comprehensive strategy is 
required to effectively overcome these barriers and leverage the drivers. 
 
Complex and inconsistent regulations continue to be a major problem in the introduction of 
regenerative processes. Solving these problems requires harmonized measures at the 
national and international levels. Policymakers should focus on aligning regional regulations 
to reduce administrative burdens and create a uniform framework that supports regeneration 
equally across the board. For example, the introduction of universally recognized standards 
for regenerative practices, such as a globally applicable certification system, would provide 
greater clarity and prevent greenwashing. These efforts can be further supported by simplifying 
reporting obligations, especially for smaller companies, through digital tools and standardized 
templates. 
 
Financial support mechanisms such as subsidies, tax breaks, and grants can help mitigate the 
high compliance and implementation costs associated with regenerative practices. For 
example, tax incentives for companies that adopt regenerative practices, or invest in the 
needed infrastructure, or do community work could lower the economic barriers to entry. Public 
procurement policies that prioritize regenerative practices could also serve as a lever to 
mainstream these initiatives in supply chains. To further incentivize regenerative practices, 
regulatory frameworks should introduce additional rewards for businesses that achieve 
“positive net zero” outcomes, going beyond sustainability to actively restore and replenish 
ecosystems. These rewards could include enhanced subsidies, tax breaks, or access to 
exclusive funding pools for companies that demonstrate measurable positive environmental or 
social impacts. Such incentives would not only acknowledge but also promote practices that 
go beyond standard environmental compliance, encouraging organizations to aim for 
ambitious regenerative goals. This approach could promote innovation and position 
regenerative leaders as benchmarks for their industries, accelerating the broader adoption of 
transformative practices. 
 
As discussed, earlier Policies such as the EU Green Deal and the Circular Economy Act 
demonstrate the transformative potential of regulatory frameworks. To increase their impact, 
governments should incorporate regenerative objectives more explicitly into these policies, for 
example by setting ambitious targets for biodiversity restoration and carbon sequestration.  
6.2 Economic and Financial Constraints and Benefits 
Economic and financial barriers are significant barriers to the widespread adoption of 
regenerative practices, but these barriers can be addressed through strategies that also 
leverage existing economic drivers.  
 
The high initial costs associated with the adoption of regenerative technologies or practices, 
as highlighted in both the experts' findings and the literature review, require targeted financing 
mechanisms such as subsidies, tax incentives, and funds. These mechanisms, mentioned in 
the regulatory recommendations earlier in the chapter, can close economic gaps by reducing 
==PAGE:111==
 
106 
upfront costs and providing more accessible pathways for businesses and consumers to adopt 
regenerative solutions. However, the survey also found that a significant proportion of 
consumers are willing to pay a premium for regenerative products to support the regenerative 
impacts of these products. Companies can capitalize on this willingness by strategically 
positioning regenerative offerings as premium products to justify higher prices and offset the 
additional costs associated with regenerative practices. 
 
Another recommendation is the introduction of circular economy models that focus on reusing 
resources and reducing waste. By reusing materials and designing products with future 
recycling or reuse in mind, companies can significantly reduce their production and operating 
costs. Circular economy practices therefore help to optimize resource efficiency, minimize 
waste-related costs, and reduce dependence on expensive raw materials, which is in line with 
regenerative principles while offering clear economic and environmental benefits. 
 
To further reduce the financial barriers, companies can introduce pay-as-you-save models for 
regenerative technologies. The pay-as-you-save model offers a financing strategy that is 
particularly suitable for investments in regenerative infrastructure and technologies where 
upfront costs are prohibitively high. This approach allows companies to introduce regenerative 
solutions by spreading payments over a longer period that is directly linked to the measurable 
benefits of these technologies. By reducing the initial financial burden, the model encourages 
wider adoption of costly regenerative systems and aligns costs with their long-term value. In 
practice, companies make a smaller initial investment to introduce the technology, followed by 
incremental payments as the benefits materialize. For example, a water treatment system for 
industrial use that significantly reduces water consumption and wastewater discharge. 
Companies could pay an initial installation fee, with subsequent payments tied to the amount 
of water saved or treated. This model could lower the barriers to entry for businesses, 
especially small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs), and allows them to adopt advanced 
regenerative infrastructure without straining their budget. It encourages long-term commitment 
by demonstrating the value of regenerative technologies in real-time, building trust, and 
incentivizing ongoing investment (Slipstream Inc., 2022).   
 
To effectively promote regenerative practices and products, marketing campaigns should 
prioritize target demographics identified as most receptive to these initiatives. Survey results 
highlight that women, students, and individuals with higher education levels exhibit the 
strongest preferences for regenerative products. Customized outreach efforts, such as 
educational campaigns, workshops, and social media content, should be designed to resonate 
with these groups, leveraging their demonstrated interest and engagement. By focusing on 
these demographics, businesses can maximize the impact of their messaging, fostering 
greater adoption of regenerative practices while building a loyal consumer base committed to 
regeneration and increase the profit. 
6.3 Cultural Resistance, Change, and Leadership 
Overcoming cultural resistance and fostering leadership-driven transformation are critical to 
incorporate regenerative practices within organizations. Addressing deeply rooted and learned 
norms and leveraging leadership as a driver of change can dismantle inertia, enabling 
businesses to adopt systemic regenerative approaches.  
==PAGE:112==
 
107 
To begin with, leadership training programs can play an important role in overcoming 
resistance to change. These programs should focus on educating leaders about the long-term 
business benefits of regenerative practices, such as improved resource efficiency, alignment 
with consumer trends, and enhanced corporate resilience. For example, the workshops could 
illustrate using best practices examples of how regenerative principles align with long-term 
profitability and resilience against regulatory and market changes. Providing leaders with the 
skills and knowledge to support regeneration could ensure that change begins at the top and 
spreads throughout the entire organization. 
 
Embedding regenerative values into the corporate DNA is another essential recommendation. 
Organizations should integrate regenerative principles into their mission statements, vision, 
and operational goals. For instance, companies can adopt key performance indicators (KPIs) 
that measure environmental and social impacts alongside financial performance. This might 
include metrics such as reductions in Scope 3 emissions, increases in circular resource use, 
or contributions to biodiversity restoration. By aligning these values with corporate objectives, 
organizations can make regeneration a central tenet of their strategic direction, ensuring it is 
treated as a core priority.  
 
Furthermore, employee engagement and education are equally critical. Companies should 
host workshops and campaigns to educate employees about the importance of regenerative 
practices and their role in achieving them. Interactive tools, such as gamified training sessions 
or storytelling campaigns that highlight successful regenerative initiatives, can make these 
efforts more engaging. These campaigns could include storytelling efforts that showcase the 
tangible benefits of regeneration, such as cost savings, market differentiation, or positive 
community impacts. Highlighting individual success stories, such as an employee’s idea 
leading to significant resource savings, can foster emotional connections and drive deeper 
engagement with the company’s mission. Additionally, internal innovation programs can 
empower employees to propose regenerative ideas, fostering a culture of ownership and 
innovation.  
6.4 Supply Chain 
Supply chain barriers significantly impact the adoption and scalability of regenerative practices. 
Tackling these issues requires targeted strategies that focus on collaboration, system 
redesign, and innovation to build more resilient and regenerative supply chains. 
 
A critical area for improvement lies in addressing Scope 3 emissions, which are often beyond 
the direct control of individual companies. These emissions account for a substantial portion 
of the carbon footprint across supply chains, yet their mitigation remains a challenge due to 
the complexity of multi-tiered networks. To combat this, companies should adopt standardized 
reporting and tracking tools that provide greater visibility into supply chain emissions. 
Collaboration with third-party organizations can enhance transparency and accountability, 
ensuring that data is accurate and actionable. Moreover, aligning supply chain partners with 
regenerative goals is essential for addressing Scope 3 emissions effectively. Companies 
should establish clear criteria for regenerative practices and incorporate these requirements 
into supplier contracts. This ensures that upstream and downstream partners actively 
contribute to emission reductions and share a collective commitment to regenerative 
==PAGE:113==
 
108 
objectives. Such alignment not only improves overall supply chain regeneration but also helps 
to build a network of stakeholders working collaboratively toward shared goals. 
 
Recycling inefficiencies present another significant barrier to regenerative supply chains. Many 
materials and products are currently designed without consideration for reuse or recyclability, 
making it difficult to close material loops. To address this, companies must prioritize the 
redesign of products and materials to facilitate reuse and recycling. Modular designs, for 
instance, could allow for easy disassembly and material recovery, making end-of-life 
management more efficient. A practical example is in the furniture industry, where 
manufacturers could use standardized materials and detachable components to enable easier 
recycling and repurposing of products. Investments in advanced recycling technologies are 
equally important. For example, chemical recycling methods, which break down complex 
polymers into their raw materials, can significantly improve the recyclability of challenging 
materials like multi-layer plastics. Governments and industry should collaborate to fund 
research and development in this area, creating scalable and accessible solutions that benefit 
multiple sectors. 
 
The development of consistent industry standards, as discussed earlier, is another key 
recommendation to overcoming supply chain challenges. Clear, universal guidelines for 
regenerative practices can align efforts across industries. For instance, creating a global 
certification for regenerative supply chains would provide businesses with a framework for 
verifying their practices and building trust with consumers and partners through the supply 
chain. 
6.5 Awareness and Education 
Survey data reveals a gap between awareness and understanding of regenerative practices. 
While 44.84% of respondents were aware of regenerative practices, their differentiation from 
sustainability was rated moderately low, with a Likert score of 3.01. This highlights the need 
for targeted educational initiatives to deepen public understanding of regenerative principles. 
Correlation and regression analysis further show that greater understanding is strongly linked 
to the perceived importance and adoption of regenerative business models, emphasizing the 
critical role of education in fostering informed consumer support. To address the gap in 
understanding and acceptance of regenerative practices, comprehensive strategies targeting 
various stakeholder groups are essential. Awareness campaigns, educational initiatives, and 
accessible resources can bridge the knowledge divide and empower individuals and 
organizations to adopt these practices effectively. 
 
One key area is the integration of regenerative principles into school curricula. Educational 
institutions play an important role in shaping future generations, yet the focus on sustainability 
often overshadows the concept of regeneration. Schools could incorporate regenerative 
concepts into science, economics, ethics and geography lessons, highlighting the advantages 
of moving beyond sustainability.  
 
Similarly, universities should expand their offerings to include regenerative business practices. 
Although many institutions provide degrees focused on sustainability, regeneration remains 
underrepresented. Universities could develop specialized modules or even entire degree 
==PAGE:114==
 
109 
programs on regeneration, addressing topics such as biodiversity enhancement, regenerative 
agriculture and innovative regenerative technologies. Partnering with regenerative businesses 
to offer internships and research opportunities would ensure that students gain practical 
insights and valuable industry connections. 
 
Public awareness campaigns could be another powerful tool to educate consumers about the 
societal and environmental benefits of regeneration. These campaigns could leverage diverse 
platforms, including social media, documentaries, and community events, to communicate the 
importance of these practices. Storytelling should be a central component, making 
regeneration relatable by showcasing real-world success stories. 
 
Enhancing consumer understanding through labeling also plays a crucial role. Survey findings 
emphasize the influence of certification labels on purchasing decisions. Companies can use 
QR codes on product packaging to provide consumers with detailed insights into the 
regenerative practices involved. Transparent communication not only builds trust but also 
highlights the tangible benefits of choosing regenerative products, encouraging informed 
consumer choices. 
 
Internal education within organizations is equally important. Workshops and training programs 
aimed at business leaders and employees can align internal practices with regenerative 
principles. These programs could include environmental, social, and governance (ESG) 
training, as well as case studies on successful regenerative transitions. Empowering 
employees with this knowledge creates internal advocates who champion these practices 
within their roles, fostering a culture of regeneration from within. 
 
Accessible online resources further complement these efforts. Developing centralized 
platforms that offer guides, webinars, and case studies on regenerative practices can serve as 
valuable tools for businesses and individuals alike. These resources should cater to varying 
levels of knowledge, ensuring they are useful to both beginners and those with advanced 
understanding. 
 
Social media influencers and public figures could also play a significant role in raising 
awareness about regeneration. Authentic endorsements and partnerships with influencers 
who resonate with environmentally conscious audiences can make these concepts more 
relatable and inspire broader action. 
 
Furthermore, interactive events and exhibitions provide tangible ways for stakeholders to 
engage with regenerative practices. For example, a “Regeneration Fair” could feature live 
demonstrations of regenerative farming techniques, innovations in regenerative products, and 
talks from industry leaders. Such events not only educate but also create opportunities for 
networking and collaboration, further amplifying the impact of regenerative efforts. 
6.6 Technological Barriers & Technological Innovation and 
Advancements 
Overcoming technological barriers and leveraging progress requires a two-step approach: 
immediate action using existing technologies and fostering innovation to close current gaps. 
==PAGE:115==
 
110 
Technology is a critical factor in translating regenerative ambition into scalable and measurable 
action. By focusing on practical applications and improving accessibility, companies can 
overcome inertia and drive widespread adoption of regenerative practices. 
 
A recurring theme in the experts' insights was the observation that many companies are 
delaying the introduction of regenerative technologies and waiting for future advances instead 
of using immediately available tools. This wait and see approach hinders progress and ignores 
the significant potential of existing solutions. For example, precision agriculture tools, such as 
GPS-guided devices and IoT-based monitoring systems, enable efficient resource use by 
optimizing water, fertilizer and seed applications. These technologies are already available 
and offer measurable benefits, including increased profitability and reduced environmental 
impact, and need to be utilized. Companies that have successfully integrated these existing 
technologies show that action need not wait for breakthrough innovations. 
 
While existing technologies provide a solid foundation, addressing gaps requires innovation 
through collaboration. Companies should work with technology providers to jointly develop 
industry-specific solutions. Pilot projects and prototyping play a crucial role in this context, as 
they provide the opportunity to test and refine these innovations. Demonstrating measurable 
results through pilot initiatives can encourage wider acceptance and attract funding. Joint funds 
supported by public and private investment can mitigate the financial risks associated with 
scaling these technologies and ensure that innovations reach the market with high impact. 
 
High initial costs continue to be a major barrier to the introduction of advanced technologies. 
As highlighted in the economic recommendations, financial mechanisms such as subsidies, 
tax incentives and pay-as-you-save models can facilitate access to technologies. 
Governments and industry associations must provide such mechanisms to enable small and 
medium-sized enterprises to adopt regenerative technologies. 
6.7 Collaboration and Partnerships & Stakeholder Alignment 
As already discussed before in the other recommendations, addressing operational and supply 
chain challenges and promoting alignment between stakeholders requires a strong focus on 
collaboration, resource sharing, and innovative strategies that combine different priorities. 
Collaboration has already been highlighted in previous recommendations as a key solution for 
overcoming various barriers, emphasizing its importance for multiple dimensions of 
regenerative practices. By leveraging partnerships and fostering collective efforts, 
organizations can address systemic barriers and unlock opportunities for regenerative 
practices. 
 
To balance the different interests within supply chains, multi-stakeholder platforms should be 
developed to promote collaboration between companies, non-governmental organizations, 
policymakers, and academia. These platforms can serve as hubs for knowledge exchange, 
where participants share best practices, address common challenges, and develop common 
regenerative goals. For example, a platform could facilitate dialogue on Scope 3 emissions 
reduction by aligning supply chain partners around common benchmarks for emissions 
reduction and resource optimization. One promising idea could be the creation of a digital 
“matchmaking portal” that connects companies, non-governmental organizations, and 
==PAGE:116==
 
111 
government agencies interested in promoting regenerative projects. Such a portal could list 
current and planned initiatives, potential funding opportunities, and areas where expertise is 
needed. For example, a company looking to adopt regenerative agriculture could find local 
NGOs with expertise in farmer training or access government grants for soil restoration. This 
central hub would streamline partnerships, reduce search costs, and increase the collective 
impact of regenerative efforts. 
 
Involving local communities in regenerative initiatives builds trust and ensures that solutions 
are appropriate to the context. Companies could involve community members in the planning 
and implementation of projects, such as urban green spaces, planting new forests, or 
regenerative agriculture programs. This participatory approach not only builds local capacity 
but also strengthens public support for regenerative practices. 
 
 
==PAGE:117==
 
112 
7 Conclusion 
7.1 Summary of Key Findings and Responses to the Research 
Questions 
The research question, “What are the main barriers and drivers for the adoption of regenerative 
business practices?” guided this study, aiming to identify and analyze the key factors 
influencing the integration of regenerative practices into business operations. To address this, 
a mixed methods approach was employed, ensuring a comprehensive understanding of the 
topic. The survey offered quantitative insights into consumer preferences and perceptions, but 
also some qualitative answers to the open-ended questions, while expert interviews provided 
qualitative depth, capturing challenges and opportunities. The literature review established a 
theoretical foundation, contextualizing the findings within broader academic frameworks. By 
triangulating these methodologies, the research identified key barriers and drivers. Together, 
these methods offered a robust understanding of the multifaceted landscape shaping the 
adoption of regenerative business practices. 
7.1.1 Research Question 1 
Drivers 
 
Survey results revealed a strong consumer preference for regenerative products, particularly 
when prices are competitive with conventional alternatives. Two-thirds of respondents 
indicated a clear preference for regenerative options at price parity, and over 40% expressed 
a willingness to pay up to a 10% premium, highlighting the perceived value of regenerative 
practices. This finding is further reinforced by expert interviews and the literature, which 
emphasized that consumer behavior has shifted significantly, with a growing demand for 
environmentally friendly and socially responsible products and business practices. 
 
Experts, literature and also the open-ended questions in the survey emphasized the 
transformative potential of regulatory and policy measures such as the EU Green Deal and the 
Circular Economy Act, which incentivize regenerative practices through mechanisms such as 
subsidies, tax benefits and mandatory standards. These frameworks create accountability 
while setting ambitious targets that encourage innovation and market adaptation. Subsidies 
and financial incentives were highlighted as particularly important to reduce the high initial 
costs of adoption, especially for small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs). In addition, the 
experts noted that regulatory pressure is forcing companies to rethink and adapt their 
practices, creating a sense of urgency for systemic innovation. By providing tangible support, 
such as financial assistance and simplified compliance procedures, these measures enable 
companies to overcome economic barriers and transition to regenerative models more 
effectively. However, the findings also highlight the need for harmonized and consistent 
regulations to minimize administrative burdens and encourage wider adoption. 
 
Cross-industry and cross-stakeholder collaboration has proven to be a key driver of 
regenerative practices. Insights from expert interviews and the literature highlight that 
partnerships between businesses, non-governmental organizations, governments, and local 
==PAGE:118==
 
113 
communities not only accelerate adoption but also foster innovation by sharing resources and 
co-developing solutions. Cross-sector collaboration promotes systemic change by pooling 
expertise and setting common standards, while community engagement ensures that projects 
meet local needs, promoting social inclusion and operational success. 
 
Emerging technologies such as precision agriculture, IoT-based monitoring, and modular 
product designs were recognized as critical enablers of regenerative practices. Insights from 
expert interviews and the literature emphasized their role in simplifying adoption and driving 
systemic change. Pilot projects and prototyping further demonstrate the scalability and 
economic viability of these innovations, providing proof of concept and encouraging broader 
adoption. 
 
Regenerative practices provide businesses with a distinct competitive advantage by enhancing 
brand loyalty, improving reputation, and aligning with consumer values. Insights from expert 
interviews and literature emphasize that regeneration acts as a unique selling proposition 
(USP), positioning companies as innovators and industry leaders. These practices not only 
attract environmentally conscious consumers and investors but also appeal to younger 
employees who prioritize transparency and responsibility. By fostering a purpose-driven 
culture and showcasing commitment to innovation and long-term value creation, businesses 
can strengthen market perception, expand their customer base, and build stronger 
partnerships. 
 
Long-term profitability and operational efficiency were also identified as key drivers for 
adopting regenerative practices, as highlighted by experts and supported by the literature. 
Practices such as circular economy models and regenerative agriculture reduce costs and 
waste while enhancing resource efficiency and lowering input expenses. These approaches 
contribute to improved profitability over time by optimizing operations and reducing reliance on 
costly raw materials. Additionally, corporate partnerships and financial support play an 
important role and Corporate Stakeholder Sustainability Funds provide the financial flexibility 
needed to develop and implement regenerative initiatives. Furthermore, regenerative business 
models position companies to better adapt to market shifts and economic challenges, 
reinforcing their resilience and long-term financial success. 
 
Also, environmental benefits are another driver for adopting regenerative practices, as experts 
highlighted key advantages such as improved soil fertility, water retention, biodiversity 
enhancement, and carbon sequestration through practices like diverse cropping systems and 
hedgerow planting. These methods restore ecosystems while mitigating greenhouse gas 
emissions. These benefits align with the original motivation for adopting regenerative 
practices: addressing the urgent need to combat climate change and environmental 
degradation. Survey results reinforce these insights, showing strong consumer interest in 
products that promote environmental regeneration. Participants identified biodiversity 
enhancement and carbon reduction as key factors in their purchasing decisions, emphasizing 
the critical role of ecological impact in driving both adoption and market demand. 
 
Lastly, leadership and cultural transformation emerged as critical drivers for adopting 
regenerative practices. Strong leadership ensures alignment with long-term goals, including 
regeneration into corporate strategies and values. Beyond organizations, societal attitudes are 
shifting, with growing public awareness supporting regenerative initiatives. These insights from 
==PAGE:119==
 
114 
experts and survey highlight the transformative potential of leadership and cultural change in 
driving regeneration. 
 
Barriers 
 
Insights from expert interviews, and literature revealed that regulatory complexity and 
inconsistency across regions are significant barriers to the adoption of regenerative practices. 
Fragmented legal frameworks, particularly within Europe, increase administrative burdens and 
discourage businesses from pursuing regenerative strategies. The lack of standardized 
definitions for terms like "regenerative agriculture" further complicates the landscape, enabling 
greenwashing and undermining consumer trust. Furthermore, high compliance costs, 
particularly due to stringent reporting obligations like those under the CSRD, disproportionately 
burden smaller firms. Additionally, the focus of many current policies on short-term economic 
goals or carbon neutrality rather than more ambitious regenerative objectives leaves 
companies without adequate support to implement long-term restorative approaches. 
 
The insights from expert interviews, surveys, and literature highlight economic constraints as 
a key barrier to regenerative practices. High upfront costs for regenerative technologies or 
other regenerative materials disproportionately affect smaller businesses. Survey respondents 
frequently cited cost concerns, with many unwilling to pay a premium for regenerative products. 
Experts also emphasized that a short-term profit focus limits investment in practices with long-
term benefits. The literature confirms that the lack of financial mechanisms to offset these costs 
further exacerbates these challenges, hindering widespread adoption of regenerative models. 
 
Furthermore, insights from expert interviews were gathered, the survey and literature which 
shows cultural resistance and behavioral challenges as major barriers to the adoption of 
regenerative practices. Established organizational norms, particularly in traditional industries, 
create inertia against change, with established habits and ideological barriers impeding 
innovation. Leadership is a critical factor, as proactive advocacy is often required to overcome 
resistance and align organizational culture with regenerative goals. On a societal level, 
consumer skepticism, limited awareness, and a reluctance to prioritize regenerative products 
over cost and convenience further hinder adoption. These barriers reflect a broader gap 
between awareness and action, emphasizing the need for both internal cultural shifts and 
greater public understanding of the value of regeneration. 
 
The expert interviews and literature made clear, that the adoption of regenerative practices is 
associated with significant operational and supply chain challenges. Aligning global supply 
chains with regenerative targets, particularly for Scope 3 emissions, is complicated by the 
complexity of relationships with upstream and downstream partners. Limited control over these 
areas underscores the need for greater collaboration and shared accountability. Inefficient 
recycling systems and materials that are not destined for reuse make it difficult to close material 
loops. Furthermore, while innovative solutions exist, their scalability and practicality are often 
insufficient, creating a gap between potential and widespread adoption. 
 
Survey findings revealed a notable gap in awareness and understanding of regenerative 
practices, with 44.84% of respondents reporting familiarity but demonstrating only a moderate 
comprehension of how regeneration differs from sustainability. This lack of understanding is 
==PAGE:120==
 
115 
significant, as correlation analysis indicated that deeper knowledge of regenerative principles 
is directly linked to higher perceived importance and likelihood of adoption. Expert interviews 
reinforced these findings, highlighting a disconnect between the complexity of regenerative 
practices and consumers' simplified expectations, which often prioritize cost or immediate 
functionality over long-term benefits. Additionally, the educational gap extends to businesses, 
where limited understanding of regenerative concepts inhibits effective implementation and 
advocacy. 
 
The expert interviews and the literature showed that the introduction of regenerative 
technologies is hindered by skepticism about their scalability, high upfront costs and 
uncertainty about their long-term effectiveness. The experts emphasized that companies often 
delay action and wait for more advanced solutions, slowing progress and overlooking the 
potential of existing tools. Limited lifecycle management strategies, such as effective recycling 
systems, further limit scalability. Rapid technological progress discourages investment as early 
technologies risk becoming obsolete.  
 
Lastly, the alignment of diverse stakeholder priorities remains a significant barrier to advancing 
regenerative practices. Conflicting interests among key groups, such as consumers, 
employees, suppliers, and policymakers, complicate the development of unified strategies. 
Survey insights and expert interviews revealed that external stakeholders, such as suppliers 
and B2B customers, are often resistant to adopting regenerative practices due to perceived 
costs or implementation complexity. Similarly, internal misalignment between teams focused 
on regeneration and investors prioritizing short-term profits further hinders progress. Literature 
highlights that trust and coordination issues within supply chains exacerbate these barriers, 
delaying collaboration and hindering innovation. 
7.1.2 Research Question 2 
The research question “How can companies leverage drivers and overcome barriers to 
successfully adopt regenerative business practices?” aimed to provide actionable strategies 
and recommendations for the transition to regeneration. To answer this question, the study 
used again the mixed methods approach that built on the findings of the first research question. 
It focused on the findings from the expert interviews, which offered practical and specific 
strategies based on real-world challenges and opportunities. The survey added a quantitative 
perspective by highlighting consumer preferences and perceptions that informed the strategic 
recommendations. The literature review provided theoretical context. By triangulating these 
sources, the study identified strategies and recommendations that aligned with both the 
barriers and drivers identified, complemented by original recommendations developed through 
the synthesis of findings. This approach ensures a well-rounded framework to support 
businesses and policymakers in their efforts to promote regenerative practices. 
 
To promote the adoption of regenerative practices, the study recommends the harmonization 
of regulations at national and international levels, the introduction of globally recognized 
certifications, and the simplification of reporting obligations through digital tools and 
standardized templates. Financial incentives such as subsidies, tax breaks, and grants are 
proposed to reduce compliance and implementation costs, especially for small and medium-
sized enterprises (SMEs). Public procurement policies that prioritize regenerative practices 
and rewards for achieving positive net-zero outcomes, such as exclusive financing pools, are 
==PAGE:121==
 
116 
other strategies to encourage innovation and adoption. Recommendations also include 
improving existing frameworks such as the EU Green Deal and the Circular Economy Act by 
explicitly including regenerative objectives such as biodiversity restoration and carbon 
sequestration. These measures aim to streamline regulatory processes, remove barriers and 
position regenerative practices as an integral part of the business and policy landscape. 
 
To address economic barriers and leverage financial opportunities, this study recommends 
targeted subsidies and tax incentives to reduce the high initial costs of adopting regenerative 
technologies. These measures, which overlap with the regulatory recommendations, are an 
example of how recommendations can address multiple barriers and drivers. In addition, 
companies are encouraged to position regenerative products as premium offerings to 
capitalize on consumers' willingness to pay higher prices for products with proven 
environmental and social benefits. The introduction of circular economy models is another 
recommendation that focuses on resource efficiency and waste reduction. By designing 
products for reuse and recycling, companies can reduce production costs and dependence on 
raw materials, aligning economic goals with regenerative principles. In addition, the pay-as-
you-save financing model is proposed to make regenerative technologies more accessible. 
This approach allows companies to spread payments over time that are directly linked to 
measurable benefits such as resource savings, removing upfront financial barriers and 
encouraging long-term adoption. Finally, targeted marketing campaigns are recommended to 
reach populations that are particularly receptive to regenerative practices, such as women, 
students and those with higher levels of education. Together, these strategies aim to balance 
financial constraints with opportunities for economic growth and environmental regeneration. 
 
To overcome cultural resistance, achieve social and corporate change and incorporate the role 
of leaders, a number of recommendations have been made. Training leaders is critical to 
overcoming cultural resistance by equipping them with the skills to adopt regenerative 
practices and align them with long-term business benefits such as resource efficiency and 
resilience. Incorporating regenerative principles into the company's KPIs, such as reducing 
Scope 3 emissions or circular resource use, ensures that regeneration becomes a key 
strategic priority. Internal workshops and storytelling campaigns can educate employees about 
regenerative practices and highlight success stories that demonstrate concrete benefits such 
as cost savings and market differentiation. In addition, internal innovation programs can 
encourage employees to propose regenerative ideas, fostering ownership, engagement and a 
culture of innovation. 
 
Education initiatives are crucial to closing the gap in understanding regenerative practices, 
which is important, as the survey results show. Integrating regenerative principles into school 
and university curricula will ensure that future generations understand the value of moving 
beyond sustainability. Universities can also offer specialized modules and partner with 
regenerative companies for internships to link academic knowledge with practical experience. 
Public awareness campaigns using social media, storytelling, and events such as 
“regeneration fairs” can educate consumers about the tangible benefits of regeneration. 
Certification labels and QR codes on products help improve consumer understanding by 
providing transparent insights into regenerative practices. Within companies, workshops and 
ESG training for managers and employees can help align internal practices with regenerative 
principles, fostering a culture of engagement and innovation. Central online resources, such
