as guides and webinars, are aimed to make regenerative knowledge accessible. Collaboration 
==PAGE:122==
 
117 
with influencers and public figures also has the potential to drive broader social engagement 
and effectively reinforce these efforts. 
 
Strengthening supply chains to support regenerative practices requires collaborative 
strategies, innovative redesigns, and robust accountability mechanisms. To mitigate Scope 3 
emissions, often the largest share of supply chain carbon footprints, companies should 
implement standardized reporting and tracking systems to increase transparency and 
actionable data. Partnering with third-party organizations and aligning supplier contracts with 
regenerative criteria can ensure that all stakeholders contribute to emission reduction goals 
and foster collaborative progress. Furthermore, businesses should focus on designing 
products for reuse and recyclability, such as incorporating modular designs for easier 
disassembly and material recovery. Advanced recycling technologies, like chemical recycling, 
could be adopted to address challenging materials, while public-private investments can 
accelerate the scalability of these solutions. Consistent industry standards are essential to 
unify efforts across supply chains. Establishing global certifications for regenerative practices 
would provide a reliable framework for verifying compliance and building trust among 
consumers and supply chain partners. These measures collectively enable more resilient and 
regenerative supply chains, amplifying their role in regenerative business transformation. 
 
Addressing technological barriers and drivers requires businesses to adopt a dual strategy: 
utilizing existing technologies while fostering innovation to close gaps. Immediate action with 
available solutions can deliver measurable benefits by optimizing resource efficiency and 
reducing environmental impacts. Businesses should capitalize on these proven technologies 
to drive progress rather than delaying for future innovations. To bridge gaps, collaboration with 
technology providers to develop industry-specific solutions is essential. Pilot projects and 
prototyping can test and refine these innovations, demonstrating their scalability and attracting 
necessary funding. As mentioned before, public-private joint funds can mitigate financial risks, 
enabling high-impact technologies to reach market readiness. To support broader adoption, 
particularly among small and medium-sized enterprises, financial mechanisms such as 
subsidies, tax incentives, and pay-as-you-save models are vital. These tools reduce the high 
initial costs of advanced technologies and ensure that regenerative innovations become 
accessible and scalable across industries. 
 
Furthermore, establishing multi-stakeholder platforms that bring together businesses, NGOs, 
policymakers, and academia can enhance resource sharing, knowledge exchange, and the 
development of shared goals. A promising initiative could be the creation of a digital 
“matchmaking portal” to connect companies, NGOs, and government agencies working on 
regenerative projects. This centralized hub could list ongoing initiatives, funding opportunities, 
and areas requiring expertise, streamlining collaboration and enhancing collective impact. 
Engaging local communities in regenerative projects further enhances success and ensures 
contextually relevant solutions. Whether through urban greening, afforestation, or regenerative 
agriculture, community involvement builds trust and fosters public support. These participatory 
efforts not only enhance project outcomes but also create stronger networks of shared 
responsibility and action for regeneration. 
==PAGE:123==
 
118 
7.1.3 Sub-Research Question 1 
The research question, "Which factors influence consumer attitudes and commitment towards 
regenerative products?" was addressed primarily through the survey, which aimed to deepen 
the understanding of consumer behavior. The goal was to uncover why some consumers 
would choose regenerative products while others would not, and to identify the key elements 
that shape purchasing decisions. The survey provided detailed insights into consumer 
awareness, preferences, and perceptions, as well as their concerns and expectations 
regarding regenerative products. Quantitative analyses, such as correlation and regression, 
revealed significant influences, including the role of labeling, education, and demographic 
factors. Open-ended responses enriched the findings with qualitative details, highlighting 
concerns about cost, accessibility, and trust. By synthesizing these insights, the study offers a 
comprehensive view of the factors that influence consumer decisions, providing valuable 
guidance for businesses and policymakers to better align their strategies with consumer 
priorities. 
 
The findings directly address the research question by showing that awareness and 
understanding are significant factors influencing consumer attitudes toward regenerative 
products. Although 44.84% of respondents were familiar with regenerative practices, their 
moderate understanding (average Likert score of 3.01) suggests that this limited knowledge 
impedes deeper engagement and commitment. Participants demonstrated a clear interest in 
learning more about regenerative practices, with a mean score of 3.38, indicating moderate to 
high engagement. Only 22.03% showed little to no interest, suggesting that educational 
initiatives would likely be well-received and play a crucial role in enhancing consumer 
understanding and commitment. Education emerged as a critical factor, with a significant 
positive correlation between understanding the differences between sustainability and 
regeneration and the importance of regenerative business models (𝑟 = 0.20, 𝑝 = 0.012). 
Education also emerged as a strong predictor in the regression analysis, with participants who 
expressed interest in learning about regenerative practices being more likely to prefer these 
products (𝑏 = 0.31, 𝑝 = 0.01).  This underscores the importance of educational initiatives to 
drive consumer commitment. Therefore, improving education and communication about 
regenerative practices is crucial for fostering consumer interest and informed decision-making. 
This highlights the critical role of awareness and understanding in shaping attitudes and 
encouraging adoption. Consumers prefer diverse and credible information sources to learn 
about regenerative products, with "scientific studies" (38.33%) and "certifications and reports" 
(37.44%) ranking highest. Trust in objective, fact-based channels reflects skepticism towards 
direct company information, which was preferred by only 15.42%. 
 
The survey reveals a strong consumer preference for regenerative products when price is not 
a barrier, with 66.53% expressing a strong inclination toward these options and an average 
preference score of 4.45 out of 5. However, willingness to pay a premium varies: 41.05% are 
willing to pay up to 10% more, while 11.79% are unwilling to pay extra. These results 
demonstrate that while price competitiveness remains crucial, a significant market segment 
values the environmental and social benefits of regenerative products enough to justify a 
premium. Income showed a positive effect in the regression analysis (𝑏 = 0.29, 𝑝 = 0.02), but 
this contrasts with the correlation results, highlighting that income matters when other factors 
are controlled. The open-ended survey responses underlined these observations, as a key 
finding was the importance of affordability and perceived value. Many participants highlighted 
==PAGE:124==
 
119 
the high cost of regenerative products as a concern, emphasizing that consumers are more 
likely to commit to these products if their pricing aligns with conventional alternatives or if their 
long-term value is effectively communicated. 
 
Labels and certifications significantly influence consumer attitudes toward regenerative 
products, with a mean importance score of 3.85 and 34.36% of respondents rating them as 
very important. The correlation shows that labels and certifications play an important role, with 
significant correlations showing their influence on purchasing decisions (𝑟 = 0.71) and 
preference for regenerative products (𝑟 = 0.58). The regression highlights that labeling is the 
most significant predictor, demonstrating its critical role in building trust and validating claims 
(𝑏 = 0.43, 𝑝 < 0.01). Certifications from independent environmental organizations are the most 
trusted, emphasizing the critical role of perceived independence and credibility. These labels 
not only build consumer trust but also serve as educational tools, bridging knowledge gaps by 
clarifying the benefits and processes of regenerative practices. The open-ended survey 
responses expressed the same, as transparency and trust also emerged as critical factors. 
Participants expressed a strong preference for credible certifications and independent 
verification of regenerative claims, which significantly influence their confidence in such 
products. Skepticism about greenwashing underscores this. 
 
To gain consumer trust, the survey underlines the crucial importance of transparency and 
accountability measures. 70.85% of respondents emphasized the need for certifications and 
independent audits. Detailed insights into production processes were also highly valued by 
69.51% of respondents, reflecting the strong demand for transparency in the verification of 
regenerative claims. While long-term environmental goals (56.05%) were important, 
immediate and verifiable measures were preferred. Incorporating customer feedback was less 
important (26.91%), indicating that trust is primarily dependent on concrete, externally 
validated commitments rather than engagement-based initiatives. 
 
Survey respondents strongly prioritized environmental benefits of regenerative practices over 
social ones, with 70% emphasizing “active restoration and protection of ecosystems” as the 
most important benefit. Other highly valued benefits included “products with a positive net 
impact” (59.57%), “circular economy” practices (58.26%), “long-term soil and water quality 
improvement,” and “enhancing biodiversity” (both over 50%). Social benefits, such as 
“empowering local communities,” were less prioritized, reflecting a stronger consumer focus 
on addressing urgent environmental challenges. Regenerative practices were seen as 
particularly crucial in the “food and beverages” category (80.87%) and “clothing and textiles” 
(74.78%), highlighting consumer awareness of environmental and ethical issues in these 
sectors. 
 
Interestingly, students displayed a higher likelihood of choosing regenerative products 
compared to full-time employees (𝑏 = 0.21, 𝑝 = 0.05), and participants with advanced degrees 
(master's or doctorate) show significantly higher preferences (𝑏 = 0.20–0.25, 𝑝 < 0.05). This is 
potentially driven by exposure to academic and social discussions. Gender also plays a role, 
as women are more inclined toward regenerative products (𝑏 = 0.15, 𝑝 = 0.04). 
 
The answers to the open questions also show that product quality is also a decisive factor. 
Regenerative products must be at least on a par with conventional products in terms of quality. 
Availability in a large number of stores and online must also be guaranteed. 
==PAGE:125==
 
120 
7.1.4 Sub-Research Question 2 
The sub-research question, “How do industry-specific and regional characteristics influence 
regenerative practices?” was explored through interviews with 15 experts from diverse 
industries and regions. These professionals provided insights into how sectoral and regional 
factors, such as supply chain structures, regulations, policies, resistance to change, 
technology and climate differences, shape the adoption of regenerative practices. The findings 
prove that both industry and location play critical roles in determining the feasibility and 
success of regenerative strategies. This highlights the need for customized approaches that 
align with specific industry-specific and regional contexts to effectively advance regenerative 
practices. 
 
Industry-Specific Characteristics 
 
In the product design industry, sector-specific challenges, such as its contract-based business 
model and strict certification requirements, significantly influence the adoption of regenerative 
practices. The disconnect between decision-makers and end-users, coupled with a focus on 
cost and functionality, limits the promotion of regenerative approaches. Certifications like 
FSC® and ISO 14001 both support and hinder progress by encouraging responsible practices 
while creating administrative burdens, particularly for smaller organizations. Additionally, 
regulatory tools like the Eco Design Directive show potential for advancing regeneration but 
face slow implementation, delaying widespread impact. 
 
In the interior design industry, the dominance of Scope 3 emissions, accounting for 97% of the 
sector's greenhouse gas outputs, presents a key challenge to adopting regenerative practices. 
These emissions, arising from material production, transportation, and disposal, often fall 
beyond the direct control of companies, making effective implementation difficult. This 
systemic issue highlights the industry's reliance on suppliers and external stakeholders to 
adopt regenerative initiatives. Collaborative networks are critical to aligning value chain 
partners with shared environmental objectives. 
 
The chemical pest control industry faces challenges in adopting regenerative practices due to 
its reliance on specialized biotechnologies and high development costs. Advanced tools like 
AI and sensors offer the potential for efficiency and reduced environmental impact but require 
significant investment. Despite these barriers, the industry's openness to regeneration reflects 
its recognition of ecosystem benefits and reduced chemical reliance, though broader sector 
insights are needed. 
 
The agricultural sector faces barriers to regenerative practices, including resource disparities 
between small and large farms, cultural resistance to change, and insufficient financial 
incentives. Smaller farms often lack access to advanced technologies, while traditional farming 
practices and perceived risks hinder adoption. Financial support, such as subsidies or rewards 
for ecological benefits, is crucial to encourage transition. Additionally, market limitations, such 
as the lack of price premiums for regenerative products, devalue their benefits. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:126==
 
121 
Regional Differences 
 
At the EU level, regulatory frameworks such as the Circular Economy Act play a critical role in 
driving regenerative practices by establishing consistent standards across member states. 
These regulations promote fair competition and systemic change, highlighting the EU's 
potential leadership in regenerative change. However, their complexity can pose challenges, 
particularly for smaller firms and those managing diverse supply chains. While these measures 
emphasize the importance of large-scale coordination, balancing regulatory ambitions with 
practical implementation support is key to fostering widespread adoption of regenerative 
practices. 
 
At national and regional levels, inconsistent regulatory approaches create challenges for 
businesses adopting regenerative practices. While the EU provides overarching standards, 
varying country-specific requirements complicate cross-border implementation. Leaders like 
France, with policies mandating recycled materials in construction, showcase the potential of 
localized innovation. However, this fragmentation highlights the need for greater alignment 
between national and regional policies to simplify processes and support cohesive progress. 
 
At the municipal level, regulatory constraints and policy inconsistencies hinder the 
implementation of regenerative practices. Public procurement laws, while promoting fairness, 
often restrict collaboration with local stakeholders, limiting customized community solutions. 
Policy contradictions further complicate long-term planning and execution. Greater flexibility in 
procurement processes and improved alignment of municipal policies are essential to support 
localized, innovative regenerative strategies. 
 
Global supply chain challenges present a significant regional barrier to regenerative practices. 
Varying national standards and compliance requirements complicate coordination, creating 
inefficiencies in aligning global partners to shared goals. Ensuring compliance across diverse 
regions demands substantial resources and oversight. Harmonized international standards 
and enhanced collaboration are crucial to reducing these barriers, fostering consistency, and 
scaling regenerative practices globally. 
 
Regional environmental and climatic factors play a critical role in shaping the feasibility of 
regenerative practices, especially in agriculture. Dry regions benefit from water retention 
strategies, while high-rainfall areas face challenges like weed growth in cover crops. These 
variations emphasize the need for localized approaches that address specific conditions. 
Additionally, climate change complicates standardization, underscoring the importance of 
adaptable strategies that align with both regional differences and broader regenerative goals. 
 
Access to land and resources is another significant regional barrier to adopting regenerative 
practices, with disparities in land availability and use exacerbating the issue. In regions like 
Denmark, limited land access, local opposition, and availability constraints hinder scalability. 
Addressing these challenges requires policy reforms, shared land-use models, and stronger 
local collaboration to ensure equitable access and support for regenerative initiatives. 
 
Regional educational initiatives are vital for addressing knowledge gaps and promoting the 
adoption of regenerative practices. Programs such as Germany's Bodenpraktikum effectively 
educate farmers on region-specific regenerative principles, offering tailored, practical support 
==PAGE:127==
 
122 
for transitioning to these methods. These hands-on, localized efforts demonstrate the value of 
context-specific education in overcoming resistance, particularly in traditional sectors like 
agriculture. 
7.2 Limitations of Data and Methods  
While the mixed methods approach employed in this study provides a robust framework for 
exploring regenerative business practices, it is important to acknowledge certain limitations, 
which were carefully mitigated to ensure the reliability and validity of the findings. In the chapter 
Research Methodology the most important limitations of the research method were mentioned. 
 
The qualitative component of the study relied on a relatively small sample size of 15 expert 
interviews. Each industry represented in the study typically had only 1-2 experts, limiting the 
generalizability of industry-specific insights. While the sampling strategy ensured a diverse 
range of perspectives from multiple industries, the findings may not be fully generalizable to 
all sectors or contexts. Furthermore, the regional focus of the expert interviews (e.g., EU level, 
national, and municipal perspectives) might not fully address global trends or challenges in 
adopting regenerative practices. 
 
For the quantitative component, the survey achieved a response rate of approximately 25.2%, 
with 252 completed responses out of approximately 1.000 individuals invited to participate. 
While this response rate is within the acceptable range for online surveys (20-25%, SurveyLab, 
n.d.), it limits the generalizability of the findings. Certain demographic groups, such as non-
binary respondents and individuals from lower-income brackets, were underrepresented, 
which may skew the insights. Additionally, the reliance on self-reported data introduces 
potential biases, as survey responses depend on participants' honesty and self-perception, 
which can lead to inaccuracies. The online survey distribution method ensured accessibility 
and facilitated the inclusion of a diverse range of respondents. However, this approach 
introduced potential non-response bias, excluding individuals without regular internet access 
or those less inclined to participate in online surveys. Open-ended survey questions provided 
valuable qualitative depth but could lacked consistency, making it challenging to generalize 
findings across all respondents. The survey was distributed primarily in Germany, Denmark, 
and to a lesser extent in the United States, which further limits the global applicability of the 
findings. While this regional focus provides valuable insights into these specific contexts, it 
may not fully capture consumer behavior and attitudes toward regenerative practices in other 
geographic regions. 
 
Access to broader data posed a limitation, particularly in terms of industry-wide datasets, 
sources about examples of the adoption of regenerative principles in companies, or proprietary 
company information, which could have enriched the depth of certain analyses. Furthermore, 
while the research touched on regional differences, the scope for comparative analysis 
between regions (e.g., EU countries) was constrained by the available data. 
==PAGE:128==
 
123 
7.3 Recommendations for Future Research 
While this study addressed key aspects of barriers, drivers, and strategies for adopting 
regenerative business practices, several areas remain underexplored and present 
opportunities for future research. A key recommendation is to conduct deeper, industry-
specific studies. While this research offered insights across multiple sectors, other industries 
such as retail, pharma, food and beverages, automotive, transportation, electronics, and 
consumer goods remain underrepresented. Future studies should focus on identifying sector-
specific challenges and opportunities to develop tailored solutions and measure the long-term 
impact of regenerative practices. While this study highlighted barriers and drivers of 
regeneration, much of the existing research is concentrated on agriculture, leaving other 
industries underexplored.  
 
Another critical area for future exploration is consumer behavior. While this study provided 
valuable insights into consumers' willingness to pay premiums for regenerative products and 
the factors influencing their commitment, the broader research landscape remains 
underrepresented. Limited studies have delved deeply into this topic, leaving significant gaps 
in understanding regional differences in consumer preferences and their impact on market 
demand. The findings highlight the importance of consumer education and awareness, but 
further research is needed to investigate effective strategies for fostering greater engagement. 
This area offers substantial potential for future exploration, as understanding and addressing 
consumer behavior is key for scaling regenerative practices and creating lasting market 
demand. 
 
Technological advancements also present a significant area for further study. Innovations are 
key drivers of regeneration but remain insufficiently explored. Future research could examine 
the development and adoption of such technologies. Similarly, frameworks and tools that help 
organizations measure and improve their regenerative capacity need further investigation. 
 
As shown, regional and regulatory variations also influence the feasibility of regenerative 
practices. Expanding the geographic scope of research to include underrepresented regions 
such as Asia, Africa, and South America could provide valuable insights. Comparative 
analyses of regulatory frameworks across different policy environments could highlight best 
practices and barriers to adoption. Additionally, studies should explore how municipal and 
national policies support or hinder regenerative initiatives, particularly in regions with 
fragmented regulations. Given that global challenges like climate change and social inequities 
affect all regions, it is crucial to identify and develop regenerative solutions that address these 
issues on a worldwide scale. By fostering cross-regional collaboration and knowledge 
exchange, future research could contribute to the creation of universally applicable strategies 
while accounting for local and cultural specificities. 
 
Long-term and comparative studies would further enrich the understanding of regenerative 
practices. Conducting longitudinal research could track the evolution of these practices and 
their systemic impact over time. Comparative analyses of regenerative businesses across 
regions and industries would help identify scalable models and best practices, offering 
guidance for broader adoption. 
 
==PAGE:129==
 
124 
Another significant gap identified in this research is the lack of explicit regenerative business 
models that businesses can directly apply. Future research could focus on developing practical 
frameworks and case studies for implementing regenerative practices. This includes exploring 
methodologies for integrating regenerative principles into existing business models and 
investigating standards and certification systems to ensure consistency and track progress.

xxii 
b. First Order Concepts, Second Order Themes, and Aggregate 
Dimensions 
Barriers 
 
Related Expert Quotes and Derived First Order Concepts 
Interview 1: 
• 
“Market unwilling to pay for regeneration costs.” 
o Quote: “The market is not yet paying for this kind of extra aspects in the 
product.” 
• 
“High costs due to trial-and-error innovation.” 
o Quote: “We are in this situation where we get pressure from the production 
side because we are developing new paths – that means a lot of trial and 
error.” 
• 
“Scalability challenges for new materials.” 
o Quote: “There are a lot of materials on the market, but not all of them make 
sense, especially if they’re not scalable.” 
• 
“Undefined end-of-life solutions.” 
o Quote: “End-of-life solutions are often missing; it makes little sense to use a 
material that ends up as waste again.” 
• 
“Costly compliance with safety regulations.” 
o Quote: “New materials must undergo specific tests, like fire resistance or 
strength tests, which are often challenging.” 
• 
“Oversimplified customer expectations.” 
o Quote: “The biggest challenge is to explain the complexity of regeneration to 
our customers.” 
 
Interview 2:  
• 
“Regulatory silos hinder systemic change.” 
o Quote: “Established structures, such as legislation and organizational silos, 
make it challenging to adopt regenerative practices.” 
o Quote: “There is a lack of integrated policies and coherent strategies at the 
municipal level, leading to conflicting goals.” 
• 
“Restricted access to land and resources.” 
o Quote: “Access to land is currently a major issue, particularly for smaller 
producers looking to adopt new business models.” 
o Quote: “The absence of viable business models shifts risks onto producers, 
especially pioneers in regenerative agriculture.” 
• 
“Uneven resource distribution limits adoption.” 
o Quote: “The willingness to spend more income on regenerative products 
depends more on interest than income.” 
o Quote: “Income inequality complicates the effort to ensure broad access to 
regenerative options.” 
==PAGE:147==
 
xxiii 
• 
“Legal barriers limit scaling of partnerships.” 
o Quote: “EU procurement laws restrict the ability to collaborate directly with 
local stakeholders on larger projects.” 
o Quote: “Scaling small initiatives remains difficult as they typically occupy 
niche roles.” 
 
Interview 3: 
• 
“High documentation demands from regulations.” 
o Quote: “The reporting obligations of the CSRD are enormous, but they 
establish essential standards.” 
o Quote: “The Supply Chain Due Diligence Act requires detailed 
documentation, which is often difficult to implement.” 
• 
“Limited access for low-income customers.” 
o Quote: “Supplementary insurance often only reaches higher-income 
customers.” 
o Quote: “Through projects like the Recognition for Child Welfare, we try to 
address societal issues affecting disadvantaged groups.” 
• 
“Challenges in coordinating global supply chains.” 
o Quote: “Standards differ across countries, making coordination more 
challenging.” 
o Quote: “Ensuring compliance across diverse global partners is a significant 
effort.” 
• 
“Difficulty in uniting stakeholders.” 
o Quote: “Involving all stakeholders, from employees to customers, in a unified 
commitment is particularly challenging.” 
 
Interview 4: 
• 
“High costs of sustainable and regenerative materials and processes.” 
o Quote: “Sustainable and regenerative materials are often more expensive 
and time-consuming to process than new materials.” 
o Quote: “Higher costs for regenerative products impact the final price, and 
customers are often hesitant to pay more.” 
• 
“Complex regulatory requirements.” 
o Quote: “Regulations are extremely complex, with numerous national and EU-
wide requirements.” 
o Quote: “Laws like the Circular Economy Act promote circular approaches and 
increase manufacturer responsibility, adding complexity.” 
• 
“Limited influence on Scope 3 emissions.” 
o Quote: “97% of our greenhouse gas emissions come from Scope 3, where we 
have the least influence.” 
o Quote: “It's difficult to encourage suppliers to reduce emissions and provide 
circular materials.” 
• 
“Customer confusion and resistance to change.” 
o Quote: “Customers need to be convinced to return old materials instead of 
simply discarding them.” 
==PAGE:148==
 
xxiv 
o Quote: “Many customers don't understand what certain labels mean and feel 
overwhelmed by the variety.” 
 
Interview 5:  
• 
“Lack of awareness about regenerative practices.” 
o Quote: “Most companies don't even know what regenerative practices entail 
and have no mindset for it yet.” 
o Quote: “The majority of firms are unaware of the need for regenerative 
business practices.” 
• 
“Limited resources for implementation beyond compliance.” 
o Quote: “Companies are stuck in reporting and can't engage in real practices 
because they’re busy generating data.” 
o Quote: “They don’t have the capacity free to go beyond compliance.” 
• 
“Focus on compliance over innovation.” 
o Quote: “New EU regulations have companies focused on compliance rather 
than actual implementation of regenerative practices.” 
o Quote: “Reporting obligations make it hard to free resources for other 
priorities.” 
• 
“Economic barriers for small and medium-sized enterprises.” 
o Quote: “High upfront costs, particularly for SMEs, make it difficult to adopt 
regenerative practices.” 
o Quote: “Global crises exacerbate economic challenges, limiting investments 
in regeneration and sustainability.” 
• 
“Cultural resistance in traditional industries.” 
o Quote: “In traditional industries, the corporate culture itself becomes a barrier 
to regenerative change.” 
o Quote: “Many organizations are resistant to shifting away from business-as-
usual practices.” 
 
Interview 6: 
• 
“High initial costs for regenerative technology.” 
o Quote: “Initial investments in GPS navigation and camera-assisted hoeing 
technology are significant barriers, especially for smaller farms.” 
o Quote: “There is currently no price premium in the market for products 
produced using regenerative methods.” 
• 
“Lack of regulatory clarity and misuse of terms.” 
o Quote: “The term 'regenerative agriculture' is not protected, allowing 
companies to misuse it for marketing without substantial action.” 
o Quote: “Bureaucratic burdens discourage farmers from adopting certified 
regenerative practices.” 
• 
“Educational gaps among farmers and consumers.” 
o Quote: “Many farmers and consumers don’t understand what regenerative 
agriculture truly entails.” 
==PAGE:149==
 
xxv 
o Quote: “Educational gaps make it difficult to convey the advantages of 
regenerative farming.” 
• 
“Challenges in scaling regenerative methods.” 
o Quote: “While modern technologies like camera-assisted tools are helpful, 
their implementation is often too complex or costly for broad adoption.” 
o Quote: “Scalability of regenerative methods remains a challenge for many 
farms.” 
• 
“Conflicting demands from stakeholders.” 
o Quote: “Farmers face conflicting demands from consumers, landlords, and 
environmental goals.” 
o Quote: “There is often resistance to change among different stakeholders.” 
 
Interview 7:  
• 
“Cultural inertia limits innovation.” 
o Quote: “The biggest challenge is the culture of doing it and the expectations 
of what you do.” 
o Quote: “It is a cultural challenge.” 
• 
“Economic focus hinders regenerative adoption.” 
o Quote: “The driving factor in our field is and was always money.” 
o Quote: “It’s about making things as cheaply as possible and making as much 
money as possible.” 
• 
“Recycling inefficiencies due to past designs.” 
o Quote: “It's not that we don't know how to recycle things, but previous poor 
processes make materials difficult to reuse.” 
o Quote: “The capability of dismantling things into sufficient detail and not 
wasting things in that process is a key issue.” 
• 
“Lack of regulatory incentives for regeneration.” 
o Quote: “There is none, no certification or regulation is driving factors in our 
field.” 
• 
“Knowledge gaps in design and production.” 
o Quote: “There’s a lack of understanding about what grounds we make 
products and why we should or shouldn’t have new ones.” 
o Quote: “We still sit in an industrial paradigm where we produce stuff, but 
that’s not regenerative.” 
 
Interview 8: 
• 
“Economic challenges for small farms.” 
o Quote: “The initial investment for planting and maintaining hedgerows is 
significant.” 
o Quote: “We need public funding to make regenerative practices viable, 
especially for smaller farms.” 
• 
“Resistance to change in farming culture.” 
o Quote: “Farmers often resist change because they are stuck in old habits and 
practices.” 
==PAGE:150==
 
xxvi 
o Quote: “We still operate in a system that prioritizes short-term economic gains 
over long-term regeneration.” 
• 
“Limited policy support for ecological farming.” 
o Quote: “The lack of comprehensive soil protection and climate laws makes it 
harder to justify regenerative practices.” 
o Quote: “There are no policies that financially reward ecological services 
provided by regenerative farming.” 
• 
“Greenwashing and lack of public understanding.” 
o Quote: “The term 'regenerative' is often misused for marketing without 
substantial action.” 
o Quote: “Consumers need education to differentiate between genuine and 
misleading claims.” 
 
Interview 9:  
• 
“Difficulty in aligning regeneration with core business.” 
o Quote: “Clients often see regenerative business practices as separate from 
their core business, which makes it harder to align them with business 
objectives.” 
o Quote: “The biggest challenge is translating regeneration from something 
abstract to something tangible and aligned with their goals.” 
• 
“Burden of extensive reporting requirements.” 
o Quote: “EU regulations like CSRD create significant reporting requirements, 
which clients see as a burden, even though it forces valuable internal 
conversations.” 
o Quote: “Upcoming regulations like CSDDD will require extensive 
transparency, which is a huge endeavor for companies.” 
• 
“Dependency on new technologies delays action.” 
o Quote: “All the necessary technologies already exist, but companies 
sometimes wait for new innovations instead of acting now.” 
o Quote: “It’s important not to rely on technology as an excuse for inaction, 
especially given the urgency of climate and biodiversity crises.” 
• 
“Leadership resistance to regenerative shifts.” 
o Quote: “If leadership doesn’t consistently push regeneration, inertia will lead 
companies back to their old practices.” 
o Quote: “Many companies struggle with the knowledge gap; adopting 
regenerative models requires unlearning old paradigms.” 
• 
“Confusion from untrustworthy sustainability labels.” 
o Quote: “Most labels lack substance and confuse consumers rather than 
building trust.” 
o Quote: “EU legislation is targeting greenwashing, but many companies still 
use environmental claims for marketing without accountability.” 
 
Interview 10: 
• 
“Disconnection between consumers and production origins.” 
==PAGE:151==
 
xxvii 
o Quote: “People don’t see how products are made or where materials come 
from; they’re disconnected from production.” 
o Quote: “Most people don’t know the materials their products originate from, 
which hinders understanding of regeneration.” 
• 
“Focus on financial growth limits regeneration.” 
o Quote: “The mindset that success is only defined by financial growth is a 
major challenge.” 
o Quote: “The current economic system is built on exploitation of people and 
the planet.” 
• 
“High costs deter adoption of regenerative options.” 
o Quote: “Adopting regenerative materials or processes costs more, making it 
financially challenging to implement.” 
o Quote: “Consumers often choose cheaper, less regenerative products due to 
affordability issues.” 
• 
“Slow societal shift toward regenerative consumption.” 
o Quote: “A paradigm shift toward consuming fewer, better-quality products 
takes at least a generation.” 
o Quote: “Many people don’t care about regeneration, making change feel slow 
and frustrating.” 
• 
“Lack of regulatory consistency across markets.” 
o Quote: “Regulations like the Eco Design Directive and digital product 
passports help, but implementation is slow.” 
o Quote: “Companies not regulated equally create unfair competition for those 
adopting sustainable practices.” 
• 
“Mistrust created by greenwashing practices.” 
o Quote: “Greenwashing is pervasive, making it hard for consumers to 
differentiate real efforts.” 
o Quote: “Without documentation, regeneration claims should not be trusted.” 
 
Interview 11: 
• 
“Navigating regulatory complexity across regions.” 
o Quote: “There are many different regulations across Europe, making it 
challenging to keep up with and comply in different countries.” 
o Quote: “We collaborate with local developers, lawyers, and advisors for 
regulatory compliance and operational management. This helps to navigate 
through regulatory frameworks.” 
• 
“Challenges in securing land and addressing local opposition.” 
o Quote: “Land accessibility is a significant challenge, especially with local 
opposition and limited availability.” 
o Quote: “We approach landowners directly to understand their concerns and 
involve local communities in planning.” 
• 
“Uncertainty due to electricity price volatility.” 
o Quote: “Volatile electricity prices make investment decisions and profitability 
uncertain for greenfield projects.” 
o Quote: “We mitigate this risk through long-term Power Purchase Agreements 
(PPAs) with fixed electricity prices.” 
==PAGE:152==
 
xxviii 
• 
“Financial and operational barriers in battery storage.” 
o Quote: “Battery storage presents financial and technical barriers, as it’s still 
an emerging technology.” 
o Quote: “We are gaining experience with battery projects to better understand 
revenue streams and operations.” 
• 
“Cost pressures and limited resource availability.” 
o Quote: “Cost pressures, combined with limited land resources, are barriers to 
scaling renewable projects.” 
o Quote: “We optimize costs through standardized practices and framework 
agreements with suppliers.” 
 
Interview 12: 
• 
“Low customer demand for transparency in practices.” 
o Quote: “What counts for our customers are good products, quality, and very 
competitive prices but less transparency regarding regenerative business 
practices.” 
• 
“Minimal influence of external partners.” 
o Quote: “External partners such as NGOs or research institutions are not 
relevant to our industry, which is more economically and rationally driven.” 
 
Interview 13:  
• 
“Circularity challenges in aviation recycling.” 
o Quote: “True circularity in aviation remains distant; we recycle and repurpose, 
but it's not complete.” 
o Quote: “The aluminum we recycle from aircraft can't return to aviation use, 
which limits its sustainability.” 
• 
“Regulatory constraints on supply chain flexibility.” 
o Quote: “Regulatory pressure impacts our supply chain, limiting potential 
suppliers and affecting innovations.” 
o Quote: “Supply chain regulations are a significant constraint, impacting 
flexibility in supplier selection.” 
• 
“Lack of industry-wide standards for material reuse.” 
o Quote: “Recycling standards for aviation materials are underdeveloped 
compared to other industries.” 
o Quote: “The aluminum we recycle from aircraft can't return to aviation use, 
which limits its sustainability.” 
 
Interview 14: 
Note: Some answers from the written interview have been used directly as First Order Concepts. 
 
• 
“High regulatory requirements for developing and approving new 
technologies.” 
o Quote: “High regulatory requirements for developing and approving new 
technologies pose a significant barrier.” 
==PAGE:153==
 
xxix 
• 
“Time-intensive development processes for new active substances and 
technologies.” 
• 
“Regeneration requirements combined with the need for commercial viability 
pose significant challenges.” 
• 
“Contradictory pressures from NGOs and state regulations.” 
o Quote: “NGOs and state regulations occasionally create contradictory pressures 
that can obstruct the implementation of regenerative principles.” 
• 
“High costs of developing regenerative technologies compared to conventional 
solutions.” 
o Quote: “The high costs associated with developing regenerative technologies 
compared to conventional solutions represent a financial burden.” 
• 
“Limited budgets for extensive research and development.” 
• 
“Difficulty convincing customers of the added value of regenerative products 
when cost or performance differences exist.” 
o Quote: “Convincing customers of the added value of regenerative products is 
challenging when cost or performance differences are present.” 
• 
“Societal lack of understanding of the complexity of regenerative practices and 
the industry’s challenges.” 
o Quote: “The societal lack of understanding of the complexity of regenerative 
practices and the challenges faced by the industry impedes their acceptance.” 
 
Interview 15: 
• 
“Greenwashing in the construction sector.” 
o Quote: “This marketing is full of euphemisms and distortions; upon closer 
examination, one becomes skeptical.”   
• 
“Slow societal and industry shifts.” 
o Quote: “It took 60 years for the insights of the Club of Rome to be internalized. If 
it takes that long again, the disaster will already have occurred.”

Drivers 
 
Related Expert Quotes and Derived First Order Concepts 
Interview 1: 
• 
“Growing consumer interest in regeneration.” 
o Quote: “There is a real shift – consumers are asking the right questions more 
and more.” 
• 
“Embedding regeneration in corporate purpose.” 
o Quote: “If regeneration is embedded in the company’s purpose, it drives long-
term growth.” 
• 
“Partnerships with recyclers and researchers are crucial.” 
o Quote: “We work with partners to ensure end-of-life solutions.” 
 
Interview 2: 
• 
“Limited but growing public demand for regeneration.” 
o Quote: “Public demand influences the adoption of pilot projects and 
innovation but represents a small portion of the food sector.” 
o Quote: “Public procurement is seen as a lever but is insufficient for broader 
systemic change.” 
• 
“Collaboration as a driver for regenerative innovation.” 
o Quote: “Partnerships play a crucial role in developing and implementing 
innovative approaches.” 
o Quote: “Collaborating with large actors in the value chain could facilitate 
greater change.” 
• 
“Pilot projects showcase potential.” 
o Quote: “Prototyping and pilot projects are essential for testing new 
approaches and fostering acceptance.” 
 
Interview 3: 
• 
“Public demand as a catalyst for regeneration.” 
o Quote: “Our customers expect transparency and responsible action.” 
o Quote: “The decarbonization strategy is directly motivated by public demand.” 
==PAGE:157==
 
xxxiii 
• 
“Internal culture supports ESG goals.” 
o Quote: “Employee training is crucial to deeply integrate ESG principles within 
the company.” 
o Quote: “Our social engagement strengthens employees' identification with 
sustainability goals.” 
• 
“Leveraging innovation for regenerative impact.” 
o Quote: “Green bonds combine ecological impact with stable returns.” 
o Quote: “Digital tools help us track regenerative metrics more efficiently.” 
  
Interview 4: 
• 
“Pioneering spirit and leadership as a catalyst for regeneration.” 
o Quote: “Our founder transformed the company after realizing we needed to 
make significant changes.” 
o Quote: “Mission Zero marked the beginning of our sustainability and 
regeneration journey and set ambitious goals.” 
o Quote: “The focus on regenerative practices is deeply embedded in our 
company's DNA.” 
• 
“Advancing regeneration through regulatory frameworks.” 
o Quote: “The EU Taxonomy and Green Deal drive sustainable and 
regenerative practices forward.” 
o Quote: “Laws like the Circular Economy Act promote circular approaches and 
increase manufacturer responsibility, adding complexity.” 
o Quote: “France’s requirements for recycled materials in buildings serve as a 
model for other nations.” 
• 
“Collaborative networks for regenerative progress.” 
o Quote: “We have close partnerships with suppliers and regularly discuss 
ways to advance together.” 
o Quote: “Suppliers are increasingly setting their own climate goals, helping us 
create shared standards.” 
o Quote: “Industry associations facilitate exchanges and the development of 
common standards.” 
• 
“Innovation and technological advances for regenerative practices.” 
o Quote: “A major milestone was the prototype of a CO₂-negative product.” 
o Quote: “Through biobased materials, we can store more carbon than we 
emit.” 
o Quote: “The Science-Based Targets Initiative helps us measure the success 
of our actions scientifically.” 
 
Interview 5: 
• 
“Regulatory pressures create urgency.” 
o Quote: “New EU regulations force companies to address sustainability and 
regeneration.” 
o Quote: “Regulations are a driver, even if initially viewed as a burden.” 
• 
“Growing public awareness for regeneration.” 
==PAGE:158==
 
xxxiv 
o Quote: “It’s essential for people to understand that sustainability is no longer 
enough.” 
o Quote: “Educational initiatives increase both consumer and corporate interest 
in regenerative practices.” 
• 
“Technological advancements as an enabler for regeneration.” 
o Quote: “Technology enables access to tools and systems that make

o Quote: “Digital tools are simplifying sustainability tracking and

• 
“Economic and market opportunities for regenerative practices.” 
o Quote: “Regenerative practices offer long-term market advantages despite

o Quote: “Companies adopting these practices are seen as innovators, giving

• 
“Cross-industry collaboration enables regeneration.” 
o Quote: “Working across industries accelerates the adoption of regenerative 
models.” 
o Quote: “Collaboration breaks silos and fosters shared innovation.” 
 
Interview 6: 
• 
“Growing consumer demand for sustainability and regeneration.” 
o Quote: “Consumers are increasingly interested in sustainable and 
regenerative produced food, driving farms to adopt regenerative methods.” 
o Quote: “The link between biodiversity and regenerative agriculture resonates 
with consumers.” 
• 
“Environmental benefits of regeneration.” 
o Quote: “Improved soil fertility and water retention make farms more resilient 
to climate changes.” 
o Quote: “Regenerative practices enhance biodiversity and stabilize 
ecosystems.” 
• 
“Corporate interest and funding as financial enablers.” 
o Quote: “Companies seeking sustainability and regeneration are exploring 
partnerships with regenerative farms for local offset projects.” 
o Quote: “Sustainability funds from corporate partners provide financial support 
for regenerative initiatives.” 
• 
“Technological advancements supporting regenerative agriculture.” 
o Quote: “Modern tools like GPS navigation and precision agriculture help 
streamline regenerative practices.” 
o Quote: “Robotic systems reduce physical strain and enhance productivity in 
regenerative farming.” 
 
Interview 7: 
• 
“Shift towards a sharing economy.” 
o Quote: “We have a slow change where we rent our bikes, we rent our cars... 
Things are continuously in use.”  
==PAGE:159==
 
xxxv 
• 
“Increasing societal awareness for regeneration.” 
o Quote: “There’s definitely a slow change in societal demand for sustainable 
and regenerative practices.” 
• 
“Effective use of existing technologies.” 
o Quote: “It’s about how we put existing technologies into use, not necessarily 
about new technologies.” 
• 
“Financial opportunities from over spenders.” 
o Quote: “People with the money to pay for proper solutions are where change 
can start.” 
 
Interview 8: 
• 
“Personal commitment and long-term vision.” 
o Quote: “I have seven grandchildren, and I want them to have a future.”  
o Quote: “Regenerative farming is not just about fixing the soil but about leaving 
the planet better than we found it.”  
• 
“Environmental resilience and soil health benefits.” 
o Quote: “Perennial plants and diverse cropping systems improve soil fertility 
and water retention.”  
o Quote: “Grass-fed livestock produce significantly less methane and 
ammonia.”  
• 
“Potential for carbon sequestration.” 
o Quote: “Hedgerows can store up to three tons of CO2 per hectare annually.”  
o Quote: “Grassland managed properly can rebuild humus and sequester 
carbon.”  
• 
“Technological advancements and innovation.” 
o Quote: “Minimal soil disturbance and reduced axle loads prevent long-term 
soil damage.”  
o Quote: “Worm composting acts like a sourdough starter, helping soils recover 
naturally.”  
 
Interview 9: 
• 
“Regulation as a catalyst for regeneration.” 
o Quote: “EU-level regulations ensure consistency across member countries, 
leveling the playing field for businesses.” 
o Quote: “Transparency laws force companies to address environmental and 
social issues, sparking important internal conversations.” 
• 
“Attracting talent through regenerative practices.” 
o Quote: “Younger generations expect transparency and responsibility from 
employers, making sustainability and regeneration a key driver for attracting 
talent.”  
o Quote: “The war for talent motivates companies to adopt sustainable and 
regenerative practices to remain competitive.”  
• 
“Economic viability and resource efficiency as a regeneration driver.” 
==PAGE:160==
 
xxxvi 
o Quote: “Regeneration often means focusing on resource use, which can save 
costs and align with long-term business objectives.”  
o Quote: “Sustainable and regenerative practices reduce dependency on higher 
salaries by instilling purpose in employees.” 
• 
“Regulatory pressure as a regeneration driver.” 
o Quote: “Although my faith in consumers is low, regulatory pressure ensures 
companies cannot avoid addressing sustainability and regeneration.”  
o Quote: “Transparency legislation forces businesses to rethink practices and 
prepare for tighter future regulations.”  
  
Interview 10: 
• 
“Competitive advantage and profitability through regeneration.”

• 
“Consumer demand and corporate clients.” 
o Quote: “Large corporate clients set regeneration criteria, pushing to innovate 
and comply.”  
o Quote: “Contract clients are more demanding of sustainability and 
regeneration than individual consumers.”  
• 
“Government regulations as a catalyst.” 
o Quote: “Legislation like the Corporate Sustainability Reporting Directive 
forces companies to address resource use and circularity.” 
o Quote: “Governments should lead by implementing strict regulations and 
fines to prevent unsustainable practices.”  
  
Interview 11:

•

xxxvii

Interview 12: 
• 
“Economic benefits for customers and their end customers.”

costs.” 
o  Quote: “Higher margins and profits after one year of switching from analog to 
digital.” 
• 
“Positive market perception.” 
o Quote: “The industry and customers have an extremely positive perception of 
regenerative practices.” 
  
Interview 13: 
• 
“Cost efficiency via circular practices.” 
o Quote: “Reusing serviceable parts reduces costs for airlines while promoting

o Quote: “Strong interest in AirflowFilm and EngineWash highlights the

• 
“Fuel efficiency as a regeneration driver.” 
o Quote: “Fuel-saving solutions like EngineWash and AirflowFilm resonate

o Quote: “Sustainability and fuel efficiency are priorities for our airline clients.”

not just at the end.”

Interview 8:

o Quote: “Agroforestry and mixed forests increase water infiltration and

Industry-Specific Analysis 
 
Related Expert Quotes 
Interview 1: 
Industry: Product Design 
  
Quote: “We are primarily in contract business, where decision-makers like facility managers 
are not end-users. This makes regeneration a harder sell.” 
Quote: “New materials must undergo specific tests, like fire resistance or strength tests, 
which are often challenging.” 
Quote: “Certifications such as FSC® and ISO 14001 are often prerequisites for large-scale 
projects.” 
 
Interview 2: 
Industry: Public Sector Entity 
Quote: “EU procurement laws restrict the ability to collaborate directly with local 
stakeholders on larger projects.” 
Quote: “Contradictions between municipal policies create challenges in implementing long-
term regenerative projects.” 
Quote: “Access to land is currently a major issue, particularly for smaller producers looking 
to adopt new business models.” 
 
==PAGE:175==
 
li 
Interview 3: 
Industry: Insurance Industry 
Quote: “Standards differ across countries, making coordination more challenging.” 
Quote: “Ensuring compliance across diverse global partners is a significant effort.” 
 
Interview 4: 
Industry: Flooring and Interior Design 
Quote: “97% of our greenhouse gas emissions come from Scope 3, where we have the least 
influence.” 
Quote: “France’s requirements for recycled materials in buildings serve as a model for other 
nations.” 
Quote: “Laws like the Circular Economy Act promote circular approaches and increase 
manufacturer responsibility, adding complexity.” 
 
Interview 5: 
Industry: Sustainability Consulting  
Quote: “Industries with access to cutting-edge technologies can implement regenerative 
practices faster.” 
Quote: “New EU regulations force companies to address sustainability and regeneration.” 
Quote: “In traditional industries, the corporate culture itself becomes a barrier to regenerative 
change.” 
 
Interview 6: 
Industry: Agriculture  
Quote: “High rainfall areas may face challenges like excessive weed growth in cover crops.” 
Quote: “Dry regions benefit from regenerative measures that improve water retention.” 
Quote: “Small farms lack the resources to invest in expensive technologies, unlike larger 
operations.” 
Quote: “There is currently no price premium in the market for products produced using 
regenerative methods.” 
Quote: “The impact of climatic change makes it difficult to standardize regenerative practices 
across regions.” 
Quote: “Local programs like Bodenpraktikum educate farmers on regenerative principles, 
fostering acceptance.”

lii 
Interview 7: -  
 
Interview 8: 
Industry: Agriculture 
Quote: “Farmers often resist change because they are stuck in old habits and practices.” 
Quote: “There are no policies that financially reward ecological services provided by 
regenerative farming.” 
 
Interview 9: 
Industry: Sustainability Consulting 
Quote: “EU-level regulations ensure consistency across member countries, leveling the 
playing field for businesses.”  
 
Interview 10: 
Industry: Product Design 
Quote: “Regional inconsistencies in regulation create challenges for companies trying to 
implement regenerative practices.” 
Quote: “Regulations like the Eco Design Directive and digital product passports help, but 
implementation is slow.” 
 
Interview 11: 
Industry: Renewable Energy Industry 
Quote: “Land accessibility is a significant challenge, especially with local opposition and 
limited availability.” 
 
Interview 12: 
Industry: Chemical Industry 
Quote: “The industry and customers have an extremely positive perception of regenerative 
practices.”

Quote: “High specificity required for biotechnological solutions targeting particular
